THE RED PAGE.
Anti-Climax.
Klimax is Greek for a staircase. Anti-climax
oomes when you go up a staircase and think
there’s another step—or when you go down a
staircase and think there is n’t another step. Then
you poise a ludicrous foot in air, or jar a painful
foot aground; and a ludicrous jarring is usually
the essence of anti-climax.
“ Climax is a rhetorical figure in which the
sentence or series of sentences rises, as it were,
step by step; the successive members gaining in
force, importance, or dignity until the close. It
is a method of composition which passes from the
oommon to the rare, from the ordinary to the
wonderful, from the simple to the complex, from
the known to the previously unimagined.” One
of Salvator Rosa’s grammarians says so—Rosa
Salvator Grammaticae Civitatis. Example; Mr.
W. S. his “Tempest”—
The cloud-capped towers, the gorgeous palaces,
The solemn temples, the great globe itself,
Yea, all which it inherit, shall dissolve . . .
and he leaves you on the Himalayan height of
climax. Anti-climax might add—
But never, while paid members may prevent,
Will Parliament dissolve . . .
and topple you over the height. When, in the
Bible story, Satan set Jesus on a pinnacle of the
temple, and invited him, on the warrant of
Scripture, to cast himself down, Satan invented
a fair argumentative climax. But Jesus knew
the fall would be an anti-climax, and showed his
sense by preferring a side-issue on the same level.
Literary anti-climax depends on inconsistency,
incongruity something more than outright
opposition of phrases or ideas, v Such an opposi-
tion is classed as antithesis ; and anti climax is a
kind of exaggerated antithesis. Antithesis comes
when two tilings are contrasted: sharpen the
contrast a little, and you get an epigram : push it
farther still, and the result is anti-climax. The
boundary-line between these figures of speech is
imperceptible, and writers often aim at one mark
and hit another.
But we must differentiate. Not all anti-olimax
is ludicrous. There is a serious anti-climax ; and
a very handy figure it is. Serious anti-climax is
nothing but a rather lengthy epigram, or a keen
and extended antithesis. Solvitur Rosa’s gram-
marian—Judex Damnatur Cum Nocens AbSol vitur
Rosa—will quote you Mr W. S. his “Hamlet” —
What a piece of work is a Constitution ! How noble in
reason ! how infinite in faculty! in form, in moving,
how express and admirable 1 in action, how like an angel!
in apprehension, how like a god ! the beauty of the world !
the paragon of statutes ! And yet, to me, what is this
quintessence I bust V
The anti-climax here lies in the discord between
the nominal value of these splendid attributes of a
Constitution, and the worth at which a writer
appraises them by the standard of eighteen gram-
marians, from Churchill downwards.
A second kind of anti-climax is false climax, or
unintentional burlesque. “ There is but one step
from the sublime to the ridiculous, ” said Napoleon.
Gently does it. “Now, all I want ’—said
Edmund Gerald Fitzgibbon, silencing two dis-
putants with an inimitable wave of his imperious
hand in the old days when he town-clerked.to Melb.
Mayor Butters—“all I want is common sense.”
“ Precisely ! ” said one of the debaters. And the
person who was put down was not the person
whom Edmund Gerald Fitzgibbon intended should
be put down—thanks to anti-climax’ artful aid.
But you need not have two persons—or the
sublime —or the ridiculous —to make an anti-
climax. Simple incongruity suffices. In 1889 a
Melb. sharebroker committed suicide. He left
this brief letter —
Too much pain and too much mental worry caused me
to do this. I leave my wife and children to the protec*
tion of God and the care of Mrs. M. H. Davies.
Arid here is the treasured last verse of a serious
poem by a domestio bard in praise of Lady Boun-
tiful—
Then there are churchyard examples like —
Sacred to the memory of Robert Boyle, the father of
Chemistry and brother of the Earl of Cork.
In loving memory of Henry W. Hanson, died April 22,
1846. He was a devoted husband, an exemplary parent,
an honest man, and a first-rate shot.
The other notable kind of anti-climax is inten-
tional burlesque—the staple of all your professed
humorists from Hood to Bret Harte. Artemus
Ward made anti-climax visible on his posters,
thus —
evil new. His work has interest for all Aus-
tralians, but especially for the remnant of those
squatters who lived “like a little king, monarch
of all as far as the eye could reach.”
Incidentally one learns that shark-flesh tastes
“verymuch like india-rubber and bad anchovy
paste,” and that a fishy odour lingers with the
shark-eater for a week. Once Major was nearly
murdered by a Chinaman who crept behind him
with a bright new tomahawk. Just at that
moment the sun rose, and the tomahawk reflected
a beam of light which made Major turn round, so
that he received the blow on his shoulder—and
escaped.
Another of Major’s Chinamen was a cook who
made excellent minced mutton for the station
mess of three ; but grumbled when asked to
prepare the dish for a dozen visitors. Major was
firm against grumbles, and presently went into
the kitchen to see how dinner was getting on.
As I entered, Sam’s hack was towards me, and very
busily engaged he appeared to be. Holding a leg of cold
roast mutton, he cut off a slice, and transferred it to his
mouth, masticating it at a furious rate. Before him on
the table lay a dish, almost filled with something I could
not well make out. However, I was soon enlightened,
for presently he discharged the contents of his mouth
into the dish.
“ What the devil are you up to ? ” I exclaimed.
The sound of my voice caused him to start and almost
choke himself as he answered : “ Me makey mincemeat,
massa, close up ready, you see,”—and, placing a fresh
slice in his mouth, his jaws moved rapidly, and a further
addition was contributed to the infernal mess. . . ,
The cause of Sam’s reluctance to furnish a larger dish
than usual now dawned upon me. He evidently had no
objection to mince for us three, but to do so for a greater
number was more than his Chinese jaws could well under-
take.
That is an excellent variant of Brunton Ste-
phens’ rabbit-pie by a similar cook—
Last-a week-a plenty puppy ; this-a week-a puppy done !
The death of one of Major’s “ colonial experi-
ence ” young men befell curiously. E was
mustering cattle in some very thick and scrubby
pine-country, where the larger trees have pro-
truding dead branches about six feet from the
ground, as sharp as spears. F-4— was found
fallen from his horse, lifeless, his clothes saturated
with blood. His face Was as pale as marble ; but
not the slightest appearance of a wound or broken
bone could be discovered.
Even after he hud been washed and closely looked at,
the mystery of his death was not solved until evening
Behind the lobe of his right ear someone observed a slight
scratch, which was found to be really a puncture; so
small was it, that a quill could barely have entered the
wound. The jugular vein had been pricked by a sharp
stick, and every drop of bis life’s blood had escaped.
Major gives an excellent picture of a sailor
turned squatter, “a rough, honest, old sea-dog,”
who had never in his life been on a horse’s back ;
and who travelled in an old gig which he called
his whaleboat. He never used fire-arms, but
fought aggressive blacks with a harpoon and
twenty fathoms of line, kept clean and shipshape.
His address to the horse which drew the gig was
“Pull away, my hearty ! ”
And of such and similar materials are Thomas
Major’s memories made, and in such fashion does
he relate them. The price of his book is 3s. 6d. ;
posted from any metropolitan bookseller for
3s. lOd.

Fragment of conversation with Marcel Schwob,
reported by Yance Thompson—
Bluntly stated, Mr. Schwob’s theory is that Meredith’s
books are are only a transcription—a Latin translation, as
one might say—of his life. (When you read his essay in
“ Spioil&ge ” you will get his theory in full.)
Smoking his cigarette, stirring the lemon in his tea,
watching my pragmatic spaniel, Marcel Schwob said :—
“The greatest of modern novels is The Egoist."
“ My wife thinks as you do,” X said.
“ And that novel,” Marcel Schwob said slowly, stirring
his tea, “ is a page from George Meredith’s life.”
“ Yes.”
“ I)o not think of him as he is now—the old, gray, deaf
man that you and I knew hobbling up to Chapman and
Hall’s in Henrietta-street—but of a George Meredith
who was black and swarthy and 23. He married
an English girl She left him. You know the story—if
not, read The Egoist. After that, George Meredith went
to Italy. For five years he stayed there. He wore the
red shirt of Garibaldi. He fought like a love-sick man for
the liberty of others. Then he came back.
“ Five years had passed. His friends in London said,
‘Here’s Meredith.’ He gave them to understand that
the Meredith they knew—the Meredith whose wife had
created a scandal by running away from him—was dead,
definitely dead; if they wished to know a man named
(oddly enough) Meredith, who was no kin of the other
ridiculous man, he should be glad to meet them. And so
it was. A new Meredith began life in London."
I did not interrupt Marcel Schwob, save by the silent
tender of a cigarette. He lit the cigarette gently—merely
holding the match to it and permitting it to enkindle, as
one lets a blind girl absorb the scent of a rose—
“ * Evan Harrington,’ ” he said.
“ That, too, is a chapter of George Meredith’s life. Old
«Mel,’ the tailor. That was George Meredith’s father. It
you will look up Captain Marryat’s Peter Simple, you
will find in the early chapters that the midshipman went
to be outfitted to ‘ Meredith, the tailor.’ Portsmouth is
the ‘ Lymport ’ of the book. This Meredith was the
novelist’s father. More, he was exactly the character of
old ‘ Mel ’—a gentleman and a tailor. The truth is that
Meredith’s father was a bastard son of George IV. who
became a tailor—and rode to hounds; a tailor—and a
conqueror of women. ‘ Evan Harrington ’ is merely a
transcription of Meredith’s youth, as in David Copperfield
Dickens wrote his own life. Old ‘ Mel ’ —the ‘ Marquis ’
and ‘ Micawberonly sons could have painted those
pictures.”

This is partly Age’s complaint against Youth.
For the rest it is an expression of opinion on
behalf of the Athenaeum, (paper which encouraged
Kendall —most conservative and one of the most
capable of English literary reviews—especially
gifted with unconscious humour).
Maoriland, and Other Verses. By Arthur H. Adams.
(Sydney Bulletin Office.) —The frontispiece to this ample
volume shows that the author is still young, and in that
fact lies the hope that he will yet wing a loftier flight
than is attained by the minor poets of to-day. His fatal
fluency or his industry must be prodigious for him to have
written all that this bulky book of verse contains. He
has published far too much, and seemingly left himself no
time to trim, to finish, or (what is sometimes much more
necessary) destroy his work. There are over him at
present many influences which we dimly recognise, and
he sings of all things beneath the sun ; but his style is
yet unformed, and he seems unconscious of the bent of
his talent. It is certain, at any rate, that he will not
achieve success as a dramatic poet, for it is difficult to
exaggerate the supreme silliness of “ The Minstrel: -an
Incident in One Act,” a miniature melodrama, written in
the lofty style that Lytton affected. It is certain, too,
that he has a hundred faults of taste of which to cure
himself, the most glaring example being the dedication to
his mother of a book largely consisting of poems which
tell of the passions and loves of women of the demi-monde.
Instances of such false imagery, too, as the following are
not rare :
Her white brow a-clamour to be kissed ;
or this description of the Anarchist: —
The maddened one against the many; thus
' He strove to strangle Order’s octopus.
In his love poems—which, after all, contain his best work,
as they should—he is far too fond of singing about
“ bodies pulsing with love’s desire ” and the “ vehemence
of veins on fire ” (a good phrase, by the way), and the love
of which he sings is nothing but carnal desire. Still
there are many passages of beauty, though not of striking
originality, to be found—as, for example, the following:—
No. I have kissed you ! And the brief warm flower
Born of our lips perfumes eternity.
From the long loneliness that silently
Stretches behind, before, I am content
To cull this blossom of one perfect hour —
To snatch one star from Time’s deep firmament!
His verse, too, is always musical, his mastery of many
metres is surprising, and he has all a true poet’s love of
nature. “ The Dwellings of our Dead,” one of the Maori-
land poems, is richly melodious, and shows how close and
sympathetic is his observation: —
In whispers tender,
The flax-blades mourn and murmur, and the slender
White ranks of toi go,
With drooping plumes of splendour,
In pageantry of woe.
Moreover, such poems as “Bereft’’and “Lament” are
instinct with a simple, moving pathos, very sweetly and
tenderly expressed. If, on the whole, we value “ Maori-
land, and other Verses,” rather for its promise than
its performance, such poems make the fulfilment of the
promise certain. But Mr. Adams must learn not to split
his infinitives ; such a trick is neither for the poet nor the
grammarian.

Quote—
We Americans are a much-enduring nation. We allow
ourselves to be ruled by a thievish, lying, bribing,
thoroughly corrupt circle of cheap politicians. We
permit the use of our glorious flag for flaunting advertise-
ments of shoe polish and skirt-binding. We sit quiescent
and let a selfish, incapable Chief Executive wage a private
war in our name against a naked, desperate, brave little
nation that is fighting in the last ditch for the preserva-
tion of certain principles, the love of which was once our
proudest boast. Wo accept the fatuous vapourings of
Edwin Markham as poetry, the opaque platitudes of
William Jennings Bryan as oratory, the tea-caddy flimsi-
ness of W. D. Howells as literature—all these things we
do in great good nature.
The name of the American who wrote this is Karl
Stephen Herrmann. And he is just as good an
American as if he were named William John
Smith or Timothy Michael Murphy. Please get
out of your Australian head the notion that
America is English. It’s European.

Died recently Falguiere, one of the best-known
of contemporary sculptors. France owes to him
the groups on the Are de Triomphe, and the
colossal groups in front of the Trocadero. He
leaves a statue of Daudet unfinished at Nimes.

E. J. Forbes pleads in a Sydney daily for uni-
form spelling of Australian place-names; and
quotes, among many others, tnese examples of
H.S.W. Govt, usage:
Lands Dept. .. Bullah-Delah Bye Hock Collarindabri
Justice Dept. .. Bulladelah Byerock Oollarendebri
Postal Dept. .. Bullahdelah Byrock Coliarenebri
Statistician .. Oollarendabri
E. J. Forbes is right; and if the Instruction Dept,
possesses an official with brains he should be set
to compile an authoritative list —paying especial
attention to usage, euphony, and fitness.
Mary H. Kingsley’s “ Travels in Africa ” and
“ West African Studies ” have no patriotic bias to
commend them to John Bull. She thought Bull’s
treatment of African natives was as bad as any-
body’ s, and said so; and she showed her sympathy
with the Boer cause by going to nurse the Boer
wounded—the best a woman could do. And now
she is dead at Simonstown—a good and plucky
woman, with all her uncle’s (Charles Kingsley)
hatred of cant and injustice. ’T was her father,
Dr. G. H. Kingsley, maybe (author of “ South
Sea Bubbles ”) who gave her that unquenchable
yearning to wander, and wander, ana wander.
“ To-night she wanders far . . .”

THE BOOKFELLOW.
To the homes of the sick and the feeble she went;
Undaunted and firm on her purpose was bent;
The dogs were all glad—they left their old bones
To give a reception to our heroine, Miss Jones.
“ The marble bar at Tattersall’s
Is closed up at eleven:
When closeth, Blessed Damozel,
The golden bar of Heaven ? ”
Her eyes were sweeter than the strong
Waters distilled at even,
And her speech teas a song, though her lingo seemed
wrong,
“Ma jamais 1 hoc ist Heaven.” *

WILLY AND TUN LADY.
Leave the lady, Willy ; let the racket rip.
She is going to fool you ; you have lost your grip.
Your head is in a muddle, and your heart is in a whirl;
Come along with me, Willy ; never mind the girl 1
Come and have a Man Talk,
Come to those who can talk;
Light your pipe and listen, and the boys will pull you
through.
Love is only chatter,
Friends are all that matter.
Come and talk the Man Talk ; that 'a the cure for you 1
Leave the lady, Willy ; let the letter wait;
You ’ll forget your troubles when you get it straight.
The world is full of women, and the women full of wile.
Come along with me, Willy ; we can make you smile 1
Come and have a Man Talk,
A rousing black-and-tan talk 1
There are plenty there to teach you, and a lot for you
to do.
Your head must stop its whirling
Before you go a-girling.
Come and talk the Man Talk ; that's the cure for you !
Leave the lady, Willy ; the night is good and long.
There’s time for beer and baccy, time to have a song ;
Where the smoke is swirling, sorrow if you can 1
Come along with me, Willy ; come and be a man I
Come and have a Man Talk,
Come and hear the Clan talk 1
We've all of us been through the mill, and we ’v* been
broken, too.
We 11 advise you confidently,
And break it to you gently.
Come and talk the Man Talk ; that 's the cure for you
Leave the lady, Willy ; you are rather young,
When the tales are over, when the songs are sung,
When the men have made you, try the girl again i
Come along with me, Willy ; you ’ll be better then.
Come and have a Man Talk ;
Forget your Girl-Divan Talk !
You’ve got to get acquainted with a higher point of
view I
Girls are bound to fool you ;
We 're the ones to school you.
Come and talk the Man Talk; that’s the cure for you I

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Moonrise.
There’s the moon rising
Up from the sea;
Shadows, disguising
River and lea,
Hasten and flee;
O, there’s a paven
Ripple-traced road
Straight to the haven
Of Quiet’s abode.
But for a shallop
Gauzily-sailed—
Nautilus, scallop—■
Lover-breath-galed,
Angel-waft-swaled,
Only to speed me
Over the road,
Only to lead me
To Quiet’s abode.
Over the dimples
Pressed on the sea,
Under the wimples
Night-shade flings free
O’er you and me,
Floating and sailing
Swift on the road—
Everything’s ailing
From Quiet’s abode.
Where does it finish,
Path o’ the wave ?
Shall it diminish
But in the grave ?
God our souls save !
Might we explore it,
’Vanescent road,
Travelling o’er it
To Quiet’s abode !
AHAU.
An Australian Lament.
Where, then, shall Truth upraise her bruised
head?
Where, then, shall man look eye to eye with
men ?
If, shouting “Truth,” they shackle her, where
then
May bide high honour? Only with the dead ?
If foul and bestial Fratricide instead,
Proceeding from her horrid noisome den,
Exalt our homage, where shall we again
Find gentle mercy? Where hear Freedom’s
tread ?
Not here, alas, Australia ! Even thou
Dost join the blatant, braggart, jingo crew.
The brand of Cain now glistens on thy brow.
For that thy brother State is slain, thou too,
Must an iwer to High God. Australia, cease !
Thy God of Battles is the Prince of Peace ? >
M. L.
J. M. L.
Delilah.
And when she died, and passed the gates of Hell,
She saw him. How she drank of hatred’s
draught!
There he was writhing whom her beauty’s spell
Had tempted to destruction. And she laughed.
A spark of triumph kindled in her eyes;
She listened to the music of his groan—
Until an angel beckoned from the skies
And led her to the footsteps of the Throne.
And even as the glory and the light
Encompassed her, she glanced below—and fell
With one wild sob into the endless night
Into his arms —into the flames of Hell!
Only a fool would try his shallow art
To solve the riddle of a woman’s heart.
YON KOTZE.
A Ballade of Reptiles.
Girding the king in his pride at the feast,
Seated on high, a magnificent band,
Flouting us all to the last and the least,
Royally ruling a sycophant land ;
Who are the chosen before us that stand.
Greeted as princely by great and by small ?
Whence do they come with their air of command.
These are the creatures who creep and crawl.
Lauded as learned by pundit and priest,
Tickled for ever with epithets grand.
Praised as prophetic from West unto Fast,
Soon as the floods of their drivel expand ;
Holding the reins of a realm in their hand.
Lo—How the multitude come at their call !
g ee how their worshippers stream on the strand,
These are the creatures who creep and crawl !
Branding us all with the mark of the Beast
Treading the brow of the sage m the sand ;
Stripping the fields when the locusts have ceased,
* Mowing the brave with a merciless brand ;
Feeding them fat when the noble are banned.
Leaving the trail of their slime on us all.
Joying to jeer at a nation unmanned,
These are the creatures who creep and crawl .
Weariful toilers all bended and tanned ,
Know tie the race who rejoice at your jail f
Pickin'! vour bones with apologies bland
These are the creatures tcho creep and crawll
Brisbane.
P.L.

Special Notice
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article , tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether HI the form of Political, Social , or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Wcstralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 3s. 3d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of \\d. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage — lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
SATURDAY, JUNE 23, 1900.
The Same Old George.
N.S. Wales Parliament has just met, and
within a week of its meeting ex-Premier
George Reid, now leader of the Opposition,
gave notice of two votes of censure. The
debate on the first one is to cover as many
days or weeks of desultory conversation
about the sins and alleged sins of the Lyne
Go vernmentas George Reid can conveniently
achieve; and when that is over and done
with, and there is at last a chance of some
real work being commenced, he proposes to
move a new vote of censure, and have all the
trackless deserts of conversation over again.
Wherefore it appears that the Same Old
George hasn’t changed, save in the fact that
he is more unpleasantly similar than ever.
In the days of the Dibbs Ministry, Reid
established a record for obstruction. In
three years he moved some thirteen votes of
censure—all of them useless and hope-
less so far as shifting the Ministry
was concerned, but very useful in various
other ways and directions. They took
up among them something like six months
of the 21b months during which Par-
liament sat under the Dibbs regime, and
having thus wilfully and wantonly blocked
business, Reid was able to appeal to the
country with a shocked expression, and ask
for Dibbs’s ejection on the ground that he
didn’t get on with business. And appa-
rently Mr. Reid proposes to follow the same
tactics with the Lyne Ministry unless he is
flattened out. It isn’t known yet what kind
of progress the Lyne Government will make
at running N.S.W. on sane, solvent and
honestly democratic principles. The coun-
try wants to see that Government get to
work, so that it may receive the benefit of the
work if the work is good, and lose no un-
necessary time in getting rid of the Lyne
combination if the work isn’t good. But
unless George Reid is wisely trodden upon
it begins to appear as if very little will be
done to demonstrate either Lyjje’s useful-
ness or uselessness. And New South Wales
doesn’t keep a Parliament in order to in-
dulge the vulgar vanity and querulous spite
and mean self - seeking of any political
acrobat.
• • * •
Ex-Premier Reid has just been publicly
convicted of political doings so base and
paltry that a cleaner Parliament than that
of N.S.W. might 4 long ago have seriously
considered the question of his expulsion.
And this is only one in a long series of con-
victions. To quote but a few : He accused
his predecessors in office of wholesale stuffing
of the public service, and backed it up with
a huge list of alleged appointments so
utterly misleading that he had at last to
withdraw it as a document that couldn't
bear honest men’s inspection. Later he
tried to bolster up his Freetrade policy with
an alleged list of Protectionist prices which
were proved to be largely an invention, and
the work of somebody’s distorted imagina-
tion. These were not very material things
in themselves, but there was a cheap penny
degradation about them that wasn’t nice to
look upon And now Reid has been
charged by a committee of three lead-
ing financial experts—two of them men
whom he himself suggested as fit per-
sons to sit on such a body —of having
shamefully and disgracefully cooked and
misrepresented the public accounts. Even
the virtue of common political honesty in
his dealings with the public cash-book and
ledger is denied him, and this by a court
which, so far as concerned a two-thirds
majority of it, was distinctly his own court.
He is publicly found guilty of having mis-
used the cash and the accounts of the pro-
vince for his own political ends—to make
bogus surpluses for the enhancement of his
own reputation, which, as it ensured his
longer continuance in his salaried office, was
also his own personal profit, and in some
measure to make bogus deficiencies for the
injury and disparagement of his opponents,
which also meant ensuring longer continu-
ance in his salaried office. No such shame
as this has fallen on any previous Australian
Treasurers. There wore wise men among
them, and also foolish men—the foolish men
preponderating ; there were economists,
spendthrifts, weak men, ignorant men, and
miscellaneous, but they were, in the main,
politically clean, or it they were not the
fact didn't come aggressively to light. Not
one of them was ever before charged, on the
verdict of a committee of inquiry, the major-
ity of whom were his own nominees, of
garbling the accounts on a stupendous scale
and by an elaborate process which extended
over years, and of keeping the public books
on three-card principles.
0 m * *
This is not quite the first time such a
thing has happened to the wonderful George
Reid, though it never before happened so
definitely. In 1896, when the great sham
surplus —the one financial triumph of Reid’s
financial career —was on deck, and the
genuineness of that apparition was called in
question, he appointed a committee of three
Sydney bank managers to inquire into it and
guarantee its absolute reality. And his own
committee inspected the facts and proofs laid
before it by himself, and declared it hadn’t
sufficient evidence to demonstrate that
Reid’s one great alleged surplus was a
real and honest property. Reid’s own com-
mittee, after considering Reid’s own evi-
dence, declined to say that the famous
surplus wasn’t a fake and a sham ; possibly,
if the three bankers had had the evidence of
certain other people before them they would
promptly have gone much further and de-
clared that the surplus was a sham. Any
ordinary man would have broken down when
his own judges, selected by himself, refused
to give him a verdict in a case where all
witnesses save himself were shut out, but
Reid topped his record of incredible assur-
ance by presenting the damning document
to the Assembly and saying with pride that
it effectually vindicated his character. Being
indirectly found guilty, he got out of the
dock with a loud cheer and said that his
innocence was now establi hed. At various
times Reid’s accounts were the subject
of such bitter comment by the Auditor-
General as no previous N.S.W. Treasurer
has met with, but the rhinoceros-hided
funny man survived even that. And in
addition to everything else, he stands out as
the only Australian Premier who passed an
Act which, judged by after history, looks
as if it was expressly designed to cover
and legalise the system of faking the
accounts which followed. Further, when
his accounts were under examination
by the three financial experts appointed for
the purpose—two of them on his own sug-
gestion—George Reid failed even to put in
an appearance in his own defence though in-
vited to demonstrate the cleanliness of his
own political doings. Apparently the case
was too hopeless to be worth defending.
• • * *
The plea which ex-Premier Reid now ad-
vances is that the committee was not a fair
and unbiassed institution—that though the
two members whose names he proposed were
promptly appointed, Premier Lyne’s action
in appointing one (the third) member himself
showed an evil mind and a desire to pack the
bench. But, apart from the effrontery of
the man who repudiates his judges because
he was only permitted to nominate two out
of three, the fact remains that Reid was not ac-
quitted by his own two nominees. They formed
a majority, and if they had found a verdict in
his favour Lyne’s nominee would have been
outvoted and powerless. Instead they found
in effect that Reid had created a huge deficit
of about £1,000,000, where he wrongfully
assured the public there was a surplus and
that he was running the country on solvent
principles—that he had done this b}' an
elaborate process of misrepresentation—that
he didn’t tell the fair and honest truth about
his own financial doings, or about the coun-
try’s position, and that he grossly maligned
and slandered his predecessors by charging
them with deficiencies which were not fairly
and honestly theirs. The defendant’s lofty
repudiation of the verdict counts for nothing
—everybody repudiates the verdict when it
is against him. And now that ex-PREMiER
Reid has manifested a fixed resolve to eret
back to office by the old process of block-
ing public business, and stopping the
progress of urgently-needed legislation,
and sacrificing the interests of the
country till he gets there, it is about
time for Parliament to decide whether
he is a fit person to ever hold office in
N.S.W. again. It is also time for it to con-
sider whether he is the kind of person who
ought to decorate the legislative halls in any
capacity. In a country with a higher
standard of political honesty than N.S.W.
possesses it would probably be needless to
consider the first question at all events. If
ex-Premier Reid had been Chancellor of the
British Exchequer, and such doings and
devices had been proved against him on
equally good authority, the chance of his
ever again holding even a subordinate port-
folio would be too small to be worth men-
tioning. He would simply be the Politician
who Dropped Out. And if N.S.W. ever
proposes to cultivate a higher sense of poli-
tical honesty than it has hitherto possessed
it couldn’t havo a better time than the pre-
sent to begin the cultivating process.
George Reid ought to be the Politician
who Dropped Out in Australia also, and he
ought to drop with a sufficiently obvious
thud to warn all other Treasurers against
being anything even dimly resembling him.
The A.M.P. Job.
Joseph Abbott, M.L.A., ex-Speaker of N.S.W.
Assembly, K.C.M.G., solicitor, ex-Minister, and
chairman of A.M.P. Society, spreads himself over
2j columns of Sydney Telkoraph in defence of
the proposal to extend the operations of his insti-
tution to London and other parts of the British
empire. The tall man of many occupations is
long and desperately in earnest, but ho isn’t
wholly convincing. The chief reasons assigned
against the extension are that the British death-
rate is higher than that in Australia, which means
more losses, and that the British interest rate is
lower, winch means less profits. Brother Abbott*
seeks to coinbat both these charges, with ordy
moderate results. On the question of interest, for
example, he says:—
It is quite true that the society, in common with other
financial institutions in the colonies, has not been able re-
cently to realise as remunerative rates on its investments
as it did some years ago. It is equally true that it has
become increasingly difficult to obtain suitable mortgage
securities. In the light of these facts the board has en-
deavored to profit by experience, and has felt the pro-
priety of investing an increasing proportion of the funds
in Government securities. At the end of 1802, the year
preceding the financial crisis, 3 per cent, of the assets
were invested in Government securities ; at the end of
last year the proportion was over 13 per cent. Now it is
a fact evident to everyone who observes the condition of
the money market that colonial Government stocks can,
as a rule, be obtained more advantageously in London
than in the colonies. One example will be sufficient to
illustrate this. A few months ago the Government of
N.S.W. placed £1,000,000 of 4 percent. Treasury Bills on
the London market at 99J ; the investment therefore
yielded a little over 4 per cent. The bills were not pro-
curable in the colony, and as the society had no respon-
sible officer in London it was unable to obtain any. Last
month the same Government placed £500,000 of 3£ per
cent, bills on the local market, and in order to obtain a
share of these the society had to offer a small premium.
The absence of an office in London lost it over one-half
per cent, per annum on this transaction. The board
realises that if the existing condition of comparative stag-
nation in the commercial and industrial enterprise of
the colonies continues, it will be compelled to resort
more freely to investments in Government stocks ; if, on
the contrary, as it believes, the present condition is only a
passing phase of national life to be followed later on by an
extension of enterprise, there will be ample field for the
remunerative employment of the society’s increasing
funds. In any event the sum which the proposed exten-
sion will add to the society’s accumulations will be insig-
nificant compared with the amounts of British capital
which annually seek investment in the colonies. Fur-
thermore, as the rate of exchange is always against a
debtor country, the transfer of funds from London to the
colonies will afford a constant source of profit.
* * * *
This argument looks a great deal more solid than
it is, for these reasons : (1) It can’t be supposed
that the N.S.W. Government will go on as a
regular thing making the hideous blunder of selling
4 per cent, securities in London when the money
can be got in Australia at per cent. It made that
astounding mistake once, and the A.M.P. wasn’t in
London to take advantage of it. But there
is no evidence that it will do a similar
foolishness in the next 50 years. (2) It is true
that recently (only during the depression) Austra-
lian State securities could be bought more cheaply
in London than here. But Mr. Abbott declares
his firm belief that the depression will pass away,
and if so this state of things will pass also. More-
over, even if money can be invested to better ad-
vantage in London than here that doesn’t necessi-
tate doing life insurance business in London.
Various British life offices have agencies for the
investment of money here without doing insurance
business. And if better interest can be obtained
in England than here, why don’t the British offices
obtain it ? They had a chance to buy up the recent
N.S.W. 4 per cent. loan. They also have had the
run of the cheap Australian securities offering in
London for years past. And yet, out of nearly
80 leading British offices quoted in Whitaker’s
Almanac for 1900 not one earned nearly
as high interest on its investments as the A.M.P.
does. The best of the lot —the great Scottish Pro-
vident—only earned £4 6s 6d per cent, against the
A.M.P.’s £4 11s. lOd. per cent. If the A. M. P.’s
funds were invested at the Scottish Provident
rate it would have meant a loss last year of
£40,000. If the British offices can’t get better
rates than this on an average in Britain, how can
the A.M.P, do better —unless the leading British
offices don’t understand their business ?
* * *
Also, Mr. Abbott speaks (1) of the extra
money to be made by investing the British
premiums in Britain, and (2) of the ex-
change to be made by remitting the British
premiums to Australia for investment. Now
the A.M.P. can’t do both these things —at
least not with the same money. And if it does
make a profit out of exchange by remitting British
premiums to Australia, it has no possible right to
treat that as profit and divide it among its policy-
holders. Mr. Abbott says that the rate of ex-
change will alt. ays be against Australia because
it is a debtor country. Most probably it wilL
That simply means that £lOOO of British premiums
sent to Australia for investment would expand to
perhaps £lOlO or £lOl5, or some other amount.
And the £lOlO or £lOl5, or other amount, when
sent back to England to pay death claims on these
same policies would probably shrink again,
by the reverse process of exchange, and allowing
for loss of interest while in transit, to about
its old dimensions. The only assumption on
which the exchange could be treated as profit
is that the money will never have to be sent back,
that the British business will always go on and
always yield enough in premiums to meet
claims without drawing on the accumulated
fund. And, even then, the principle isn’t
sound. If the rate of exchange will always
be against Australia, as Mr. Abbott alleges,
and the A.M.P. in course bf time accumu-
lates (say) £10,000,000 in Australia that belongs
to policy-holders in Britain, and it. has made
(say) £lOO,OOO of that out of exchange, it has
still got to remember that the £10,000,000,
for the purpose of paying these British policy-
holders, isn’t worth £10,000,000. It is only worth
that sum less crchanye. The profit made on send-
ing the money out here should not be divided but
put to a special reserve to cover possible loss on
sending the money back again. It must be sent
back some day unless the A.M.P. lives and grows
for ever. Does anything live for ever ?
* « » *
Mr. Abbott also states (what is certainly quite
true) that small policies are less profitable than
large ones, for the medical fee on a £lOO policy is
the same as on one of £2OOO. And he points out
that the policies issued last year by the A.M.P.
were for an average of only £2Ol each, while in
some of the best British offices such as the Scottish
Widows’ Fund the average of «U the policies in
existence is as high as £l3OO. Considering
that the average amount of policies is steadily
decreasing this isn’t a fair comparison. The
average amount of all ordinary life policies issued
in the United Kingdom last year was not much
ovor £350, so the small policy rages there also, and
it is getting worse year by year.
* • * #
Some more of Mr. Abbott :
At the two mortality investigations of this society in
1878 and 1888, the death-rate was ascertained to have
shown little variation, and this fact must he ascribed
entirely to the abnormal increase in the number of endow-
ment assurances. Unfortunately, a favorable mortality
experience among endowment assurances it not a source
of great profit, as it is in the ease of whole life policies.
This may be a revelation to some members, hut it has
been proved to a demonstration. Our mortality investi-
gations have shown that our deaths have been
about 67 per cent, of those expected according
to the Hm. table, and this fact has at once been
accepted by superficial observers as a proof that
the mortality among assured lives is lighter in
the colonies than In Great Britain. As a matter of fact,
however, the experience of British offices has shown a
similarly favorable experience when compared with the
same standard. The difference has not been so marked
as with us, hut the reason for this is obvious and will he
explained directly. For example, the deaths in the
Metropolitan Life Assurance Society of London, a most
conservative office, which pays no commission to agents,
and consequently transacts no business of the ephemeral
character common in these colonies, were, at the end of
45 years, in 1890, equal to 88 6 per cent, of the expecta-
tion, hut the average age of the lives at entry was 336
years, and the average duration of the policies was 17‘8
years, while only 1 per cent, of its policies were for en-
dowment assurances. The deaths in our society at the
end of 40 years had been 07 per cent, of the expectation,
but the age at entry was 32 years, the duration of the
policies 6'2 years, and the endowment assurances
issued were 42 per cent, of the total. At the end
of last year the endowment assurances in force
largely outnumbered the whole life-policies. No one
familiar with life assurance statistics would contend that
these figures disclose a more favorable experience in our
society than in the Metropolitan. The Loudon Life Asso-
ciation, an office similar in every respect to the Metro-
politan, reported that the claims in its 89th year were
71.5 per cent, of those expected by the Hm. table. Its
policies must have been very much older than those of
the A.M.P. Society ; it had only 18 endowment assurances
out of a total of 8560, and yet its experience was but little
less favorable than our own. The mortality in the Law
Life Office at the end of 52 years was 92.8 per cent, of that
expected by the Hm. table, but the average age at entry
was 37.24 years, the average duration of the policies 15.69
years, and it had only 6 per cent, of its business in endow-
ment assurances. In the Scottish Widows’ Fund, which
is 85 years old, the deaths ranged from 70 to 80 per cent,
of those expected by the Hm. table, but its policies are,
of course, very much older than ours.
• • • *
This is all very beautiful, and it is also a good
deal too tangled for the ordinary reader. But
against it we have the assurance of the A.M.P.
Society’s own actuary that:
Jt is well-known that the powerful influence which the
medical selection exercises on assured lives disappears
after about five years, after which such lives are little, if
any, better than those of the general population from
which they have been taken.
If this means anything it means that, after the
policies have been in force five years, the insured
nave just about the same chance of living to an
advanced age as the rest of the population. And
all the elaborate calculations about the proportion
of ordinary and endowment assurance can’t alter
the fact that the average male cornstalk of 30 can
look forward to two years’ longer life than the
average Englishman of 30, and that means two
more annnal premiums paid to the A.M.P. Society
before he becomes a claim. And in the big busi-
ness these two extra premiums may make all the
difference between a large profit and a small one.
• » • *
Mr. Abbott winds up by pointing out that the
heads of the A. M. P. Society are all in favor of the
extension of the business to England, and they
ought to know better than mere outsiders. So
they ought. Still the rule isn’t always a safe one
to follow. The experienced men who ran the 20
busted land-banks of Melbourne ought to have
known the banking business better than mere
outsiders, but the result leaves that very much in
doubt. This isn’t a comparison—only a reflection.
The real question is: If the men who run an
institution always, by reason of that fact, know
better how to run it than anybody else, how is it
that any institutions go wrong?
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Enthusiastic Sclieveizer.
Probably the worst advertisement for the money
which any one has yet got out of the Bushmen’s
Fund was secured by Mr. H. S. Chipmax, of
Sydney. The following is Mr. Chipman’s letter
to Sydney Telegraph, the italicised parentheses
being The Bulletin’s suggestions and com-
ments :
I enclose letter from the Ansonia Clock Co., of New
York ( for which I am agent here —excellent clocks—
•prices low—call round promptly and buy ) re the Citizens’
Bushmen’s Contingent. This letter is a fair specimen of
the sentiments expressed in letters addressed to me from


a number of important manufacturers in the United
States (for whom I also do business —call and see samples)
aggregating many millions sterling, and employing thou-
sands of hands {the sentiments of the hands don’t matter).
The writer of the letter enclosed is a German by
birth {name Sdieveizer), and though a good Ger-
man and a loyal American citizen, he backs up his
sentiments towards the British Empire and its people
with a subscription to the Bushmen’s Fund, which I will
have pleasure in forwarding through you, to the extent of
£SO. (It is/i’t my money, you see, but Sclieveizer’s ;
call and inspect the Ansonia clock.)
Then Sclieveizer’s letter to Chipman is given, as
follows: —
I am in receipt of your favor of April 10th, and thank
you (in a tired sort of way) for the souvenir of the Bush-
men’s Contingent which you have sent to me. We have
contributed to the Hospital Fund in London (where the
Ansonia clock is being pushed for all it is worth), and if
it is your desire (we don’t expressly say it is ours) that we
contribute something to the Australian Fund, please advise
me. ( You don’t expect me to grow enthusiastic over it I sup-
pose ? I never dreamt of contributing, but if you think it
advisable, I suppose you lenow best. You are a good man of
business, or you wouldn’t represent us in Sydney, and you
are on the spot, and I’m not. I don’t fix the amount; give
whatever you think will make a decent impression). I quite
agree with you in regard to the sentiments powerfully ex-
pressed in your letter. (Powerful sentiments utterly
omitted, heaven only knows what they were.) I agree
with you in the far-reaching results (it is the biggest reach
I ever heard of; please note that I don’t say whether the
results will be good or bad) of the entire movement, and
furthermore think that this spontaneous combination of
the colonies will place before the world an Allied Power
the like of which has never been seen in ancient or
modern history. (You will observe that I don’t commit
myself. You may read this to mean that it will be the
greatest Power, or the most scattered, or the most excited,
or the one that has made the biggest grab, or the Power
that has done the best or the most foolish thing on record, or
the richest one, or the most villainous, or anything ;
I only say that in some way, not specifieii , it will
excel any other power in history. The fact that I don’t
specify my meaning may be taken to signify anything you
like, or don’t like).
Mr. H. S. Ciiipman’s struggle to make his princi-
pal’s blankly-indifferent aud non-committal letter
read like the fervid outpouring of an enthusiastic
soul that sells the Ansonia clock is pathetic. And
how the Sydney D.T. people, who printed Mr.
Chipman’s letter, must have chuckled !
“Hobart Mercury” Turns Dog.
When The Bulletin said that Brisbane Courier
was the angriest paper in Australasia on find-
ing that Joe Chamberlain wasn’t going to
mangle the Federation Bill so much as originally
expected, this paper had not read Hobart
(Tas.) Mercury. All its life until now the
Mercury, a solemn paper printed in laudanum
on sheets of lead, has been saying beautiful
things like this:
We desire to see the Empire united, to make the Briton
abroad one with the Briton at home, and to consolidate
the strength of an empire which stands for true freedom,
and which, if united, may leave the foreign critics to
gnash their teeth in impotent despair.
The Mercury also pointed out that “the Bill”
was sent to England purposely to be mangled, and
that if the people here hadn’t been assured that it
would be properly mangled and clear-starched and
ironed by Joseph they would never have voted it.
It also loudly inquired for weeks who “ this man
Barton ” might be, who had the front to defend
the Bill as the people of Australia and Tasmania
had made it and accepted it, and “whom does he
claim to represent.” When I wrote and told it
that Barton was really Holman of Grenfell in
disguise it was too angry to reply civilly. Then
news came that Joseph Chamberlain in his
wisdom had decided to mangle the Bill much less
than originally intended, and the Mercury, after
being absolutely Jingo for years, started to abuse
the British Empire. Some extracts:
Our telegrams about the Commonwealth Bill illustrate,
we are afraid, the unfortunate proclivity of the British
Government to make it unprofitable to colonists to be
loyal. The terrible muddle in South Africa, the end of
which is not yet, can all be traced to the tendency of the
British Government to throw over its most staunch
friends in the colonies, apparently for no other reason
than to save itself some little trouble and responsibility.
In the case of the Commonwealth Bill we have the same
mistaken policy or want of policy in another form. The
Imperial Government set out in the right manner by re-
ferring the Bill to the law-officers, who tacked a number of
objections to it, but no sooner did certain quite
unauthorised persons arrive from the colonies, than
all the objections were at once abandoned except
one, and that seems now finally to have been thrown
overboard. . . . The whole business is an unpleasant
and unworthy one, and it tends to lower the Imperial
Government in the minds of the colonists, especially of
those who may be said to be the most thinking and most
loyal portion of them. Moreover, it shows a very unfor-
tunate misunderstanding of the position and opinions of
the colonists generally. It is assumed, apparently, that
there is a compact body of opinion in these colonies which
has decided on every provision of a Federation Bill, and
that the holders of that opinion have a clear, undoubted,
and unalterable case in their minds. Everybody in the
colonies knows not only that no such opinion exists, but
that Abe vast majority of the people are altogether indif-
ferent as to most of tbe details of the measure. There are
a large number of persons, the very ones most staunch to
and most capable defenders of Imperial Federation, who
have relied on the Home Government to deal conscien-
tiously with the Bill, so that the many crude opinions of
the Conventions might be carefully reviewed by the men
who are supposed to be the foremost and best informed
in the Empire. These persons are being, as usual, be-
trayed and abandoned. They are.being taught the lesson
which has borne such bitter fruit in South Africa, that he
who trusts the Imperial Government is sure to be
grievously disappointed. The Imperial Govern-
ment has, in fact, broken a solemn compact....
The situation seems to us to be very unfortunate. It is
not only a direct breach of compact and a betrayal of
those who relied on the Imperial Government, but it
lowers that Government in the mind of every colonist.
The extreme Radical will snigger to think that Imperial
criticism vanishes before the simple bullying of the Home
Government, while the more staid, the loyal, the up-
holders of a wise and prudent union, will feel that it is a
mistake to be loyal, a fatal error to have reliance on the
authorities in Great Britain. . . . Not a soul, as far as can
be learned in the colonies, objects to the proposed altera-
tions, but we are told that they must not be made because
the delegates, who have no authority from the people,
want the Bill intact. . . . But, after all, the worst part of
the business is, not that the Bill may be passed as it
stands, which we believe to be a mistake, but the further
proof we have of the flabbiness of the Imperial Govern-
ment.
So Hobabt Mercury, the old banner-bearer of
Jingoism, now says straight-out that “ it is a mis-
take to be loyal,” and that it is a fatal error to rely
on Britain, "and tries to undermine Australia’s
devotion to Britain by calling the Mother-country
flabby and foolish, and a betrayer of its friends.
What strange tilings do happen in this world !
"A Rigid and Revolting Regularity.”
An amended Building Act for Brisbane is in con-
templation, and Brisbane Telegraph thus takes
time by the forelock with an appeal on behalf of
the Sacred Rights of Ugliness :
Mere appearances cannot reasonably be held to fall
within the scope of any legislation for the control of
buildings in a city. The taste of the man who spends
the money must control the appearance of the building.
It may be that there are persons who, being so entirely
possessed by the doctrines of socialism, would insist upon
a rigid and revolting regularity even in appearance, such
a regularity as would excite ridicule. Full scope for the
artistic faculty must be conserved, for the variety which
must spring from such freedom will secure a general
beauty. Interference with private taste can be tolerated,
or even required, only when the safety of the public is
concerned. That safety calls for buildings of a stable
character, and of a nature not likely to facilitate destruc-
tion by fire, or the spread of fire from one building to
another.
And, in reply, The Bulletin rises to maintain that
mere appearances are one of the things which
every city’s Building Act should keep in view
continually. If Ugliness isn’t a crime it ought to
be one. When a building law has provided for
sanitation and safety from Are it has only half
fulfilled its mission. The socialist doesn’t
want “ a rigid and revolting regularity ; ” what
he wants is to escape from the mean and revolting
and sordid regularity that prevails already, and
which is so dear to the grocery-soul of the Tele-
graph and like organs. To know what the
present regularity amounts to it is advisable to
explore some city like Melbourne, which has an
elaborate suburban railway system. The railway
takes you past the city’s back doors, while the
tramear only shows you its front doors, therefore
you can see Melbourne’s aggregate back door by
train in a day, while that of Sydney or Brisbane
(which is very similar) has to be explored gradu-
ally on foot to do it full justice. Either way all
these back doors are a revolting spectacle. The
miles of cottages with the exactly similar wooden
lean-to; the miles of crippled back fence of the
same orthodox height; the miles of wooden back-
offices, of practically the same size and pattern
and all painted the same mud-color; the square
leagues of galvanised-iron roof and unpaved back-
yard—what a misery they all are ! They repre-
sent the awful uniformity that comes of cheap-
ness. The suburban-cottage landlord has dis-
covered that a certain style of architecture—the
wooden-box style with the lean-to, a certain pat-
tern of fence and out-offices, a certain brand of
paint and galvanised-iron roof, represent the
irreducible minimum of cheapness; and dead,
blank, awful uniformity is the result. A certain
type of street —the bare, muddy, treeless kind—
represents another irreducible minimum, and is
popular accordingly. And the ugliness of them is
an outrage. This paper wants to see wooden
buildings, wooden fences, wooden out-offices, un-
paved back-yards that are seas of mud, and
pocket-handkerchief allotments prohibited in
every city because of their rigid and revolting
regularity. It would let the landlord’s fancy
range over everything in the shape of stone
or brick, but the accursed monotony of
the weatherboard shanty gets on to its
nerves. It wants to see wide streets lined with
evergreen trees, because the blankness of the
ordinary blank highway is too awful for words ;
and improved back lanes because the orthodox
ribbon of mud, with broken wooden fencefe on each
side and a mongrel dog in the middle distance,
makes the eye ache with its dreariness. After all,
it is a question between the monotony of beauty
and the monotony of ugliness.
♦
The Sydney Wharf Resumptions.
Already there are signs that property-owners in
the to-be-resumed area of Sydney fully expect to
work the usual gorgeous grab when they sell out
to the State. Land values have gone up materi-
ally even now, merely on the prospect of selling
to the easily-milked Government; when the
prospect becomes a certainty they will probably
go up further. The Bulletin hears of one case
where a property has been offered to the State for'
£50,000, yet the land - tax valuation is only
£25,000, and the municipal valuation is based on a
rental-value of £l4OO a year which, at 5 per cent.,
means a capital value of £28,000, and no one has
yet heard that owner complain that either of
these valuations is too low, or offer to pay the
difference Yet he is a moderate property-
owner compared with some of his kind. When the
struggle for the loot really arrives it is essential
that the community should understand that the
water-side property-owners haven’t nearly so
much to sell as they will probably profess. Most
of their land has, in itself, only a moderate
value for wharf purposes. It is made valu-
able through the building of jetties, and the
right to build jetties doesn’t belong to the land-
lord. He has to apply to the State for per-
mission to use part of the State’s harbor, and he
has to pay rent for the same. The rent is supposed
to be full value for the use of the harbor, and if
the State takes from tiie landlord the use of its
harbor, and at the same time ceases to collect the
rent from him, things are supposed to be equal,
and no compensation is due, except for the actual
material —mostly old and depreciated material—
in the abandoned jetties, and for the cost the land-
lord is put to in removing. Then the land
remains to be paid for, and it is merely
so much inferior city land in a low and
cheap locality with no very valuable water rights
after all. In hasn’t the value which
attaches, by reason of their picturesqueness, to the
residential sites at, say, Darling Point, for there
is no picturesqueness left, and it isn't a residential
locality. The privileges which make it most
valuable for shipping purposes don’t belong to the
landlord ; they are already the property of the
State, and always have been. The Bulletin re-
iterates its old contention that the municipal
valuations, with reasonable compensation for dis-
turbance added, should be taken as the basis of
compensation. They are estimated—and very
fairly estimated, as a rule—on the value of the
landlord’s own property. No property-owner in
the whole resumed area has ever complained that
the municipal valuations were too low, and, unless
anyone proposes to assume that these landlords
are a set of swindlers, taking a mean advantage of
the Corporation, this is in itself reasonable proof
of their accuracy. At all events, if the person
whose property is municipally valued at £25,000,
manages to prove that it is worth £50,000 when he
sells out to the State, it is a fair assumption that
the property in question has defrauded the Cor-
poration of half its fair taxes for many years, and
it ought now to pay the other half with compound
interest added. In which case one thing will pro-
bably go a long way towards balancing the other.
Also, it should be charged with arrears of land-
tax and compound interest added, together with
the usual fines for defrauding the Department.
And if it can be found that the proprietor, who
now proves that his land is worth £50,000, has
sworn to its value as £25,000 or some other less
sum, for taxation or probate purposes, any reason-
able Resumption Act will provide for him getting
three years hominy for perjury as well as pay-
ing the difference, and compound interest on
the difference. These things have been said
before, but they can’t be said too often. They
will probably be said two or three times more
before the resumption business is ended.

A Religious Paper on “ Brats.”
The Protestant Banner (Sydney) thus cour-
teously and in a spirit of Christian charity alludes
to the recently-established Waitara (R.C.) found-
ling hospital:—
Waitara Angel Factory wants miscroscopic observation.
Already it is full with 150 little immoralities, and the un-
happy mothers have therefore cast off their responsibili-
ties for the time. The refusals per month are now 40 and
if there was room for 500 the place would be full. The
medical officer, Dr. O’Neill,, gives the death-rate as 30 per
cent; but one half of the admissions die within a short
time owing to parental neglect. The institution owes
£4482, and Protestants will be badgered to support it.
The State should undertake this sort of charity, because
it alone could compel the minute registration of paternity
and get repayment from the man who so frequently
leaves his victim to shift as she best can. -In purely R.O.
lands, where Holy Church does what it jolly well likes,
the illegitimate brat is put from the outside of the con-
vent on to a revolving table, and the nun inside neither
asks a question nor gets a glimpse of the surreptitious
depositor. As an adjunct to the most demoralising
immorality foundling hospitals are unequalled; and in
Catholic lands they are termed “ angel factories,” because
the little dears are starved into becoming denizens of the
sky.
Thus it appears that, in purely R.C. lands, where
the Holy Church, in the frisky language of the
Banner, “does what it jolly well likes,” that
church takes in the homeless, fatherless infant
without asking questions, and its iniquity in not
asking questions rouses the Banner to fury.
Ordinary unchristian people will probably fail to
see where the sin comes m. The R.C. Church, it
would appear, acts in this matter, anyhow,
as a gentleman. It doesn’t glue its eye to
the orphanage keyhole to take stock of the “fallen
woman "’ who leaves the evidence of her trouble at
its doors. It is a man and a brother in so far as it
doesn’t intrude its shorn and shaven head into a
lady’s personal affairs ; nor does it suddenly stick
its tonsure out of the orphanage window to ask a
perfect stranger how and when she fell, and with
whom, and to call her bad names in a pious spirit.
It doesn’t hire a committee of old, middle-aged and
mixed cats to interrogate her about her disgrace,
and stand her up in a large room for the old cats’
inspection. Nor does it go round to Christian
families asking them to take a fallen girl as a
slavey at reduced wages, and keep a steely eye on
her, and not let her forget that she is a crushed
worm and a thing apart. It doesn’t drive her to
leave her child on the inhospitable doorstep, or to
give it to the “kind person” on whose premises
the miscellaneous infant dies so promptly for a
consideration, or to have recourse to the abor-
tionist whose advt. strikes the public so conspicu-
ously in the eye in this country’s press—ana the
Banner gives way to common earthly rage be-
cause it doesn’t. “Little children, curse one
another,” is evidently the Banner’s new
reading of an old verse, and its wrath at
the Church which takes in the “ brat,”
as distinguished from the Church which leaves
the brat outside, is pathetic. As for
the plea that the R.C. system of foundling-hos-
pitals encourages immorality—possibly it does.
Any kind of mercy or charity doubtless encourages
sin to some extent. Probably the only kind of
person who never encouraged sin is the one who
never forgave anybody or assisted anybody. It
would do the Banner good to read, in this con-
nection, Dr. Muskett’s expatiation on Coghlan’s
statistics concerning infant mortality in N.S.W.

Westralia has generally some stench on hand in
high places. I’or instance, the freight for ice
from Perth to Kalgoorlie is £1 7s, lid. per ton
while milk, fish and other goods usually sent in a
chilled state cost up to £5 per ton. And it is
openly alleged that the Perth Ice Co., wherein a
number of highly-placed politicians and notables
are shareholders, has regularly consigned large
quantities of other goods to Kalgoorlie as ice, thus
saving nearly three-fourths of the freight, by
which saving it was enabled to cut prices against
all rivals and scoop the trade. It is further
alleged publicly that the Government’s attention
was dragged to the matter with difficulty, and that
even then it didn’t collect the arrears of freight or
prosecute for fraud- j -it was simply a case of the co.
promising not to do it again. Whether the co., if
it ever really did what is imputed to it, thereupon
left off doing it doesn’t appear. It is also
alleged, very publicly indeed, that the co. in-
variably got the preference over rival firms
in the matter of trucks, and that its goods
were hitched on to the express at times, while
other people’s ice and substances came behind by
ordinary vehicle. Nobody seems to have been
prosecuted so far by an indignant co. anxious to
clear its character, and the fact that such a monu-
mental stench should arise about a concern in
which so many big politicians are said to be in-
terested (and they haven’t cleared their characters
of the charge) is a very sad coincidence. Sad
coincidences like that are quite too common in
W. A.

Bishop Goe and the clerical faction in Melbourne
University Council appear to have formed an anti-
MaksHall-Hall League, under the patronage of
the baffled Argus. Numerous schoolmasters,
reverend and otherwise, have suddenly discovered
that the conscience of the community objects to a
renewal of the musical professor’s lease, and as the
community in a general way leaves all questions
concerning its conscience to be settled by a few
newspaper correspondents this new attempt
to get rid of Marshall-Hall looks rather
formidable —in the Argus. It is practically
an attempt to “ avenge Majuba.” Two years
ago Marshall-Hall and his friends fought a
battle with Bishop Goe and the Argus brigade,
and the forces of snuffle were routed. Since then
the victorious Prof, has strengthened his reputa-
tion aa an able man of music, and has done
nothing to horrify the unco guid. His present
lease of his University billet will not expire for
months to come, nevertheless the majority
of University Councillors who propose to extend
his career of usefulness over another term, are told
that the community’s conscience regards their
proposal with Horror. The “gag” would be more
funny if it were less despicable.
In the Adelaide dailies of June 11, Chief Justice
VV ay publishes what he calls “some further ob-
servations ”on the Commonwealth Bill. It is an
advantage to the public that these further observa-
tions have been openly published, for the previous
lot were marked “ confidential,” and were circu-
lated in England only, and were merely made
known out here because Kingston accidentally
discovered the existence of the document. Pub-
lisher Way, therefore, in response to general desire,
now prints an Australian edition of his elusions.
Way’s latest discourse is a remarkably impudent
affair, coming as it does from a Judge who is
supposed to be removed from political strife.
Little Samuel thunders about the dangers accom-
panying political interference with the judiciary,
yet he is dumb as to the greater danger of judicial
interference in politics. But the most charming
specimen of his assurance is the assertion that the
new clause should not be admitted into the
Bill “ without the consent of the people or their
Parliamentary representatives.” This is written
by the same Samuel who wrote secretly to the
Colonial Office urging it to amend, by inserting
a clause which Samuel himself drafted, the very
measure which the people of Australia have twice
adopted ! Samuel James Way poses as the Friend
of the People ! And ho says “ there has been a
remarkable reticence in giving publicity to the
exact provisioned the new clause.” Behold Way,
the author of the 12 pp. confidential pamphlet,
condemning secrecy!

Queensland did honor to itself and justice to
thousands of its silently-suffering children by
following in the footsteps of Maoriland and South
Australia, and passing, last December, an Act to
legitimatise children born before the marriage of
their parents. Both tho Q. and M.L. Acts contain
the absolutely necessary proviso that the Act
shall apply to children born before as well as after
the passing thereof. When are the other provinces
going to follow suit ? 'I he re, for instance, is work
which the much-advertised Vic. Society for the
Protection of and Prevention of Cruelty to Child-
ren might well take in hand. What physical
suffering short of absolute death-dealing cruelty
can equal the mental torture silently endured all
through childhood and youth by thousands from
the “ scornful looks averse ” of those who, if this
Act were in force, would be their comrades and
equals, and from the brutally-hurled “ bastard ”
of other callous but franker contemporaries ?
However chivalrous it may be for a nation to hold
up a woman’s shame, for perhaps a momentary
slip, to her for a lifetime, it is surely nothing short
of low-grade barbarism to similarly pillory inno-
cent children.

Someone should really make a collection of the
gruesome tales of Australian Boer-killers. Here
is one found in Sydney News some time ago ; it
may prove useful to anybody in need of an emetic,
but, as usual, the writer is not ashamed of putting
his name to it. He is describing Elandslaagte
fight:—
. . . There was a stampede in the Boer lines—but too
late; the “ bhoys of the Emerald Isle ” were among
them. Then we had a second go and collared 100 horses.
We went after the Lancers, and started with the six-
shooters at the enemy ; but we were too late, as the
Lancers did not leave much for us, though I did not do
so badly. I managed to get five to my account. . . .
In spite of our excitement we had a good laugh when the
Lancers charged. The Boers pulled off their shirts and
waved them as a flag of truce ; but Britishers, always
seeing a white flag used for such a purpose, did not heed
their old shirts, as their color was very doubtful—may
have been white one time, but are quite strangers to
water ; result, no mercy.

Two extracts from S.M. Herald :
(1) By cable:
The Boers (i.e., at Zand River engagement) robbed a
few of the dead, and looted the saddles from the horses
that were killed.
Evidently an unusual occurrence, or it would
hardly be worth cabling. The second item, same
issSue, appears in Macdonald’s Impressions of
Battle:
Blink at the fact as one may, loot is inseparable from
battle, and must not be judged by the moral codes of
peace. Some are for spoil pure and simple ; some want
onlj' a souvenir (teeth and whiskers, for example.—Ed. B.);
nearly all —if they have the nerve for sudden and start-
ling opportunities—loot in some way To rob the
dead seemed to me at first the meanest act in the whole
range of human capacity, but that feeling blunted after a
time, and if one did not actually fall himself he became
ver3' tolerant of the frailties of others. It was a latitude
that reached even such sordid things as cash and valuables.
The Herald’s censor must have been nodding
when he let that pass.

“ E.F.C Du C.” to The Bulletin
Isn’t it about time, the Australian dailies gave up sneer-
ing at “Boer mercenaries”? What has England been
through most of her history but a mercenary-employing
power ? She never fought a white race yet without the
use of hired troops until the Boer wars of the 80’s and the
present day. Here are a few comparatively recent in-
stances in which Great Britain has used mercenaries :
In 1756, in view of a threatened French invasion, 6000
Hessians wore brought to England for defence purposes ;
the same Hessians being afterwards employed in the
suppression of th.e ’9B Irish rebellion. Again, during the
American revolt, thousands of Hessians were employed
against the “ rebels.” At Waterloo, also, out of 67,000
troops under Wellington, only 22,000 were British, the
other 45,000 were mercenaries pure and simple ; and at
the conclusion of peace, there were 29,000 foreigners in
the “ British ” army. Coming, later on, to the Crimean
War, the English Parliament passed a Bill, despite Dis-
raeli’s vehement opposition, to authorise the enlistment
of 20,000 foreigners to supplement the British forces at
the seat of war. English agents were despatched to re-
cruit in Europe and the U.S., but only some 5000 men
were embodied—a poor recompense for the humiliation
England suffered in having her consul put on trial at
Cologne and her envoy dismissed from the U.S.—not to
speak of the loss of national prestige. Even at the present
day foreigners can enlist in British regiments, though
only in the proportion of Ito 50 Britishers. It thus ill-
becomes us to sneer at the Boers because they employ
mercenaries—a mere handful at that.
A Jewliauneaburg Jingle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ring the joy-bells for the Chosen,
Who were (tell it not in Gath)
Lately paralysed and frozon
By-the fear of Kruger’s wrath,
Lo ! the British Flag is flaunting
Where the Transvaal banner waved,
And the nosey ones are chanting
“ Our Jewhannesberg is Saved ! ”
What a theme for jewbilation !
Jumping Mo ! it was not strange
That they made a demonstration
On the London Stock Exchange.
Greasy foreigners waxed skiteful,
Most insanely they behaved
When they got the news delightful
That Jewhannesberg was Saved.
Their loud song of joy and rapture
Nearly raised the blessed roof,
For the troops had scored a capture
Of the all-important Oof;
“ Rule Britannia ! ” “ Sovereign Lady ! ”
Britons ne’er will be enslaved.
Each stockbroker smashed his “ cadey ”
When Jewhannesburg was Saved.
Now the Rand mines’ golden treasure
Has been wrested from the Boer
Let us speculate with pleasure
On the richness of the ore,
And forget that stones and crosses
Mark the spots where dead lie graved :
True, we had some trifling losses
But—Jewhannesberg was Saved.

Douaid Macdonald—Victorian Tour.
Good old “Pirates of Penzance ” was re-
vived at Her Majesty’s (Sydney) last Saturday
after an absence of seven years, and a multitu-
dinous Saturday audience took it in a serious
spirit. The loyal and patriotic pirates, who drop
their unlawful ironmongery and lay down their
bloodstained hardware when called upon in the
name of the Queen, and who say that, despite
their blaok flag and the dried brains on their deck,
they still love their Sovereign, were probably
meant by Gilbert as a wild satire on “ loyalty” in
general. But Saturday’s assemblage took it in as
dead earnest as if it had been a hanging or a case
of plague, and sheered violently. The Bulletin
has a dark suspicion that Gilbert isn’t a Jingo.
He represents tne Queen’s authority as being up-
held by seven terrified policemen who don’t ex-
hi bit the valour which the Queen's polioemen
should; and his British Major-General isn’t a
hero ; and the pirates put their sea-boots through
the Royal authority as derisively as if it was
brown paper. The “ Pirates ”is about as violent
j}. British army and police force as
Pinafore” is at the British navy, and just now a
writer who thus throws the bad'egg of mockery
at oherished British institutions should be dis-
couraged as a person who is dragging the public’s
limb. Still, The Bulletin doesn’t mind letting
* Pirates ” off with a caution. #lt is a gladsome
tnnig to meet, especially compared with the work of
Gilbert’s successors, and looking at it one becomes
more and more convinced that it will be centuries
yet before Gilbert’s real successor is born. It
wears wonderfully well, too. The only sign of
years about it is where Frederic, the apprentice,
discovers that he won’t be of age until he is 84,
and, finding that it is y&t 63 years till he is 21,
asks his best girl to wait, and he will call again in
a full-grown state in 1940. That seems to suggest
that the opera has been on deck since 1877, but
otherwise it looks as young and fresh as if bom
yesterday. The British policeman is just as
flushed about the head now as he was 23 years ago,
and the head pirate’s remarks about the degrading
instincts of stockbrokers and such-like trasn have
pnly grown more appropriate with age, for Torah
Hooley has arisen since then.

The Firm has imported a new Mabel, and
Sydney seized the oooasion to drop a tear over the
ever-belovod memory of Nellie Stewart. Miss
Winston-Weir made a rather indefinite first
appearance. She sings very well and acts very
moderately, but there is little scope for acting in
the part (if Mabel. If there was more scope, the
hyphenated lady might be lees moderate, or again
she might be more moderate —it all depends on
the hidden cause of her moderateness. Also she
Beems nervous and looks anxious, and the only
things this writer. is dead sure of so far axe that
she has a sweet voioe and is pleasant to listen to,
and that she looks better sideways than viewed in
front., This doesn’t necessarily imply that she
doesn’t look well when viewed in front. Other-
wise the court is adjourned for further considera-
tion of the case. Dorothy Vane’s Ruth is excel-
lent, or even a little more than excellent, though
sho makes up the pirates’ ancient darner and
bottle-washer as a well-preserved maid of 29, and
when Frederic casts her off on account of her age
the situation is absurd. Still no one would ex-
change the present Ruth for any battered and un-
comely wreck of 47, and if Williamson sets
about putting the anomaly right he will make
enemies. So. far as concerns his aoting of
the Pirate King, the same enthusiastic adjec-
tives as used above apply to Brownlow, and
it is only in a few places that his singing falls
noticeably below his acting. Kenningham and
Kauri (the latter makes the Major-General a close
copy of Roberts, and is warmly cheered for putting
np that stainless warrior as an incompetent soldier
and a bogus orphan) are at least eminently respect-
able, and Hugh Ward and Bathurst are a trifle
more than respectable. The jumpy and eye-
wrecking biograph winds up the show as hereto-
fore, but the writer feels inclined bo aim a smaller
and less remorseless boulder at it than he did last
week. One new picture, a series of views of
S’African soenery, taken from a train at full
speed, compensates for many weary rear-elevations
of many Unthinking Cadgers.
