Sydney has resolved definitely that it
likes Bland Holt’s “ Women and Wine ” and will
take that gorgeous but not too logical production
as it comes. Miss Frances Ross looks very sweet
and heavenly and resigned when she loses her
character through being found calling on a
lawyer on a matter of business, and when she
refuses to explain the business and thereby
drives her shocked lover away with the
bad woman it looks reasonable enough the
way she does it. Later on, when the bad
woman has cleaned him out and left him without
the price of a drink, and he is in danger of being
“scragged” as well, she explains the old mys-
stery, and the hero kicks himself at the top of his
voice. That also looks reasonable—the way she
does it. All the same, if she had explained earlier,
the wreckage of her idol might have been averted,
but the dramatis heroin* ha* no sense in small
things like that.

Rev. Charles Clark, a lecturer too well-
known and esteemed to require elaborate descrip-
tion, paid Sydney a flying last week. And,
aa Rev. Charles arrived on the tail-end of the
war-enthusiasm, he felt bound to start by lectur-
ing on “ British Valor, Old and New,” from Agin-
court downwards. The grand old man pounded
eloquently over many battle-fields; struggled over
heaps of dead men, horses, and piles of cannon and
scrap-iron ; put his venerable head briefly, but
firmly, into the hospitals where the wounded
were getting their legs sawn off ; carefully
dodged the workhouse, where the balance of
Tommy spends his declining vears after
he comes out of hospital, and luxuriates
on the rich gruel of his grateful country: skimmed
over plains, deserts, Soudanese bones, Zulu bones,
Kaffir bones, Boer bones, Afghan bones, assorted
bones, and bones miscellaneous, and said old
things about the Flag in a way that sounded almost
new. He held forth about many other things as
well, but his bright and clever deliverauce on
Valour and Bones pleased the audience best. His
colleague, Mrs. Te-Rangi-Pai, the stately
light-brown lady from Maorilnnd, sang in a
contralto voice in between times. She doesn’t
sing about bones. When her turn came the skele-
tons of those w r ho were gloriously belted in the
good cause had a rest.

Alfred Dampier’s bright and child-like
officer with the stutter continues to scorn Kruger’s
pot-hat at Sydney Criterion in a way that brings
tears of joy to the eyes of all true Britons.
Dampier’s stage lisp, which he has wof-ked into
many parts, isn’t quite like any other lisp that
this writer has met. It makes one feel as if he
was reading an old, old book —one of these time-
yellowed works where the letter “s” is long and
looks like “f, ” so that when the reader comes
across the word “ possession ” it looks like
“poffeffion,”and “suspense”becomes “fufpenfe,”
and he pronounces them that way instinctively.
The gallant officer in question gets off some very
ancient and silly jokes on his besieged country-
men. He is a kindly person, seemingly in some
degree out of his mind, but his smile is a beam of
sunshine, and the story he tells the wicked Boer
about the dog lasts nearly a quarter of an hour.
** * *
At Sydney Tivoli, Professor Almond
oomes out with affairs like sledges on his feet, and
walks about on the points thereof; ho also dances
with roller skates on his feet upon an elevated
stand about the size of a pocket-handkerchief. It
is a clever performance, but it lacks spectacular
effect. If fie' could contrive to fall off without
breaking his neck and every bone in his body the
effect would be better. The Martinottis ' have
struck another long-winded pantomime entitled
“The Keyhole,” in which certain people who
look through keyholes are thereby led into
matrimony—this as a warning to shun the
inviting aperture, in the sure and certain
knowledge that bondage is lurking behind.
The Century Animatographe goes on its tremulous
way and displays pictures from South Africa, and
other views. Some are decidedly good, and
others are put out of court through the tremulous
flicker that seems to be the bugbear of all moving
photographs. Charles Seel is back from Melbourne
and sings a good song about “Jock Magee ” and
another that is of sky-blue complexion. Charles
is a clever artist, and it is a sad thing that he
should resort to suggestive songs.
•* « *
“ Elizabeth,” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.),
was, from this present writer’s standpoint, an
unwise production, not so much because the
piece is a dull jumble of historical facts, as
because it makes the booming of Nance O’Neil
look foolish. Concerning bonnie Nanoe’s share in
this noisy undertaking there can be no two in-
telligent opinions. Physically the fine woman is
disadvantageously placed in her ungracious gown,
huge oollar and stiff red hair. The square-built
skirt takes a couple of inches off her height. Her
muscular arms are lost in the hideous sleeves of
the period, and her back and shoulders make no
direct appeal to public feeling. For the present,
her “Magda ” barrackers are rather disillusioned,’
but they will probably renew their faith when the
massive lady gives them Camille.
• • * •
As a tragedienne Miss Nance O’Neil is not
equal to the part of Queen Elizabeth, and that’s
all about it. Something more than a physique, a
voice, and a scowl are required of her. It is not
always an easy part to play, and the crude, hard
actress fails just where a brilliant, nervous artist
would be strongest. Her full-toned ranting and
viragoic scowl are effective for a while, though
even groundlings grow tired of having their ears
split. In the earlier scenes Queen O’Neil’s rant is
a royal monopoly and a relief to the tedium of the
dialogue. One would prefer that she didn’t de-
claim a soliloquy exactly as though she were talk-
ing to the parson or Von Keller in the pre-
vious drama, but a little fault like that nearly
esoapes observation. The Queen’s stormy method
boosts her along all right until Essex sets
up a rival rant, which is so obviously more
impassioned than the rant of his Sovereign Lady
that she tries to keep up her position by roaring.
And when Nance O Neil roars her top note the
angels weep—they do, indeed. Also the unin-
fatuated sections of the audience laugh. As for
Saturday’s performance of the last act, it was a
ioke, or a have, or both, and if the public had not
been thinking about their last train they might
have felt aggrieved. The unkind scribe who pens
notice (and who would not state the truth so
candidly but for Miss O’N. being elsewhere rated
tar above her merits) remarked the other week
that Magda turned her back or hung her head
whenever she felt unable to produce a suitable
racial expression, or rapid changes of expression.
In short, -N ance O Neil is as artful as she is high,
ihe last act of “Elizabeth” requires her
to impersonate a wicked old hag dying of
general decay and mortal funk, aggravated
by grief for the loss of a young man who
lay sleeping without his head, in the cold, damp
ground, rhe order is too large for Nance O’Neil,
so, m>tead of attempting to look the character on
Saturday, she simply talked it in semi-darkness.
j* intents and purposes, Nance remained in-
i' lsi jle what time she was said to be petering out
m the gloom.

At the Princess’s, a revival of “ Hans the
Boatman ” has happened ere this, unless the advt.
broke its promise. Hans Arnold and the kiddies,
and all the things (bar the big dog) that helped to
make a success in days when Melbourne was
younger and more difficult to please, were under-
stood to fall due on Wednesday of this week.
Charles Arnold’s season at the Princess’s is now
at the Last Nights stage, so the new revival will
again before a lot of people have seen
, bins the Boatman” for the first time, if
they forget to hurry up. Meanwhile, “ The Pro-
fessor s Love Story ” is numbered with the shows
that fail for want of a good start. It was
a charming comedy when Arnold and Co had
played it for a fortnight, and is as likely as not to
get a prosperous run m Sydney later on. Two or
throe changes in the cast, and an all-round de-
termination to make the moat of every bright little
him and small business detail ought to compel a
certain amount of success for sucli first-rate work
as Ihe Profeasor’s Love Story.” Managers and
actors seem to leave undone so many tilings that
nught be done so easily. On the first night of
Barrie s comedy two out of throe Scotch characters
were in hopeless trouble with their Scotch accents,
and the other one was not too happy, yet they
might have pioked up a Sootohman in the street
who would nave taught them their iines for
fifteen ‘ boh ” and the same number of whiskies.

At Melbourne Royal thereareonly medium-
sized audiences for the new farcical melodrama,
so the girl who divorces Tom Foster behind his
back in order to marry the woman-hater, is not
likely to be robbed of her child after next week,
ihe Bulletin has a certain respect for Tom
roster and his friends and enemies, especially the
tall, glowering villain in unusual boots. They
enter with all their hearts into the spirit of the
burlesque, whilst resisting every temptation to
their parts. A more frivolous comedian
than Inman, the common sailor, would pro-
bably favour his messmates with some
song and dance when they seleot him for the
billet of sole lieutenant of a war-ship, but Inman
goes on acting seriously, and leaves the joke to
speak for itself. The bigamist who drinks herself
to death by the light of a thunder-storm is another
conscientious performer. She refrains from ex-
aggeration. She doesn’t spoil the effect by
whistling a solo, or blowing strange noises down
the neck of the empty brandy-bottle. Also, the
scenery is remarkable for self-control, and as for
the final triumph of British arms, in which a brass
band turns the Dervishes’ flank with a patriotic
tune, it is the < quintessence of tragic humor in its
most enthralling aspect. “ Two Little Drummer
Boys will be the next matter at the Royal.

A sweeping change of bill happened at
the Bijou on Saturday night. All the old, faded
and dusty “ turns ” were thrown out in a body,
and a lot of this season’s goods filled the vacuum’.
The Flying Jordans, Venus, the wire-walker, and
Schrader, who falleth like bricks from Heaven off
his bicycle, are new—to the Bijou, anvhow. Also
Miss Daisy Coppin and little Dorothy Leigh and
the Haytors in a sketch called “Desperation.”
Moreover', some of the songsters are there for the
first time, and others had been away for many
weeks before they came back. The bill at
Rickards’ was in need of extensive alterations, and
now the plaintive moan of the pay-box is silenced
until further notice.

The Athenseutn Hall, deserted by the War
Biograph, awaits the arrival of Rev. Charles
Clark and the lady vocalists. The Town Hall,
too, lost Donald Macdonald last week, when that
budding capitalist and good fellow began his in-
vasion of the Transyarra with decisive victories
in Geelong and Ballarat. On Saturday afternoon
Marshall Hall conducted an orchestral coneert at
Melb. Town Hall, and the soulful followers of the
Ormond professor piled chrysanthemums on the
platform, and waved their hats and bonnets, and
beat the air with their umbrellas as evidence of
trouble in store for those godly people who are
just now after the Prof.’s scalp. One way and
another Marshall Hall is a very entertaining in-
fidel.
“ Aletes ” discourses on Australia’s best
woman-pianist:—
Later impressions of Elsie Hall confirm the earlier.
Lacking the elements of greatness, there is yet a suf-
ficiency of charm in her playing to warrant grateful and
generous recognition. Purely feminine in her outlook
she is, and always will be. For which the gods be praised.
There is room, after all, for sex in art—interpretative art,
at all events ; hence we may understand the consistent
absence of Beethoven from her programmes. Given a
dash of defiance, a touch of Bohemian lawlessness and
revolt, of lurking humor, she might succeed, notwith-
standing ; but hers is a serious, placid nature, while the
discipline of school is stamped firmly on her tempera-
ment. Sometimes she surprises by failing just wh' re
failure, for her, would seem absurd. The writer always
feels a little breathless when the Chopin “ Berceuse ” is
developing, fearful lest some false note or rude touch
should jar the delicate gossamer-like web of melody.
And although Elsie Hall handles the theme far too ten-
dency for such a fear to materialise, yet it lacked,
in my opinion, that remoteness, that hint of
far-off fading hills and misty stars, which, to my think-
ing, broods within it. It is a sigh for the unattainable, a
sigh of the soul, pathetic, yet resigned, of a soul content,
indeed, that the unattainable is, and lies before it. Yet
one asks too much. Amid the glare of many lights, the
jar of confusing murmurs, the hundred infinitesimal dis-
tracting details, what artist can give of his best! Re-
clining comfortably in the soft twilight, an ocean of
nothingness around, this graceful and deft-fingered
pianiste might, methinks, beguile me into whole-hearted
recantation of all and sundry heresies concerning her.
rending which one may pray for a continuation of the
Wednesday night recitals, finding therein a comfort that
is missed in these days of ultra-patriotic clamor and vapid
sing-song.

Mayor M‘Eacharn wanted to “buy the
house ” for one performance of the War Biograph
at Melb. Athenaeum last week, but lie was too
late. The Bio. was turning money away, and
seats for each of the few remaining matinees and
night shows were already booked, so his Worship,
for about the first time in Corporation history,
found himself a patron of no importance. It is
remarkable that nobody ever offers to buy the
house when business is bad. No matter how good
a show may be, if it doesn’t happen to get talked
about the management may whistle for a large
wholesale order.

N.S.W. Treasury bills in Sydney, a very remarkable story
hangs thereon. The Lyne Government has made two

cent, in London and one of £500,000 at 3* per cent,
in Sydney. When the 4 per cent, issue was made
the Lyne Government simply scorned the idea of letting
the N.S.W. investor have any of the securities. What
he was offered was Funded Stock at 3 per cent Three
per cent, was quite good enough for the wretched Aus-
tralian, but when a 4 per cent, security was going it was
reserved for the higher-class London buyer. Ana when
London took up the 4 per cent, securities at about £9O
10s for every £lOO Minister John See told a newspaper in-
terviewer that “ nothing could have been gained bv
offering them here ” (in Sydney), and that it was doubtful
if the 4 per cent, securities would have realised £99 10s
in Sydney (this in face of the fact that N.S.W. was at
that time paying £lOO for 3 per cent, securities). Then

The Austral Otis Engineering Co., of South
Melb., has just completed the large Bucket Gold Itredge
for Mr. David Marks, at Kiandra, N.S.W. The dredge lias
started its active career and results have been most satis-
factory. The machinery, which was all built at the Otis
Works, S. Melbourne, has worked without a hitch from
the start, and the Company deserves credit for the expe-
ditious manner in which it executed the contract at so
remote a place. The Otis Co.'s contract was for the
whole plant, including the building of the pontoons on
site and handing the machinery over in full work.*

batteries, rolls, trommels, jigs, classifiers, vanners, &c.,
will be proceeded with by competent engineers.*

Mercury, in the form of sulphides or cinnabar, is
found in N.S.W., Queensland and Maoriland. In N.S.W.
it is found on the Oudgegong River near Rylestone, at
Bingara, Solferino, (Yugilbar and Cooma. The world’s
supply of the mineral was for ages drawn from the Almadan
mines in Spain and those of Idria in Illyria (Austria),
which have been worked for nearly two thousand years.
Latterly these have been eclipsed by the deposits at New
Almaden, in California. China, Japan, Bavaria, Hungary,
Transylvania, Bohemia and Peru all contribute a little to
the world’s stock, but nothing to be considered seriously.
The supply is not kaeping pace with the demand, which
increases rapidly, while the Amerioan yield alone fell from
79,963 flasks in ’7B to 28,100 in ’9O. Last year China
became for the first time a buyer, having imported several
thousand flasks. Prospectors should carefully note, for
it is a solemn truth that a good cinnabar mine may easily
be a more valuable article than anything on the Golden
Mile at Kalgoorlie.

liable, that the alleged great surplus was a lie, that the
vear when he claimed to have a surplus of over £300,000
he really had a deficiency of £550,000, that Reid seized
upon moneys which should have been credited to the time
of Dibbs’s Administration, and put them into his own
revenue, which make his accounts look better and nis
opponent’s look worse, and that he charged back
liabilities to Dibbs’s Administration which should
not have been - so charged, thereby again making
his own accounts look better than they were and
his opponent’s worse. Also that he largely invented the
“Dibbs deficit,” about which he mouthed with such
sickening pretence of political virtue—the committee de-
clares that the shortage which Reid inherited from his
predecessor was £125,090 instead of over £1,000,000, and
that Reid himself piled up a new deficit of nearly £1,000,000
which he concealed by various devices and insisted on
calling a surplus. This was only the sort of result which
might have been expected, for one damning piece of
evidence has been sticking out ever since the great Cash
Basis Fake was performed. It appears in the expenditure
accounts (Reid’s alleged expenditure) for the various
years of his administration :
1895- .. £8,924,804 1897-8 .. £9,462,676
1896- .. 9,491,644 1898-9 .. 9,756,974
The first year was the one when Reid did his great
re-arrangement and piled so much of his own expenditure
back into the “ Dibbs deficit.” He always claimed that
each year was charged with its own proper ex-
penditure, but he never explained why, in the year of the
Great Fake, he managed to run the country for £538,000
less than in any succeeding year, and this without cut-
ting down salaries, or abolishing offices, or sacking
public servants, or defaulting on the public debt,
and with less assistance from loan funds than he
had in every succeeding year, and with £400,000
more to pay for public works out of revenue
than he paid in 1898. Reid’s figures for 1895-6 were
simply an impossibility if he charged that year with its
own real expenditure The fact that he didn’t has been
too conspicuous to be worth arguing about. His only
plea is that his Great Fake was legal, because he passed
an Act which made it legal—an Act so cunningly devised
that Parliament didn’t see the true inwardness of it till
long afterwards. And that only aggravates the Great
Fake. It shows it to have been a carefully premeditated
affair worked out with vast ingenuity.

The National Bank of Tasmania, in its balance-
sheet for half-year to 31st May, 1900, shows another
material improvement. Profits for eight years are as
follows:
November, 1892 £5382 November, 1896 £3534
May, 1893 .. 4776 May, 1897 .. 4012
November, 1893 4701 November, 1897 4127
May, 1894 .. 4618 May, 1898 .. 4129
November, 1894 4695 November, 1898 4516
May, 1895 .. 3989 May, 1899 .. 4459
November, 1895 4136 November, 1899 4678
May, 1896 .. 4299 May, 1900 .. 6528
The profits are equivalent to £6 6s. Bd. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ capital and reserve fund,
which is a good way above the present average. A div.
of 5 per cent. (£3801) and div.-tax (£190) absorb £3991,
and the remaining £1537, added to £3460 brought
forward, leaves £4987 at credit of profit and less. There
has been a considerable improvement in business during
the half-year, and things look well generally. At the
same time, it is difficult to understand why the directors
don’t transfer at least £4OOO of the profit-balance to
reserve-fund, and thus make a start at rebuilding that
much-damaged item. The National Bank’s total reserves
arc now £27,487 (reserve-fund £22,600, and undivided
profit £4987). At the beginning of 1893 they were
£39,074, so there is still a great deal of leeway to make up
before the institution gets back to the moderately strong
position which it occupied seven years ago. Over-anxiety
to pay 7 and 8 per cent, dividends when the National was
still young prevented it becoming a really strong institu-
tion in the good days, and the writing-off of £15,000 to
cover losses in ’94 made it a rather weak one. It has im-
proved a good deal since then, but some better evidence
of a fixed resolve on the part of the directors to strengthen
the reserve-fund would be acceptable.
* * * *
Mr. J. St. Vincent Welch, Sydney manager for
the Commercial Union Insurance Co., defends the pro-
posed extension of the A.M.P.’s business to other parts of
the Empire on the ground that many British life and fire
insurance offices and other concerns do a world-wide
business and prosper. As regards British life-offices this
seems hardly relevant. Because an office belonging to
Britain (where interest rates are about the lowest
and the death-rate is only medium) does well by
spreading to countries where the death-rate is lower
and interest-earnings are higher, that certainly doesn’t
show that the A.M.P. (belonging to a country where the
death-rate is the lowest and interest-earnings compara-
tively high) can improve its position by spreading to a
country where people die faster, and where it can’t get so
much interest on its accumulations. The fact that an
English office is benefited by coming to Australia doesn’t
prove that an Australian office is benefited by going to
England. Nor does the fact that a Hindoo can make
better wages in Australia than in his own land prove that
an Australian can make better wages in India than in his
own land. Nor does the fact that a man with 30s. a
week is benefited by a change to 40s. prove that a
man with 50s. a week is benefited by a change to 40s.
Mr. Welch says (quite truly) that some companies which
do a world-wide business make money, and he adds “ I
can point to local offices here that have been directored
to death.” So can The Bulletin. And several of these
local offices died through doing a foreign business at the
instance of foolish directors —the Pacific was one, and the
Standard of M.L. only pulled up by abandoning a great
part of its foreign business. Also the most prosperous fire
offices here do no foreign business. Curiously enough,
Mr. Welch doesn’t say whether these offices (not named)
which were “ directored to death ” lost their money here
or dropped it through doing a foreign business. This is
an important point.
The Arrow Investment and Mining Co., an
Adelaide concern formed to deal in mines and mining
shares, has put up a dreary record in the two years of its
existence. It started with £40,000 paid-up capital. In
its first balance-sheet (30th Sept., ’9B) it stated that it had
paid for Brookman’s Golden Arrow mine £9959 more than
the asset was worth. Said asset was then put down as
actually worth £22,365, so apparently it cost the co.
£32,324. Next half-year it was announced that the dread-
ful thing had been sold for £6OO, and the co., then only a
year old, wrote off £32,000 of its £40,000 capital. Against
its £BOOO remaining capital it has now losses and depre-
ciation amounting to £3253, which leaves an actual sur-
viving capital of £4747 out of the original £40,000, and the
shareholders have received £l2,ooo"in divs, so their net
loss may be set down at £23,253, made up as follows :
Loss. Profit.
30th September, 1898 .. £3,192

In the half-year ended 30th Sept., ’99, the Arrow Invest-
ment and Mining Co paid £BOOO divs. (out of a profit of
£3822), and in the half-year just closed it paid £4OOO divs.
out of a loss of £5713. And with only £4747 left out of
the original capital of £4o,ooo—the balance having
vanished inside two years—the report alleges that the
Oo.’s position is sound.
* * • *
The little Argus. Permanent Building and
Investment Society, of Melbourne—its good and bad luck
in recent years :
80th April, 30th April,
1893 Profit £941 1897 Profit £lB
1894 „ 366 1898 „ 42
1895 Loss 123 1899 „ 109
1896 Profit 7 1900 „ 237
The Argus is now earning £1 Bs. 2d. per cent, per annum
on its capital of £16,825. Out of £l5OO of reserves £561
has been written off to cover losses and £939 still
remains, also there is a profit balance of £640, making
total reserves £1579. An 8 per cent, dividend was paid in
October, 1892, but since then the Argus has lain very low
and struggled with its small difficulties, much after the
fashion of a fly laboring heavily in a stormy teaspoon.
Still things are obviously coming round, and the Argus
looks a sound office ; though a concern of this size, with
£1773 overdraft and its properties mortgaged for £5472,
has still rather a large contract on hand.
• * * *
On account of the drought-losses sustained
during the year, the Scottish-Australian Investment
00. has been compelled to write off £lOO,OOO as a result
of the year’s work. This amount was taken from the
reserve, reducing it to £lO,OOO. This leaves the co.* to
carry forward a surplus of £2,499. Last year the
co., after paying 6 per cent, on £200,000 stock, 5 per cent,
on £400,000, 4 per cent on £200,000 perpetual debentures,
paid 24 per cent, on £500,000 of ordinary stock.
* • • •
Of all the Barrier’s lead-shows, the Pinnacles
has had the most strange eventful hist Ty. The curious
shape of its outcrop struck the eye of the very early
prospectors, and it was pegged by one of the first half-
dozen bushmen who went across from Menindie long
before the Broken Hill rush began. First the show fell
into .a mostly-Melb. syndicate’s hands, then into a Sydney
co’s, next into a Sydney tribute co’s, then into a re-con-
structed tribute co’s, then back to the original co., then
to a consolidated co., and finally into the New Pinnacle
Group co’s—with probably two or three forgotten ones
in between. The ore is plentiful, fairly rich, with occa-
sional very fine bunches, but mostly refractory, on
account of the presence of pyrrhotine ; yet, despite the
difficulty of treating the stuff, it is the best mine on the
Barrier off the main Broken Hill lode, so far as the work-
ings allow one to judge. Nearly all the time it has been
controlled from Sydney ; and that alone means failure,
according to an invariable rule in relation to Barrier mines.
Mostly the trouble has been (as it generally is) that the
new co. began with no capital to speak of; and as
every new co. has had its own idea of what the plant
should be, a practically new start has been made each time,
and no co. has been able to get in calls long enough to give
the thing a fair trial. It altogether affords a shocking
example of the asininity of starting on a big refractory-
ore “ proposition ” with empty pockets, relying upon a
population which knows little or nothing of mining to
keep the calls going. YVhen the present co. was formed
with a ludicrously inadequate capital, this writer sug-
gested that it would be a more Christian act to give him
the money at once and save further trouble. Somehow
the shareholders didn’t see it in that light; so they hung
on, paid in a call or two when they felt that way, and now
have whatever satisfaction they can get out of the
announcement that their mine has been “ taken over” by
the Queensland Smelting 00., and that their manager has
been sued in Broken Hill for unpaid workmen’s wages.
. * * • *
Sulphide Corporation, which owns the B.H.
Central mine, follows its div. of 10 per cent, paid last
Xmas with one of 7£ per cent. The co. is managed from
London, with Messrs. Gibbs, Bright and Co. as Australian
agents, and it has not been made very clear what this 7J per
cent, really is, but it is probably about six months’ profits.
Chief-metallurgist Savage, of Cockle Creek works, is
on the way to London to consult with the directors about
a proposed extension of ore-reducing works. Mr. Savage
is a young Australian-trained official who has already done
work which has won warm praise from professional men
in Europe and America, and still he remains the most
modest of men.
• • • •
Kenney, Frewin & Co., at Long Flat, Gundagai,
N.S.W., appear to have made a really splendid find in
well-defined Lucknow-like lode-channels, picked ore from
which looks like from 60 to lOOoz. stuff. Previous
crushings have gone from 3 to 18oz. Other excellent
local developments are reported.
Occidental, Cobar, now running a 40-head mill,
is gradually equipping itself out of gold returns. The
story is that divs. will be suspended until the mine is
provided with 50 head of stamps and a rock-drilling outfit,
after which there will be an increased division of profits.
Cobar Gold Mines has been running 100-head and a huge
cyanide plant for many months, but local shareholders
know next to nothing about results. All information is
carefully bottled up and sent to London. So effectually
are the mine’s secrets kept that t’other day a well-known
Sydneyite sold his 1000 shares in disgust at 6s. each,
though they were possibly enough worth 255. So much
for London management, which is the curse of Australian
mining, and will have to be made sit up by local legisla-
tion.
« * • *
Cobar Chesney has installed a skilled metallur-
gist as general manager, and is putting in a plant to con-
centrate 200 tons of ore daily. The lode is 30 to 40ft.
wide where now seen, and the ore from the two levels
worked has averaged 5 '3l per cent, copper, which should
mean substantial profits, concentration expenses not-
withstanding. t
Baker’s Creek Consols hung up—for re-flotation
in London.

By all accounts Peak Hill (N.S.W.) Proprietary
Mine’s smelting operations are a success. Once the fact
is established, a big future awaits the Proprietary and
adjacent mines.

A syndicate has pegged out a large area of
swampy flats around Kelly’s Basin for gold-dredging.
Prospects are said to be very rich—and flotation is nearly
sure.

The Australian Smelting Co., which during the
Barrier boom coined money at Dry Creek smelting works,
S.A., finds business in these sulphide days not what it
used to be ; and so it goes out of the business shortly.

• * * *
“London” wants somebody to tell him about
the prospects of the Retrieve Mount Morgan, Q.
Cashman’s Golden Gate 3 and 4 S. mine, Croy-
don, is held under lease by a private syndicate : portion
of mine let on tribute. The Tribute Syndicate during May
obtained 31720 z. gold from 558 tons (gold £2 12s 5d per
oz.), or £8312 2s Bd. Proprietary received £l5OO royalty,
and the Tribute divided £5750. carrying forward £317.
Tribute expires in August.. Mine paid during May
£15,360 in divs., a record for the field.
Several Croydon deep shafts on Golden Gate line
are nearing “ bottom," ana the boomster is already on the
war-path. Recently, an attempt was made to boom
shares in a co. known locally as “ Drawbridge’s,” and to
distant shareholders as the Golden Gate and Nancy Lee
Consols G.M. Co., Ltd. The attempt was made by means
of the old telegram dodge. Somebody wired that 3ft. of
golden ore had been bored through ; scrip was anchored,
wire shown round, and at attempt made to un-
load. Local paper, Mining News, sent its reporter down
the mine, and put the fake away. Found about Bin. stone
and not enough gold to swear by. Then there was gnash-
ing of teeth in boom-circles.
• * * *
Warrentina (Tas.) gold-field is looking up of
late. A fair amount of work is being done on some
allegedly rich lodes and they are waiting the right moment
for flotation.
« * » *
At Dunedin and on the West Coast of M.L.
lately dredging-prospectuses has been appearing at the
rate of about one per day ; and the craze is extending to
Southland district, where river-options are being secured
by speculators and capital is being sunk in all directions.
One firm of engineers has let contracts for dredges total-
ling £82,100, and is inviting tenders for another batch,
estimated at a similar amount. Then 54 other dredges
are being designed—cost, about £7OOO each.

flFor the year ended 31 Dec. last, Ivanhoe Co.
earned net £286,000 as compared with £140,000 for
previous term. The Co. recovered 90'3 per cent of gold-
contents. Mr. Hewitson gave the mine’s ore-reserves on
December 31 last as 211,873 tons.
* * * *
At last, whipped up to it for six months by
public opinion, the Adelaide directors of the Associated
are rising in self-defence. Flouted and fleeced by the
London jibers, Australian shareholders at last realise
how mistaken they were in crediting Bull with a reason-
able amount of honesty. Adelaide henceforth demands
the fullest and earliest information.
* * • *
Fraser’s G.M. Co., the old-established W.A.
low-grade affair, got for the half-year 36700 z. gold for
11,326 tons crushed—6}dwt. per ton, but it didn’t pay
expenses, by £1034. 200,000 tons of tailings available,
which, when treated, are expected to pay off £3OOO over-
draft and the cost of a £2OOO cyanide plant.
Brownhill Extended (Hannan’s) has a good
position—on the map, but unfortunately its own develop-
ment and that of the Brownhill, as well as the Associated
Northern, prove beyond doubt that the lode passes
through the corner of the lease at an acute angle, and the
strike is away from it instead of into it. These shares
were at one time under ss. and within five weeks they
were over £6. It had a few rich crushings, but the pro»-
Eect of more is remote. Two of the directors, Captain
epage and Mr. Oordner James, recently Inspected the
mine and “ an order has been issued that no visitors are
to be allowed on the mine unless armed with their written
authority.’’ Writer found some consolation in the fact
that the best mines on the camp are the easiest to get
down, and there is probably not one mine closed to
visitors that has not got something unsatisfactory to oon-
ceal. There can be no doubt that had the Brownhill lode
continued through the Extended lease it would have been
mopped up by the Brownhill long ago, and that was the
movement in contemplation when the shares boomed last
year.
• • • •
Perseverance is the best-pegged mine on the
fields, although that was an accident. It does not lose
any of its own lodes and it will certainly get one of the
Associated’s in depth. Being in a valley its oxidised ores
turn into sulphides within 100 ft. of surface and with the
exception of one or two shipments the sulphide bodies are
intact. Mine splendidly developed to 600 ft. with levels
driven and everything in readiness as far a* mine is con-
cerned for a continuous big output. Not much oxidised
ore left, but enough to last till the completion of the
sulphide plant, which is practically a copy of the Great
Boulder’s. Dry crushing in Griffin mills, roasting in a
different design of furnace, grinding in amalgamating
pans, the residues going to the filter presses. Has one of
the biggest main shafts in the fields, and an assured div.
future before it. No movement in shares likely till the
success of the sulphide plant is beyond doubt, all on
account of the ignominious failure of the Associated
sulphide plant and the partial failure of that of Lake View.
• • * *
Pride of the Valley, Hannan’s : Writer found it
impossible to find this mine by its name. Even when on
the lease nobody living on it seemed to know it by that
designation, but immediately one of the inhabitants was
asked to point out the place where an “ old gray horse ”
worked, identification was established. Pity some Sydney
shareholders could not see the spot on which they threw
away thousands of pounds. It apparently never had a real
show of finding anything ; cannot be regarded as even a
speculation ; and to talk of picking up good lodes worked
in other mines was mere moonshine. It is one of the
great laughing-stock# of the place.
* * • »
Chaffer’s. — For nearly two years this mine has
been given to announcing “ developments” with astonish-
ing regularity. At last they became too monotonous,
and shares fell from 245. to 12s. in two months. About a
quarter of a million drop. Why this mine should ever
have had a valuation of half-a-million has always been an
enigma to the writer, and it is now quite as great a one
that it should be worth its present price, over £250,000.
It has never had a crushing worthy of the name ; there is
no pay-ore, much less a div., in sight, and it has done
enough work to prove the ore-poverty of the lease. True,
it occupies a splendid position right on the southern boun-
ary of the Golden Horseshoe and the Boulder, but if its
area, 12 acres, happened to belong to either of those
mines, to what extent would it have increased their value?
Certainly not £lOO,OOO. The Great Boulder itself has a
lease on the northern boundary of the Ivanhoe, with much
better prospects than Chaffers, but it has never been
taken into account by the market, and the opportunity of
floating it into a separate co. hag been lost for long if not
for ever. Big as the slump has been in Chaffer’s the mine
is still monstrously over-valued.
* * * •
Paringa Consolidated (Hannan’s) is a reconstruc-
tion that took over Cassidy’s Hill, the Sir John Forrest,
and the Paringa leases, concerning which Mr. C. E. Hogg,
0.E., cabled that the management was excellent, that
some’ of the veins went as high as lOoz. to the ton, and
that the future was assured. But Mr. Hogg is a gentle-
man too much inclined to go the whole hog. The co. is
working the Paringa lease, which is between the Brown-
hill and the North Kalgurli; it has a modest capital of
half-a-million, and has issued over 700,000 ten-shilling
shares. Recently boomed up to par, they are now worth
about 75., and at 7d. would not be too cheap. There are
veins in other leases besides the Paringa that will go over
lOoz. to the ton. The getting of the ton is the only
trouble. .
* * * *
Brownhill has started to put up the plant for the
treatment of sulphides per Diehl process. The mine’s
present dry-crushing plant is the most elaborate in Aus-
tralia. Built entirely of iron ; has an elevation of over
70ft; a capacity of 200 tons a day ; must have oost over
£lOO,OOO and deals with the oxidised ores with a high
extraction.
• • » »
The Boulder Workers’ Association was cornered
the other day when the truckers at L. V. Consols struck
work owing to their wages being reduced from IJs. Bd. to
10s per day. The Mine Managers’ Association some time
ago fixed the truckers’ wages at 10s. 6d. But the Boulder
and Ivanhoe were then paying 10s. and—the workers
making no move to get the extra 6d—have continued
paying that wage up to now. L. View Manager M'Kinnon
mentioned these mines when a deputation protested
against his reduction, and the men, finding their attitude
scarcely logical under the circumstances, took a poll, re-
sult of which favoured the acceptance of the 10s. 6d they
were ultimately offered, with the assurance that truckers’
work would be trucking only. Ex-manager Callahan’s
plan wa* to employ truckers who could do miners’ work
when wanted. Golden Horsehoe and many others do the
same, and pay 11s. Bd. M'Kinnon didn’t mention them.
The greatest difference, however, between the truckers’
work at the Boulder and Ivanhoe, and at the L. V. Consols
and Horseshoe mines, is that the former’s trucks go three
to the ton, while the latter’s are alleged to hold I7cwt.
So the truckers’ protest was not so illogical as it might
appear.
* « • *
Fremantle Smelting Works. Westralian Govt,
continues to feed these with a spoon, this time using a
larger one than the last; and how big the spoon will have
to be to keep them going time will show. When they
were started it was declared by experts that there was
plenty of lead and copper ores in YVestralia within easy
distance of rail or sea to keep the works busy. As the
works had to buy lead from Broken Hill mines, and are
said to have made a loss of over £20,000 in one lead
transaction, W.A. Govt, now comes to the rescue with an
offer of bonuses amounting to £3OOO for the discovery and
production of lead, and has also issued a regulation agree-
ing to convey ore and concentrates up to lOoz. value for
jd. per ton per mile, same as charged on 2oz. ore
going elsewhere.
By the way, in addition to the higher freight on
ores above that value there is an export duty of 6s. per
ton on all ores the value of 10ozs., and 10s. on all above.
This is protecting the Westralian mining industry with a
vengeance, and indicates what outrages the Forrest Govt,
is capable of. Nothing of this sort exists at Johannes-
burg.

Society
For many decades it had been the custom
of S.M. Herald to report Governors’ speeches
word-for-word, in the first person. The rule has
been departed from in Gov. Beauchamp’s case.
Hunter-street has the temerity to cut him down,
sub-edit him, and put him in the third person.
Once Granny would have regarded it as high
treason and ten broken commandments to put a
Governor in the humiliating, inconsequential,
damp, musty, and degrading third person. Third
person is the nearest relative of no person—of
nobody. \Y hat has Gov. Beauchamp done to the
Herald ?
* • » .
Most of N.S.W. opposition leader Reid’s
attack on the Lyne Govt, fell flat, but the one part
of it which roused both sides to cordial acquies-
cence and sympathetic cheers was his reference to
the impudent appointment of Minister Wood to
the command of the Australian Horse, vice
Kenneth Mackay.
FITZGIBBON-THE MAN IN CHARGE.
-r-, T , .
Premier Lyne accounts for the appoint-
ment of N.S.W. Justice-Minister Wood to the
command of a cavalry corp 3 on the grounds that
Colonel Mackay, before he left for South Africa,
had asked the G.O.C. to appoint Mr. Wood a
major in the Australian Horse, as he, Colonel
Mackay, thought the welfare of the regiment
would be looked after thereby during his absence
from the province. So, in N.S.W., the amateur
C.O. not only owns his own corps, body and soul
(vide Supreme Court judgment), but may nominate
his successor ! Also, the possession of the most
elementary military knowledge,, or of what
Roberts calls “the very exceptional qualities
necessary for a cavalry leader,” is not essential to
the commander of this corps; and it is only
necessary that he should live in Sydney “to deal
with certain matters.” What are those “matters”?
It would be interesting to know.
% ■» • « »
Premier Lyne made a rather lame reply to
ex-Premier Reid’s charge of appointing his son
and the son of Minister See to commissions in the
Lancer Regiment over the heads of competent
men with an implied right to the positions. His
chief defence was absolute ignorance. He spent
over £lOO in equipping his boy in the necessary
fal-lals for a lieutenant, and apparently expected
the House to believe that his son had not discussed
the matter with him. The Premier also remarked
that the morning papers would not refer to these
appointments; yet he also said that when they
were “criticised” the young men withdrew.
They certainly did withdraw—in a violent hurry
and at a cost of £lOO each. The squadron openly
mutinied and their “criticism” consisted in flatly
declining to go into camp, coupled with a threat
of a wholesale resignation. The Premier did not
deny that the boys were appointed on the single
qualification of being Cabinet Ministers’ sons.
Ihe first day one of the young hopefuls appeared
m full war-paint a non-com. had to carefully ex-
plain to him the proper way to draw his sword.

On Queen’s Birthday, when Gov. Gorman-
ston, of Tasmania, gave his levee and there was a
big attendance to celebrate the relief of Mafeking,
the local Council of Churches was absent, to a
man. The reason popularly assigned is that Gov
Gormanston is of the R.C. faith !

It was generally predicted that when those
printed “letters from the front” found their way
back to S’Africa there would be trouble. Well,
now we have it. Private H. Bradstook, of H.
Company (N.S.W.), 2nd Regt., writing to his
mother:—
I saw two of Sid’s letters in the E. News, and one of
Sandy Hardie’s, which were straight enough, but some of
tiie letters the chaps have been writing to their parents
are a iot of lies, for we never saw a Boerat Enslin. There
is one chap in particular, whose letter was published in a
Bathurst paper. The captain is going to nave his letter
read out to all, and give him a lecture. That ought to
show him up a bit. We were thinking about giving him
a duck in a dam close by.
* * • *
A very well-known former resident of
Junee (N.S.W.), now serving at the front, writes:
They are starting now to enrol a Colonial Police Force
for the Free State. A lot of our chaps have volunteered
for it. The pay is 10s. a day and forage allowance. I
don’t think many of them will get it, however, as they
are a poor iot—mere lads, jockey boys, hangers-on at
country pubs and billiard-rooms, and bicycle riders.
There are only about 60 good sorts in the squadron. The
whole thing was messed up at the start by the selection
of such men. They are always trying to shirk their work,
and instead of taking a pride in their horses and looking
after them, they have to be driven to feed them. It is
awful, and I would like to join another squadron. Our
men are caving in. I fear we are a badly-selected lot; no
less than 24 out of 130 in the hospital at Bloemfontein
with fever and one thing and another.
The sort of letter that does not get into the daily
press—just yet.
• » • •
The brains of Melb. University are all on
the side of Marshall-Hall with the exception of
thote which have good practical reason for opposing
him. Prof. Allen and Dr. Bride lead the Hall
party, and as Bishop Goe heads the persecuting
gang its rank-and-file may be well imagined. Dr.
McFarland is about the only man of intellect
behind Goe and—well he holds a good post in a
Scotchbyterian College. The Hallites showed bad
tactics at the first meeting, when they could easily
have carried a motion for re-appointment.
Instead they allowed the minority to bluff them
into a postponement which led to next meeting
being swamped by the Stiggins crowd. Heaven
and earth are now being moved to oust Hall.
* * * *
There was a big wreck—that of the Sierra
Nevada—at Portsea (Vic.) lately, and the local
Samaritan came out strong. He fished out the
very few survivors, and warmed and fed them, and
he buried the nine dead like a man and a brother.
The nine graves stand in a row, as a silent evi-
dence that kindliness of heart belongs to all lands
alike, and a touch of nature makes the whole
world kin. A further evidence is a bill of £54 for
funeral-expenses, which has just made the Vic.
Government sit up—£l3 10s. for digging nine
graves, £4 14s fid for nine burial services at 10s 6d
each, £35 15s 6d for sundries; also another bill
for £37 for rubbing-down the survivors and giving
them beef-tea, &c. A certain inclination to curse
the good Samaritan of Portsea is manifest in
Melbourne just now.
* * * *
The other day a Carlton J.P.— Carlton is
the place where they deal tenderly with the most
savage of larrikins—ordered an old man to gaol

arrears for the maintenance of his son, who had
been committed to the department of Neglected
Children, The man is a small stall-holder in the
market, and his average earnings are 14s. per
week, out of which he has to pay ss. for rent and
keep himself and his wife in food. When the un-
fortunate man tried to explain that he was 60
years old, and had never been in Court before,
and for that reason thought it hard he should be
made a criminal of, lie was sternly ordered to be
careful what he was saying or he’d get seven days
additional.

• • * *
Salomons, Q.O.’s impending return to
N.S.W. will make a difference to the local bar.
At present the only Q.C. available for Govt, briefs
is “ Dick ” O’Connor—which probably accounts
for his non-acceptance of a seat in the Upper
House. The rule with regard to members of
either House being debarred from accepting Govt,
work—a very wise rule, too—deprives the Crown
of the services of Want and Pilcher. Salomons
will, as a matter of course, share the Crown briefs
with O’Connor. And a magnificent harvest looms
ahead for the Devil’s Children—the Sydney water-
front resumption cases.

Several N.S.W. country journals issued
gratis second editions chronicling the relief of
Mafeking. “Stood me £3,” said one ; “stood
me £5,” said another.
The editor of the
Mountaineer set an
example worth remem-
bering. He fee’d the
local bellman, and for
5s had the news oried
lustily through the
streets.
UP TO DATE.
“'Ere yer are — on'y a penny a time, and all
British-Born !”
Chief Secretary Jenkins told a questioner
in the 3.A. Assembly that it is the duty of the
Govt. House sentries to challenge any suspicious-
looking person entering the enclosure. Evidently
their authority stops at the challenge, for the other
day one of them asked a seedy man what his busi-
ness was, and got for reply, “ What the devil has
that to do with you ? ” The stranger passed on,
and amused himself by breaking the vice-regal
windows.
• • * •
The shocking inaction of the police on re-
cent occasions in Adelaide will probably lead to
an agitation for reform now that 3. A. Parliament
has met. There is a strong impression that Com-
missioner Madley, formerly a Govt, school-
teacher, is unsuited for the position. The other
night, when ex-priest Slattery was lecturing, a
howling mob held complete possession of a portion
of the city for hours and defied the police to the
number of two !
• • • •
In Sydney Hospital died recently of
starvation and exposure an old man of 80 years
who had been found by the police on the steps of
the Salvation Army’s Sydney People’s Palace,
where he had been lying all a wet and bitter
night. It is a grim satire on the loud-mouthed
“philanthropy” of the Harmy that a poor old
creature should have been allowed practically to
die on its threshold, for want of the base, ungodlv
“ thrum.” '
• * • •
“ Ratting ” is more profitable than rabbit-
ing in M.L. In Dunedin the animals are haled
to the gasworks—the headquarters of the rat in-
dustry—from early morn to dewy eve. Dear little
children bring them in their schoolbags along with
their sum-book and their joggraphy and their
bread and jam “piece.” A Wellington young
lady rode up with rodents dangling from her
bicycle; but this was outdone in Dunedin by a nice
old Scotch body who lifted her outer skirt and
disengaged from round her waist several rats, ex-
plaining, “ one frae Mrs. Maeallister, ma neebor,
and twa from a trap, and this one ma cat caught—
the guid beastie.” The next lot of goods for the rat-
emporium came in a pram, and it is said that baby
yelled for half-an-hour when forced to give up her
playthings.

Christchurch Spectator (M.L.) has 'been
giving the British generals in S’Africa fits for
their incompetence, and so on. When “Colour-
Sergeant ” wrote to say that perhaps said generals
weren’t quite such fools as they looked, the editor
replied, “ In spite of ‘ Col.-Serg.,’ we can inform
our readers that nine-tenths of our volunteers are
of our opinion.” Poor Buller!

A Tommy Atkins in a letter to an English
paper:
“ I really think the Colonial lacks the reckless courage
of the British soldier when all’s told.”
That Tommy had better be put down as a disguised
Boer emissiary and shot accordingly. It will save
much painful argument.

A Leichhardt (Sydney) resident has re-
ceived from her brother, a gunner serving in
S’africa with N.S.W. “A” battery, a letter which
is published in Sydney E. News. Extracts
The Boers are the most contemptible (ate) beings on the
face of the earth, and when you get them beat they cringe
like a dog. ... At Modder River there were thousands
of prisoners, who were strictly watched. A sentry was
asked by a Boer if there was any chance of escape. “Oh,
there might be,” replied the sentry, who turned away as
if to give him an opportunity to escape. Two Boers then
attempted to make off, but were immediately shot dead
by the sentry.

A Kind Neighbour.
The kindest and most neighbourly thing one
woman can do for another, in case of illness, is to
tell how she herself was brought out of trouble
and distress : and urge her neighbour to seek the
same remedy.
“ Neighbour, you are looking glad !
What good news have you to tell ? ”
“ News ? That, when I was so bad,
Warner’s Safe Cure made me well! ”
Thousands have cause to bless the same kind,
neighbourly spirit which actuated Mrs. Melle, of
Abercrombie-street, Balwyn, Victoria,*who wrote
on the 29th. January last as followsl have
good cause to be thankful for what Warner’s Safe
Cure and Safe Nervine have done for me. By
their use I was saved from an early grave and
much pain and suffering. During some great
family trouble I was attacked by heart weakness
and. swelling under my breast and around the
head, accompanied by nervous depression to an
alarming degree. The slightest unusual noise
would make me tremble all over. I became very
irritable and lost flesh rapidly, and was altogether
unfit for my domestic duties. I was under the
doctor’s treatment for some time, but continued
to get worse. The doctor said he could hold out
no hope for me, so in despair I tried Warner’s
Safe Cure and Safe Nervine, and very soon began
to improve in health and spirits. The nervousness
left me, swelling and pains vanished. The doctor
and friends were surprised at my rapid recovery.
I am pleased to say I am now in good health.*

Sydney juries struggled nard last week to
measure sentimental damages in pounds sterling.
One plaintiff, held to be libelled by the Evening
News (which, trapped by a fake paragraph, was
led into publishing an account of a wedding that
never occurred) recovered £300; though defend-
ants were plainly innocent of malice, and instantly
apologised. Another plaintiff was a maker of
kola-nut mixture; and, when _ charged with
assaulting a publican’s wife, a rival kola firm
advertised rather pointedly that .the assailant
did not play in their yard. A jury gave him £2o;
though on the reports he had a far worse grievance
than the £3OO man. A third jury, led by Justice
Owen, gave £1250 damages to the mother of a
“seduced ” girl for “loss of services.” Altogether
matters were mixed.
• * * *
Compare that Sydney jury’s recent award
of £1250 to a mother for loss of her daughter’s ser-
vices (through seduction) with the £l5OO “com-
pensation” to the railway-smashed Taylor family,
of Victoria, which had a daughter killed and the
mother and two sons severely injured (one boy
losing an arm and a leg). Now which award was
absurdly large, or which ridiculously small ?
• * * •
The Rockhampton (Q.) Chows have been
“ proceshing,” with a view to scaring away the
plague. There were two lorries with lanterns,
Chinese bands, gorgeous banners, and torches;
also crackers galore, and two dragons with
hideous pasteboard heads, and tails made of long
rope. A big, fat Chow brought up the end to cavort
around and give finish to the show. Their first
visit was to the shop of a sick countryman, where
they raised a horrible din with crackers. Then
they marched through the principal streets,
stopping in front of the infected places and repeat-
ing the cracker-business. Their fire-baslcets—on
long poles and burning some pungently-smoking
wood—effectually kept a clear space for the
dragons to jump around in, and made enough
vapor to fumigate all Rockhampton

A far-west (N.S.W.) publican, being sued
for wages recently by his barman, pleaded that he
had acted the Good Samaritan to that hired beer-
dispenser, and had taken him in out of charity
when he was “ stony.” He didn’t deny that the
barman worked the usual awful public-house
hours, or that he served the beer efficiently and
dispensed the gin in capable fashion. He didn’t
deny, either, that when he took a barman as a
matter of business he would have to pay him, and
if you save £1 a week by an act of charity, where
does the charity come in ? The publican simply
said he was amazed and shocked that a man whom
he engaged when he was dead-broke should want
any wages. The barman got a verdict, and the
publican went away, still shocked. Reminds The
Bulletin of another story. A widow was in deep
distress and wanted laundry-work, and a chari-
table society woman, hearing of this, sacked her
regular wash-lady (ss. a day), and gave the poor
creature a show. She did 12 hours’ hard washing,
and was offered sixpence. And when she put in a
plea for at least half-a-crown, the benevolent lady
was pained at her ingratitude. “ I only took you
out of charity," she said.

Bit of typical “ fine-writing ” re an acci-
dental-poisoning case, taken from Australia’s
dullest and most respectable paper:—
The landlord had spread some poison, known as “ Rough
on Rats,” upon some sandwiches with the object of
bringing about the death of the large vermin which the
composition referred to is calculated to exterminate.
Surely the acme of journalese !
* • • *
Someone writes in the N.S.W. country
press that the late Parkes was always ready, even
when Premier, to enter the private bar of an hotel
and drink with an acquaintance, provided the
acquaintance provided champagne. Now the fact
is that Parkes never, while Minister or private
member, entered private bars to drink. He
attached no virtue to this—just didn’t do it, like
cutting his hair. N.S.W. Ministers of the Crown
are divided as to the practice of hotel drinking.
Lyne’s batch are unusually liberal in their views
that way. Some of the defunct Reid Cabinet
wouls rather cook their financial statements and
seal a dozen political jobs than take a single six-
penny drink at a hotel bar. But ordinarily
Ministers admit that their office carries certain
social restraints and keep fairly within the imagi-
nary lines.
• * * *
Launceston (Tas.) had a weary little drunk
on Mafeking Day. After the local constable had
fired three shots out of an old gun in front of the
Town Hall, the populace rubbed its eyes and
meandered round, and enquired “YVhat’sup?”
When they quite grasped that Mafeking was re-
lieved no one neglected his beer for his work for
several days ; and ever since Launceston has been
gathering up its boots from among many snakes
and wondering where it has been.

mock banquet.

That celebrated English surgeon Frederick
Treves, F.R.C.S., addressed a crowded meeting
of the Royal Medical and Chirurgical Society in
London recently, and proceeded to knock the
stuffing out of another Boer atrocity. The alleged
verdigris with which the Boer bullets were said to
have been poisoned had been analysed
and proved absolutely non-poisonous. It was
only paraffin, which acting on the nickel
of the bullets, showed a green color, and
it was used to prevent the rifles foul-
ing. Some day, when the atrocity-monger
has ceased to manufacture sensational
lies for heavy news-headlines the
British nation will awaken
to the fact that the Boers
have fought a fair
straightforward,
manly battle,
and having
failed, deserve
the respect of a
generous foe.

Commissioner Turner, of Melb. Metro-
politan Board of Works, is a little man with a
squeaky voice and a great flow of language. He
is also a most aggravated Jingo, even for the Board
of Works, and at the meeting to consider the
sacking of those anti-Jingo employes he “ waxed
awful. “ There was the Union Jack ! he cried,
“ the flag that braved a thousand years the battle
and the breeze.” Then old chairman Fitz Gibbon
interrupted. “Asa matter of chronological ac-
curacy,” he pompously said, “the Union Jack is
only 200 years old.” Poor little Turner tried to
finish his speech, but his eloquence bad gone
bung. And, by-the-way, the present Union Jack
isn’t quite one hundred years old.
The just complaints as to the inferiority
of much of the army stores—flour, saddles, etc.
sent from N.S.Wi to South Africa are not alone
common in army contracts ; they are common
wherever Govts, employ contractors to feed,
clothe or otherwise provide any large body of
people. When N.S. W. was in its infancy over
100 years ago, Gov. Phillip again and again re-
proached the British Govt, for the scandalously
•bad quality of the food, merchandise and tools
sent to his order. “One would think,” wrote
Phillip, “that you sent them out for barter on
the coast of New Guinea.”

The reports of the unveiling of the new
statue of Queen Victoria at Ballarat were full of
enthusiastic references to Councillor This and
Councillor That, and to the loyal utterances of
Fat, Esq., and thirteen different reverends and
Moneygrubs, but not one solitary syllable was
said about Mackennal, the insignificant artist,
who hewed the stone. The statue is merely an
advt. for a handful of “prominent persons ”—a
standing column, in fact.
• • * *
Lost in the mists of history is the date
when N.S.W. Parliament authorised the construc-
tion (out of loan-money) of large gaols at Cooma
and Glen Innes. These institutions were built at
a cost of about £40,000 ; and up to date have not
received a single prisoner, nor have they been
used for any purpose whatever. The latest idea
is to thrust the buildings on to the Chief Secre-
tary’s department as institutions for refractory
children, who, though unfit for the usual boarding-
out process, are not bad enough to be committed
under the Industrial Schools Act, the only now-
available remedy. Both places, Cooma especially,
are intensely cold in winter, and owing to this,
combined with inaccessibility and other objections,
the idea is not looked on with favor by the officials

CORRIGENDUM.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In olden times ’twas quite the thing
(Before these days of nice distinction !)
To call the good physician “leech,”
Who helped along our race-extinction.
But this is changed ! ’Tis but a step
From one profession to its brother.
And we ought now, to designate,
The lawyer “ leech,” and not the other.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
, r Sydney, Jun® 19, 1900.
Mt Dear Myee,—
An interesting' account of a voyage ’Ome was
given me the other day. On board was a pretty,
lively little Sydney girl, who had once been on the
stage, going away to get over a recently-broken-
off engagement—not theatrical. Also, a hand-
some young Sydney eligible, who had hitherto re-
mained impervious to local belledom. The num-
ber of women who have described that man as
“my ideal of a man ” defies contemplation. Let
us pass over that. On board, the pretty little Syd-
nev girl used to sit on the next lounge to him.
Before the end of the voyage their engagement
was announced. They will probably be married
m London, from the house of a big sister of the
bride. To satisfy your curiosity, I suppose I had
better mention that the gentleman is Mr. Harry
Austin, of Sydney ; and the lady, Miss Loloa
Roberts, on her way to her sister, Mrs. Stanley
Mylius. J
1 here were lots of Australians on that boat.
After Ismailia, their proprieties were quite too
severely put upon. Two women and a man, who
came on board there, made a sensation by drawing
three cigarette-cases out of their pockets at dinner,
and smoking three calm and steady smokes before
the whole table ! Australians were dreadfully
shocked !—until they found out that the women
were the Hon. Mrs. K and the daughter of a
Queensland millionaire, whose entertainments are
the talk of London, and the man was the brother
of the young Countess of Dudley.
A new engagement is that of Miss Edith Marks,
the farr-haired, eldest daughter of Mrs. James
Marks, of Elystan, Woollahra, to Mr. Arthur
Shuts, of Goldsbrough, Mort & Co.
Do you know, I have come to the conclusion
that ‘Rumour” isn’t a she after all. I believe
its a he, and a parson—because he’s always
marrying someone. Rumour’s latest is that the
ladies man ” of all our politicians is going to be
married soon—to the well-known widow of a racing
man. °
On dit, of a certain engagement, that the fianc6
with one e—began by admiring the mother,
went on with the eldest daughter, and ended with
marrying little sister.
The wickedness of marrying two people without
asking them was demonstrated by a recent libel-
action brought by a ohemist against a Social
column. The chemist had been wedded for ever
so many years to an excellent wife, who used to
help him in his business, when suddenly a social
column broke into .a graphic description of a
marriage that had just happened between that
same ohemist and quite another lady, named “Miss
Brown, of Crown-street.” Not a false detail
was missing, down to the bride’s uncle who gave
her away, and the obliging guest who livened up
the nuptial with “ Remember me no more.
Many of the chemist’s customers left suddenly on
reading this. For the flight of imagination the
chemist got damages—£ooo. Patients will now
nock back to have their teeth pulled out in com-
fort by a decent and more-than-substantially-vin-
dicated married man.
It is quite possible that that verdict will affect
all social columns more or loss. There is even a
faint hope that we have chanced on the beginning
of the end of Mrs. Jones’ Musical Party, as
recorded in the News, and written by Mrs. Jones
herself ; and of that deadly cortege : “ The pretty
wedding and the pretty bride, who looked cnarm-
nig thereat ; “ the mother of the bride ”in avoir-
dupois, satin and an aigrette; the wedding pre-
sents that always were “both numerous and
costly ’; the bridegroom’s presents, to the brides-
maids ; the happy couple, who “departed among
showers of rice and confetti.” Doesn’t it make
you yearn to be a perfectly plain bride, whose
couple of wedding presents went into a band-box ?
J here were no bridesmaids, and the bridegroom
didn t give anything away, and the happy couple
departed in a cab amidst a shower of heavy rain.
If the libelled chemist gets rid of the others, what
a blessing!
A wedding that came off last week in Sydney
was that of Miss Nellie Bruton (for some time a
Truth penweman) and the well-known Armand
Jerome. They set off for Paris Exhibition a few
days later.
Lieutenant Dowling, of the Australian Horse,
who lately returned from captivity in the Trans-
vaal, where he was badly wounded, has again
been taken prisoner. At Mudgee, on Monday,
he was married to Miss Grace Cox, daughter of
the late Mr. Alex. Cox, of Oakfield.
Some coming dances are North Sydney Tennis
Clubs hop, Friday, June 22, at local Masonic
Hall; St. Vincent’s ball, date not definitely fixed
yet, pending the meeting next Thursday, June
21st, at St. Vincent’s Hospital, Darlinghurst; and
the University ball, June 28th, sandwiched be-
tween the two V.R. evenings.
Apropos of the latter, I am not going to stake
my immortal soul on the truth of the following
storyette, but here it is, and if it keeps anyone
out of trouble on June 25 or 27, don’t thank me. I
was only told the story. At last Govt. House
"Jp nglish Evenings,” as Mrs. Brough called them,
a delighted guest was folding up her programme,
preparatory to pocketing it. Suddenly a vice-
regal official tapped her on the arm. “ Oh, please,
don’t take away the programme. It is requested
you should nqt do so ! ” She fluttered, reddened,
and dropped it as if it had been the silver spoons.
Spoons one could understand, but programmes!
A mystery unless His Ex. wants all the
mementoes he can muster of the beautiful hours
spent in his red drawing-room.
Mrs. B. R. Wise left us for a trip Home last
week, taking her only son “Tony” to Rugby;
which reminds me that the V. R. educational diffi"
oulty has-been settled by the engagement of Miss
Leas as French governess to —well, there are such
a lot of young people in the V.lt. menage just
now that I had better mention that little Mary
Gilmour is the raison d’etre of a governess at
Government House.
The Governor’s latest-arrived sister dresses her
hair with daring, but extremely’ becoming, seve-
nty wears it parted in the middle and plainly
put away at the sides, with never a lock escaping.
Her face is of the conventional Beauchamp
pattern rounder, less nose—a winning, piquant
face, with a delightful smile that is rapidly’ salving
Potts Point’s wounded feelings.
I hear of a society woman, who, unable otherwise
to escape thePlague-and-wartopics of conversation,
has retired to bed these last few months, drawn
the curtains, lit the gas, and told her maid to let
her know when the world has grown cheerful
again. When the influenza scare and mining
boom were on, some time ago, this lady behaved
m the same sensible manner, shooed the ordinary
newspaper away, and read nothing but the share-
list, as this, she declared, was ttie only current
literature that had anv hilariousness about it.
Significant ! Eleven out of 21) probationers
seceded from Prince Alfred Hospital last year, and
of the eleven four left through weakness, three
through unsuitability, three from dislike of work,
and only one to get married. Spite of effective
puritanical costuming the hospital doesn’t seem
good hunting-ground for the matrimonial aspirant.
Man sick, and man well, are two such different
beings, and doctors—well, all doctors don’t want
women who know too much.
Charles Clarke brings back whiter hair than of
yore, but the same voice, the same humorous
little-boy mouth, the same irreproachable shirt-
front taking shirt-front metaphorically for purple
and fine linen generally—and the same delightful
way of getting tilings over quickly. It would have
been a pity, even though natural, if he had aged
in the least. But no ! He whips up his recita-
tions, drives them at you, bows, smiles, and is
gone. This is your real epicure. The man who
can feed himself as well as you; the man who
always stops before he has given you just enough.
buch a gitten’ upstairs ” was gone through in
the same luce, droll way, that hasn’t much spark
of genius in it, but is charming, always charming.
Ihe only new feature was the Patriotic Oration.
Hus took the house by the wool, and raised it to
the seventh heaven, where sheep are glorified
(opposed to goats, you remember). For my part,
lam not sure whether [ like orations or not; or
should I say whether I understand them ? A
deep study of the classics has left the fancy with
me that there ought to be somebody dead. Not
so. The Centenary Hall was very much alive,
first floor patronised, galleries full, upper
galleries crowded—and the Oration smote them
into cheers, which would have been missed if the
usual sad circumstances of corpse and pile had
been complied with.
Mrs. Howie—otherwise “ Te-Rangi-Pai”—came
on in the Rev. Charles’ intervals. This warbler
is new to us. She is a half-Maori, lam told, but
was clad all over in a long black jetty dress with
long sleeves, and a garden of pink roses on her
left shoulder. In a mezzo-contralto she sang
ballads from the programme, and ballads for
encore. Her voice is ordinary and sweet, her
style simple and devotional, and, for the rest, her
dark face and artless manner captivate. Hetty Hol-
royd joined the concerts during the week, and
goes on to Melbourne with them later.
new music there seems to be great activity.
I had begun to hope there would be no “Battleof
Prague,” no “ Jessie’s Dream ” (descriptive of the
biege of Lucknow), no brilliant fantasia com-
memorating the screams of the wounded and
dying m South Africa. It seemed a great hope.
And it was too great, too vain. The War-music
is upon us, in battalions, many pages strong,
operas, fantasias, marches—they are getting ready
to bombard, furioso. Have you heard about the
Transvaal War Symphony, ” just being published
m London ? The composer is Miss May Louise
White. She brings out her symphony in three
parts, as she goes along ; but the first movement
gets a little behind things, does it not ? It repre-
sents “ An earnest appeal to the British Empire
to pause before she strikes.” Then there is also,
according to the composer, “ The simplest musical
cadence suggesting how easily matters of discus-
sion can be settled.” I should think this was the
sort of music you hear about, that makes men
fight like the devil.
But this pales before a local fantasia, “ The Re-
j Ladysmith,” by Landon Roberts (Allan
and Go.). In a few well-chosen chords “The
British retire from Dundee ” —first andante maes-
toso, then allegro—but non troppo, you will be
glad to hear. I was sorry to see that “The
British surprised by Boers” was expressed by
runs. Couldn’t the composer have managed
otherwise? Everything warlike you ever heard
or read of is included in these 14 pages, from the
Anxiety of the Besieged ” to a clock striking
at sunrise in low bass—rust, I presume. All
the war-tunes are introduced freely, a magnifi-
cent two bob’s ” worth that puts Prague to the
blush. Pleyel and Co. send “The Pretoria
Waltz, by Lionel Alexander, which is spirited
and tuneful. Paling and Co. send Lawrence
Phillips “Grace,” a charming little piece that
never fails to fetch an audience. Also from Pal-
ing, a new and excellent edition of the ancient
enemy, Czerny’s “Hundred and One,” at the
nominal price of one shilling; and Irene Frank-
lin s Tivoli success, the pretty, plaintive “Won’t
you take me back to Dixie?”
I am glad to say that the concert at Parra-
matta in aid of the widow and family of the
late Corpora] Harkus was a distinct' success,
financially and otherwise.
The friends and admirers of Mr. Rivers Allpress
are rallying up a benefit concert for that well-
known fiddler, who has fallen into bad health
lately. If anyone deserves a ben. it is Mr. Allpress,
for he has never had one in his life, though he has
played for charity hundreds of times.
“The Pirates of Penzance” followed “Pina-
fore ”on Saturday at Her Majesty’s. Miss Ada
Winston-Weir made her debut among us as Mabel
of the thin white stockings and large white feet.
The new star, who was too chalkily made up—
following her predecessor soprano in that—sang
with a good clear voice persistently vibrato, and
was well encored. Apropos of the white feet,
how boldly hosed and booted is the comic opera
artist of to-day ! Her sister of “ The Halls ”
vide “Dainty” Irene Franklin—twinkles jewels
of feet, not particularly tiny, but set in—ah—
such stockings, and such shoon. Snappy bronze
shoes and stockings would be a welcome emenda-
tion in Miss Weir’s toilette, and the white track-
ing would gain instead of suffer. Dresses are not
a feature in the “Pirates.” Pretty Cecil Engle-
hart reminded one of a full-blown pink rose, and
dainty little Annie Cubitt was worth admiring ;
but these merely aggravated the chorus crudities
of yellow, red and ginger confections, topped by
hats apparently slept in, or on. But what has
become of the Gilbert and Sullivan traditions ?
Dolly Vane, as Ruth, was no “ remains ” of a fine
woman.” She was quite the prettiest and most
tastefully-dressed woman on the stage, snappily
frocked in black and scarlet. From the late
Little Buttercup’s hideous green bonnet Dorothy
emerged with a conspicuous new polish—grateful,
soothing—applied equally to clothes and make-up.
