Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
The Skating Carnival in the South Yarraßink,
I riday night, June 15th, made a snug sum for the
Children’s Hospital, thanks to the energy of Janet
Lady Clarke, President of the Skating Club. As
a spectacle, however, the affair was marred by dim
lights. The President herself, in tame black
frock and Vandyke collar, seemed a smaH,
scrimped suburban imitation of the scintillating,
rustling Personage we’re accustomed to. Miss
Hodges, the Club secretary, was big and blooming
in trailing blue muslin, opening over white under-
skirt, and, of course, white furs. Secretary Richard
Nevill, in dark tweeds, looked surprisingly well-
nourished. Either the Madden plumpness is
catching, or the Lieut.-Governor’s little cupboard
is less bare than his predecessor’s. One of the
most amusing things was seeing Lord Richard
counting the potatoes in the various buckets, after
the potato race ; one wondered if he had ever seen
the horrid things before, don’t-you-know. Pretty
Miss Madden, in parrot-green, nursed her white
muff and looked listlessly on.
“Local sassiety is stupidly imitative in its dress
tactics. Every pretty girl’s waved hair is combed
up in a rounded basin-shape, and every “she”
affects a flowing, ample, Madden-daughter contour
and carries blonde muff and tippet.
“N.B. —Now that swansdowrfis rampant in the
suburbs, white fox and the smartest will soon part
company.
Skating will soon supersede dancing. After
all, it’s a prosaic pastime. At the Carnival a
pretty young daughter of Toorakia, in pique suit
and sailor-hat, glided round like the Spirit of
Youth and Spring. Then she sat down hard in
the middle of the floor, sounding no more poetical
than a sack of potatoes. Another winsome spin-
ster retired behind sister’s skirts to pull up her
sock. She took her time over the process, fondly
imagining that her friends were shielding her—
when a man o.i the other side audibly sized them
up as ‘spindle-shanks.’ Had her ankles been
visions of deiight, catch her chums letting the
sharpest eye in ti e room steal a glimpse of them !
“In the ladies’ skating competition, the win
wasn t popular. Most people backed a graceful
glider in dainty heliotrope blouse, trim skirt and
Fair hair coilcu under toque of turquoise, violets
and sable. Judge Richard Nevill and Co.
awarded it to a much more utilitarian figure in
tweed and sieh.
“ In the ‘ Professor > Love Story ’ the smartest
frock was Inez Bensusan’s flame-silk with polon-
aise of biscuit lace, emerald-green rosettes, and
toque of black plumes and flame-satin. The last
chapter of the Professor’s wooing, or, rather, its
illustration by shadows on the blind, went against
the grain. So ghoulish of the lover’s devoted
sister and oldest friend to stand outside gloating
over ‘ dear old Tom’s ’ reflected billings and over-
heard cooings, even though the discomfiture of a
husband-]muter palliated their eaves-droppings.
At the start, before Goodwillie and his ‘little
Lucy thing ’ had rehearsed the most effective
modes of shadow-production, their reflections were
at times grotesque. The scholar, flourishing his
hand to stroke his lassie’s frisette, as she stood
gazing upward with her chin pillowed on his top
vest-button, repeatedly had the air of cheekily
putting his thumb to his nose. Talking of noses;
Dot Frederic’s looked yards long whenever she
shyly bent her head and inspected her hands, as if
mentally encircling her third .finger with an
engagement ring at the Professor’s expense.
“At Her Majesty’s ‘Queen Elizabeth’ first
night drew a big dressy crowd, including the
Lieut.-Governor, who brought all his available
daughters, not forgetting the baby, presumably
with a view to improving the latter’s historical
knowledge.
“ Regarded as a junior history lesson, imparted
by a splendidly-handsome rampageous lecturess,
the production*is a success. Queen Bess, I guess,
though hot-tempered, must have been a less-con-
tinuous scold than Nance O’Neil’s edition or she
wouldn’t have worn her crown for 44 years. ‘My
sweet sister Temperance,’ as Edward Tudor called
his canny kinswoman, boasted too many staunch
adherents to have been a tireless termagant.
Humor, methinks, is one of the things wanting in
the showy O’Neil’s version of Bess Tudor, who, to
do her justice, had a tang to her tongue as well as
a clapper.
“ Mrs. George Chimside, for one, came to ‘ Her
Majesty’s ’in black satin cover-all. These black
pelisses, though gloomy, promise to find favor
with the Smartest, who tire of the elephantine,
bathing-dress effect of baggy light-colored coats—
especially now that suburban imitators are ramp-
ant —dressing-gown-like garments of cardinal or
navy cashmere edged with mock fur.
“Overheard in a Melb. dress-circle: ‘ls she a
lady ? ’ ‘ Well, ah—she’s not English.’
“ The attendants at a smart restaurant still keep
a black mark against the memory of Mafeking
night. First of all, the Naicest, trooping in from
the theatres, were hustled and chaffed by side-
walk critics, because of a bevy of airy, fairy brides-
maids. Then, the suppers, ordered in advance,
suffered like that lovers’ dinner in ‘ The Lim's, ’ as
no one could settle down to eat them. The wai-
tress, in despair, moved the tables to the windows
so that the company could fill eyes and mouths
simultaneously, but fresh bother began when a
parcel of gilded Jingoes grabbed one of the at-
tendant nymphs, danced her up and down stairs,
out into the street and back again, till her hair
came down and the rough side of her tongue came
up, and all was anarchy.
“ The khaki-and-scarlet craze is only a craze—
not a fashion. It is the sign of the bounder and
the boundeross. Yet it is made effective in some
instances. This scribe, dining at a cafe, saw a
smart woman wearing a bodice with the front
made like an officer’s mess jacket, only of scarlet
silk, with a row of tiny gold pea-shaped buttons
sewn closely down the centre. It looked pretty
and neat. But military millinery is too obvious
to suit smartly-dressed people who like the
fashions that a sluggish pocket and limping taste
will adopt twelve months hence—not those that
are seized upon immediately by every barmaid.
One beauty of the plague is that hypochon-
driacs refrain from openly boasting its symptoms.
You know the bore who is always first in the field
with up-to-date La Grippe, ‘suppressed typhoid,’
&c. ? The slightest ailment at present may lead
to medical consultation, quarantine, and cremated
furniture. In side-streets mothers scoot their
snipes out of the gutters with the throat, ‘ Gress-
well will get yer ! See how you’ll like it when yer
burnt in tar 1 ’ Dentists thrive, for a puffed
cheek may boycott the owner as a bubonic case.
T’other day in a rural suburb a baby indulged in
the mildest form of scarlatina while constables
guarded its home, and an excitable neighbour
warned passers-by to ‘ cross over before you come
to the Black Death house!’ v
“Bats are now selling at 6d. a heaa in Mel-
bourne and suburbs, and sixpence is now known
as a ‘rat’ in infected areas. The jump in prices
has developed a new industry. The collector now
calls at your front door in one or two suburbs
with a, bag and the question : ‘ Any Bid rats this
mornin’, missus?’
“A Melbourne manufacturing chefnist rejoiced
over the plague-scare in my hearing the other
day. ‘I ve made so much out of the scare that I
can afford to go to Paris Exhibition if it really
takes hold here. Why, there’s a small fortune to
be made out of flea-powder alone these times. It
isn t pearl-powder that all you ladies are carrying
in those dainty little silver boxes? Not a bit of it
—it is insectimort, or something of the sort.
Watch and see it being spread round at church.’
The poverty of the church mouse is nothing to
that of the church flea. The latter only dines
once a week.
“Mrs. Tom Fitzgerald, also known to friends
of Fitzgerald Bros.’ Circus as a pretty ‘ globe-
trotter ” and smart equestrienne, is in Europe at
present inspecting novelties. She left Adelaide
when it was 114 in the shade and got to London
in a snowstorm, but is still alive and much in-
terested in a pious English dog named ‘ Markis. ’
He’s a handsome fox-terrier to look at, but more
to his credit is the fact that before each meal he
folds his paws, bows his head, and presumably
says Grace sotto voce. When he’s helped himself
to anything he fancies, he ‘ offers thanks ’ on
the same lines as before. He took a great fancy
to Mrs. Fitz., and when she was leaving it was
found that he had stolen her purse and hidden it
in his house. She thinks he wanted it as a keep-
sake, but I’m afraid ‘ Markis,’ with all his pro-
fessions of piety, must be a whited sepulchre.
“The next Victorian Treasurer who is on the
look-out for something to tax should ‘ have ago’-
at armorial bearings. They are taxed in England :
why not here ? The use of these symbols of bar-
barism on carriage-panels, letter-paper, envelopes,
as ornaments for gates, for bookplates, and on
finger-rings is distinctly on the increase. Few—
scarcely any—of the hideous griffins are genuine
crests, but they give snobs a chance of setting up
a brag about family connections. Perhaps it was
not of aboriginals that Emerson was speaking
when he said that heraldic devices ‘ belong, with
w % s > powders, and scarlet coats, to an earlier age,
and may be advantageously consigned, with paint
and tattoo, to the dignitaries of Australia and
Polynesia.’

PERSONAL ITEMS.
The mouth of demised Banker F. C,
Smith was framed to say No.
The scribes have a brutal habit of report-
ing verbatim interviews with Mayor Harris, of
Sydney.
Wigram Allen was the only N.S. \Vales
Speaker who lost the Speaker’s chair by a direct
vote of the House.
An English illustrated prints a portrait of
Col. Secretary See, whom it labels “John Lee.”
So much for ambitious John’s Imperial fame.
Mr. T. W. Taylor, the narrowly-defeated
candidate for Canterbury (N.S.W.), was at one
time private secretary to the Earl of Dufferin.
Died in harness at Bowen, Q., editor
Rayner, after spending 35 years in that Sleepy
Hollow. During all that time Rayner never went
five miles from his office.
Jack Higginson, the old-time champion
jockey of Australia, is now a ranger at N.S.W.
National Park. Jack, who is 86, is the father of
27 children, the eldest a chubby-faced youth of 63.
N.S.W. Assembly has had three native
Speakers—Allen, Barton, and Abbott; three
English-born—Cooper, Arnold, and Young ; two
Irish—Murray and M‘Court ; and one Scotch—
Hay.
Gov. Tennyson strictly adheres to his
sensible rule not to deliver speeches at random,
and he frequently stipulates that he shall not be
called upon to talk before consenting to attend
any semi-public function.
Millionaire Tyson’s Q. estate will pan out
about £200,000 to Q. in various duties—so says
Treas. Philp, who complacently adds that it will
help to pay for the Q. Jingo Contingents. Verily
the evil that men do lives after them.
During the London Exhibition of 1886
the papers announced that an Australian
greatly distinguished himself at the Prince of
Wales’ garden-party by appearing in a low-
crowned hat. The perpetrator of that crime was
a big vignerou, who is now some sort of a society
man.
Anderson, M.L.A. for Waterloo, objecting
to the number of questions asked without notice
in N.S.W. Assembly, brought down the House
the other night by naively inquiring “if it would
not be possible to have all the questions asked
without notice placed on the business-paper the
day before '! ”
Knighthood knocks at the door of the big
men (physically) of N.S.W. There are Speaker
Abbott, Ex-Republican Dibbs, Chief Justice
Darley and Mayor Harris who are more or less
giants in stature, while the newly-patented Lyne
is also a mountainous and tree-like person.
Probably this is because the British authorities
once knighted a small man named Ren wick, and
were so sorry that they swore olf small Corusta Iks
for ever after.

Paris Nesbit, Q.C., lecturing in Adelaide,
recently, advocates a State Bureau of Justice,
where lawyers would not be welcomed. “At
present,” says Nesbit, “justice is bought by the
man who is able to fee the best and most able
lawyer.” It is rather singular, in the face of this,
that Nesbit is one of the strongest supporters of
the maintenance of the appeal tp the Privy
Council!
The recent death in Sydney of the aged
Mrs. Honora Davies revives for an instant the
memory of her son, the late John (“Jannery”)
Davies, Parkes’s bottle-holder and untiring under-
ground political engineer. Mrs. Davies lived and
died in the 8.. C. faith; her son was an Orangeman
of high degree.
It was Chief Justice Way who gave
Kingston his first start in the law, as his articled
clerk, over 30 years ago. When Way became C. J.
in ’76, Kingston “started on his own.” They
have always been fast friends; it is generally
understood that Way owes his title to Kingston’s
persistency. But when Kingston comes back
Premier Forrest’s likening the repudiation
of himself by some of his party to the denial of
the Saviour by Peter was fully paralleled by a
certain N.S.W. politician at the stormy close
of his Premiership. “Yes,” he cried, “ye howl

crucify Him ! ’ ”
R. B. Sibley, one of the pioneers of the
Hustler’s line of reef, Bendigo, and 30 years and
more ago one of the goldfield’s heavy plungers,
died lately as the result of accident. “ The
Major ” —he had dropped the title of late years—
came a bad cropper after one of Bendigo’s wildest
booms, and thereafter, much broken, chasea a
crust as local truant-inspector.
An acidulated correction :
Dear Bulletin. —Old Nathan Young, who lately left
Parramatta (N.S.W.) Benevolent Asylum (B. 9/6/’00), did
not “ share in the war between North and South,” but in
the ’46 Mexican war, in which McClellan, Grant, Lee,
and Stonewall Jackson got their war-experience. Young
said he was “ in the war ” (the only war he knew of), and
the daily-paper men jumped to the conclusion that he
meant the Civil War—the only American war they knew
of.— David G.
There is pathos in the complaint of M.
Wiegand, Sydney’s Town Hall ex-organist, that
the alderman who took the lead in dismissing him
owns the butcher’s shop with which lie (Wiegand)
has dealt for meat. And the musician, if en-
couraged, would probably go on to explain how
mucli better his organ performances have been
than the alderman’s daily beef.
New England (N.S.W.) small-debt court
item:
Angel Gabriel Madercine v. A. T. Mitchell.—Claim £l,
for wages. Verdict for defendant.
Which is all very good for defendant now, but
he’ll be at the thick end of the trumpet on resur-
rection-day, and Angel Gabriel at the thin end.
And then the matter will be argued afresh, with a
billion years’ compound interest.
The even-tempered but somewhat slow
Mr. M‘Court, just appointed successor to N.S.W.
Speaker Joseph Abbott, is, or was, proprietor of
a N.S.W. country-paper, but his main business
has lain in politics, lie has long been Assembly
Chairman of Committees, and, though there wore
members, such as Ashton and others, who would
probably have more impressively filled the office,
the House, recognising Mr. M‘Court’s long ser-
vice and excellent intentions, felt.that it would be
ungenerous to pass him over.
“Alba.”: Henry Lucy’s recent letter as
to what would happen if Cronje went to England,
makes writer feel dubious. Shortly
after the Zulu war, an enterprising
manager got hold of six warriors,
said ta have belonged to Cetewayo’s
impi, and shipped them direct to
the ’alls. Immediately upon their
arrival in London a huge crowd
gathered at the landing place armed
with bricks and sticks that numb. It
took about 180 constables and in-
numerable hired pugs to stave off
the welcomers (they were still sore
about Isandlana.) A biick hit Um-
lani on the chest—a quiet-looking
chief with childlike eyes. He smiled
tenderly, picked up the missile and
smelt it, then he handed it to his
impi-men and each of them smelt it
long and sorrowfully. Umlani said
afterwards that he thought it was a
charm to keep off a cold—he had a
bad cold. Now if Cronje went to
London: well, well; public opinion
is generally divided : he’d get
bouquets for a certainty, and probably
a little boiling oil.
He Lord Roberts’s alleged dis-
approval of Kitchener’s conduct in
ordering Kelly Kenny’s attack on
Paardeberg. “Chief of Staff” is
the modem equivalent of the old
“ Second in Command ” —adviser,
“ dry-nurse.” When Wellington
was appointed to the Peninsular com-
mand he gave his dry-nurse to under-
stand that he, W., was “on top,” and
would stay there. Looks almost as
if there was some such notion at the
bottom of Kitchener’s appointment;
but, if so, Roberts is the wrong sort of old bird
to be hustled into a backseat by the “victor of
Omdurman ” —such a victory as it,was !
It turns out that the row kicked up over
Marshall Hall’s frisky verses and his heterodox
utterances was actually followed by a large increase
of pupils (especially female) at Melb. University,
instead of the expected great falling off. Hall is
the hardest-working and most-earnest of the pro-
fessors, but the pietists hunt him like wolves.
They would prefer a good Christian and a bad
musician.
Baden-Bowell, of Mafeking, is brother of
George Baden-Powell, who was Private Secretary
to Gov. Bowen at Melbourne in ’77-8 —Black
Wednesday epoch. Powell was then a budding
statesman, and it is not unlikely he gave Bowen
some advice at that stormy period in Vic. politics.
Bowen was sent to Malta to arrange a new consti-
tution, and he took Baden-Powell with him once
more. Bowen and Powell are now both dead.
At a certain recent good dinner at an
Australian metropolitan club a high and able legal
dignitary presided; and, unbending for the
occasion, became a little elated. A well-known
self-confident operatic artist was present as a
guest, and, as His Honor arose to depart, the
mummer walked up, slapped him hard on the
back, and remarked hilariously: “A nice blanky
old party you are to go and sit on the bench in the
morning, by ! ” That mummer will not
readily forget His Honor’s stare. Truly, famili-
arity breeds cockroaches.
Only one N.S.W. Speaker died during the
term of his office Speaker Arnold, who was
drowned. Murray left the S{leaker’s chair to take
the Presidency of the Council. All the others re-
signed on account of health or money. The first
Speaker, Daniel Cooper, resigned through ill-
health, but still survives. Dr. Nicholson and
Alex. M'Leay were officially styled Speakers, but
they sat in the reformed Leg. Council, liefore the
Assembly was called into being. Both these
pleaded ill-health on resigning, and one of them,
Dr. Nicholson, is still alive.
Ex-N.S.W. Sheriff

Re the statement that Mrs. W. J. Lyne
objects to the “My Lady” shadow of her hus-
band’s knighthood, and even speaks of the N.S. W.
Premier as “ Mr.” Lyne, it is an authenticated fact
that the late Mrs. Farnell, mother of ex-M.L.A.
Frank Farnell, protested against her husband,
.James Squire Farnell, then N.S.W. Premier,

“F.” “rises to disbelieve the statement
that Duncan Gillies, in his Ballarat mining days,
was ‘ rated as A 1 with pick and shovel ’ and ‘ got
top wages—4os. a day.’
The late James Cowie, who had been Mayor of Geelong
in the earty ’so’s, and a member of the first Vic. Leg.
Council in ’56, and who knew the history of every old
identity in his province, once told me (it was at the time
when Duncan's alleged indolence as a Premier was often
joked about) that the clean-shaven tactician had always
shown a distaste for hard work. D.G., said my ancient
friend, was noted among the Ballarat crowd of the roaring
camp days as a ‘lazy digger’ and an inveterate talker,
and when it came to the question of electing a fit and
proper member for Ballarat they sent Gillies into the
Assembly in order that he might get a chance to live by
the work of his tongue. They felt quite certain that the
wordy young man would never acquire riches by the sweat
of his brow.”
President Chaffey Baker, of S.A. Leg.
Council, would give even his K.C.M.G. to be
President of the Federal Senate—although he
would hope to get another one. So just at present
he totters on the brink. To achieve his ambition
he must leave his present rock and his safe con-
stituency of property-voters, and go into a stormy
general election on the one-man-one-vote prin-
ciple. And Baker—about the most unpopular
man in S.A.—would have, on present appear-
ances, quite as much chance to float in that kind
of political sea as an anvil would off Cape Leeu-
win.

The other night an ex-Minister (say,
Johnson) who hopes to regain office went to speak
in the electorate of a political supporter. Many
women were present, and this is part of what he
said :
I atn only sorry that the future Mrs. Johnson is not
here. I can promise you that shortly after we are married
but our marriage will not take place until we kick
the present Government out. I will promise you that as
soon as we get settled I will bring her down and intro-
duce her to you ladies. I promise you ladies that faith-
fully, because we both believe in children. (Laughter.)
I promise later on to introduce to the ladies of this
locality all the little Johnsons. (Laughter.) I really
don’t know what you are laughing at. I suppose you
think there will not be any. If you have surprised me
with this presentation 1 will be surprised if there is not
any ;it won’t be my fault. (Laughter.) 1 think it is as
well that I did not bring the future Mrs. Johnson down
to-night.

Some current news items:- "Crisis in China.—An Emperor in Gaol.—The. cry of
Kwang Hsu, Emperor of China, to the Powers is, ‘ Let me out!’—A Masterful

Some years ago a Chinese maiden, Ah
Qua, was, by order of Govt., taken from an Ade-
laide Chow, who was said to have abducted her.
She married recently a fellow-countryman named
Sin Can, who, out of gratitude, christened the
first baby “Gordon,” in honor of the S.A.
Attorney-General. “ Gordon Sin Can ” is the
Celestial infant’s name, but “Johnny” Gordon
explains that, though he can, he doesn’t.
Bishop Thornton (Ballarat) is a great
admirer of the revised version of the Scriptures.
Recently preaching a farewell sermon at Portland
he said it pained him to see in the Church the old,
garbled version with its 30,000 errors. Arch-
deacon Allnutt, who was within the communion-
rails, was so shocked that he advanced to the
lectern and gazed in mute horror at his ecclesias-
tical superior. The sermon was stopped for a fqw
minutes, and the atmosphere was electrical, but
nothing was said on the subject. The Quarterly
Review stated a year or two ago that the new
version was as dead as the dodo. This is some-
thing like an example of the up-to-date Bible :
Instead of “ And he fell on his neck and wept,”
read “And he advanced towards him and shed
tears.”
“Smiler” Hales, whose London Daily
News war-sketches are so much reprinted this
side, and also appear to be a good deal talked of
on the other, is a restless Bohemian who, bom in
reposeful Adelaide, has already wandered into
many queer places. He was half a carpenter in
S.A. ; got into country journalism in Vic. ; was
first a prospector, then a press-contributor, and
finally a mining- reporter in early Broken Hill;
thence, through Sydney, on to ’Murka as a sport-
ing pressman; back to Australia as prospector,
pressman and publican in Westralia —and finally
to the war as a free-lance. Hales, whose heart
was always to be trusted rather than his head,
lost a first finger in “ a bit of a turn-up” which
arose out of one of his early press articles; and
after that his hand-writing was more awful than
ever—-more awful than almost anything except his
spelling. When in Sydney he had political am-
bitions, but a defeat in his one candidature
damped them.

Re the placing upon Sydney market, this year, of over
100,000 dozen Chinese eggs, it may be interesting to learn
that fresh eggs can be bought in China all the year
round in inexhaustible quantities, at from Id. to ljd. per
dozen. Packing-labor costs in Hong Kong 14s. per
month, and freight to Sydney is 255. per ton. These eggs
(peculiarly dark-colored, owing to having been dipped in
a preservative solution) can be sold in Sydney (allowing a
good profit) at 3d. per doz. ! Biscuit-factories and pastry-
cooks, whose requirements are estimated at 1,000,000 doz.
per annum, are now nearly all using these eggs, and the
local producer is hopelessly handicapped by such compe-
tition. Even the duty of 2d. per dozen on these imported
eggs exacted in the other provinces makes no difference
i i the bulk of the importations from China, and nothing

destroyed. In the past, if the N.S.W. Northern river
farmers happened to have a bad season they could
generally manage to ke. p things going with the aid of
their poultry and egg production ; but, as things are
now, that chance is gone.

“ A.A.A. ” : Your correspondent (8.,
26/5/’00) might have finished that Kararabum
chestnut. The true gospel saith that the tramp
whom “Jacky” Dow set to purposelessly turn
tlie grindstone commenced to turn all he knew,
backwards, for the expected length of time.
“ There you are, Dow, yer cow,” he said when he
had finished; “I’m even with her after all.”
Never waited to get his tucker-bag filled either.
Dear Bulletin,—The “Jacky” Dow yarn in
8., 25/5/’OO recalls that in the early ’4o’s old
Mar ley Maxwell, of Narragool Station, N.S.W.,
was never known to refuse a man work. One
day when a tramp appealed for a job Maxwell
started him off with a bullock team and a note to
a storekeeper in Wellington. Two days it took to
do the journey, and when the order was delivered
to the storekeeper he laughed and said, “ There,
give this man a tin of sardines, and enter it to
Maxwell, of Narragool.” Another time Maxwell
put a man on to swing a gate to and fro at 6d.
per day, and when, later on, another tramp called
and asked for a job, Maxwell sent him to sit on
aforesaid gate until sent for. A few minutes later
the gate-swinger went on strike, and was paid-off.

Harry Stockdale writes .
I lived for a considerable time with “Jacky Dow,”
whose eccentricities have been recently referred to in The
BoMjETIN, and consequently well knew that kind-hearted,
though eccentric, old gentleman. Mr. Dow was the best-
dressed and most courteous squatter I have met in forty
years of bush life ; he always looked as though he had
just popped out of a band-box, so scrupulously neat was
he from head to toe. A favorite practice of his was to set
an applicant for work to grind an axe “ so that the edge
will be round and blunted—not sharp ; and when you
have made a good job of it bring it to me.” But the man,
thinking Dow was playing a practical joke upon him,
would invariably sharpen the axe. Then, when he’d
bring it to Dow,'the latter would feel the edge, remark-
ing ' “ Yes, it’s sharp ; very keen, indeed—but you won’t
suit me mv man ! ” “ How’s that, sir ? ” “ Because I
want a man to do as he’s told, and not to think he knows
my mind better than I know it myself. Good morning ”
and the old gentleman would hobble away. One day,
whilst he and I were driving “up the river,” we met a
“ sundowner,” and Dow, as was his custom, pulled up.
“ Good day, my man ; where are you bound for ? ” “ Oh,
only as far as old Jacky’s, to see if I can get a job.”
“ And if you don’t succeed, what will you do then, my
man?” “Oh, I’ll just ‘streak’ it down to the big hut,
and roll up at supper-time to sample his mutton.” “ And
what then, my man ? ” “ Why, I’ll ‘ hump bluey ’ down
as far as Hay,’and then come up t’other side.” “ Just so,
my man, just so ! Up one side of the river and down the
other— sampling old Jacky’s mutton l Go along ! ”

A Roma district settler fell ill recently
and was taken to hospital. His family was too
young to work on the farm and destitution stared
them in the face as no land was ready for planting.
Neighbours assembled, put in a dozen ploughs or
so, and soon had some 30 acres ready for seed.
Not all the heroes of this continent left for
Mafeking and Krugersdorp.
Dear Bulletin.—The
"gang - gang” (Callocephalon
galeatwm) is a cockatoo, closely
allied to the black cockatoos.
Color, slate-grey, the male hav-
ing bright scarlet head and crest.
The gang-gang is, as far as I
know, a mountain bird, and its
habitat extends as far south as
Tasmania. I have come across it
at Mt. Wilson (N.S.W. Western
Line) and in the mountains of
the South Coast, and it is com-
mon at Mittagong. It feeds on
seeds and, as to its voice, imagine
a combination of countless locusts
and steam-trams with the brakes
on. I once winged a pair, and
the row they raised while I was
securing them has left my hearing
a bit shaky ever since. They
live, well in captivity, but arc
not often seen caged—probably
on account of tneir seraphic
voices. —L.H.

“ Yarrum” : The gang-gang
parrot (8., 19/5/’00), as far as I
know, is rarely, in N.S.W., found

shot a stray one as far back as
Queanbeyan. No bird can shell
a cob of coni as quickly us a
gang-gang. When one is wounded
it sets up a loud cry, and the rust
of the nock continue to circle
around, so that your shooting be-
comes rather murderthan “sport.”
Eating, not bad ; and soup, “just
lovely.

Many years ago one of the
B ’s took charge of a station
on the Bogan, and at once in-
stituted porridge as a staple for
breakfast. Mary accordingly
made for the first breakfast an
ordinary dishful. “ Make just
a little more porridge to-morrow
morning, Mary,” said the boss.
And at the second breakfast,
Mary brought in a fresh platter
after the first was exhausted.
“ I’ll thank you to make a little
more porridge to-morrow,
Mary,” was again the comment.
At the third breakfast, after
the last of a series of bounteous
supplies, it was again “ Make
more porridge to-morrow, girl.
Hash my buttons, do you want
to starve me ! ” On the fourth
morning, Mary, rising to the
occasion, had made a bucket-
ful. “Yes, sir; plenty in the
kitchen,” was her smiling reply
to each of many requests for
“ just one dish more, please,
Mary.” But at last when the
bucket was empty and the
bell tinkled yet again, Mary
fairly staggered to the dining-
room. “ Any—more—por’ge,
M’ry ? ” grunted the boss, with
difficulty. It nearly settled
both of them.

* • • *
Sydney reports a curious dream. A wharf-
laborer named Salvador told his sister at breakfast
on June 11: “I dreamt last night that I fell into
a big hole, and I couldn’t get out of it. I’ve been
feeling strange ever since. I don’t like it.” On
June 12 Salvador was wheeling a truck along a
wharf. “He was in the act of turning round,
with his back to the edge of the wharf, when he
overbalanced and fell into the water. He clung
to the truck, trying to save himself, but the only
result was that the truck went over with him and
he was drowned.” The best explanation is that
Salvador suffered from a functional weakness of
the brain, involving some loss of control of the
motor nerves, which made him liable to fall, both
in waking life and in the mimic life of dreams.
Thus the dream and the drowning were kindred
effects of the same physical cause.

AS THEY MIGHT BE.
At Hughenden (Q.)
station, lately, a boring
party struck a supply of
water, and bored further
for more. As a result,
they struck a sand-drift
and lost the water. Filled
up the hole above the
sand-drift, and got back
the water. At Lad bury
wool-scour, on the Rich-
mond, arrangements are
being made to run 1(5
shearing-machines by a
Pelton wheel, to be
driven by bore-pressure
—to the extent of some
10-horse-power. In some
Western towns, the bore-
water is a splendid irrigant; in others, it ruins
vegetation.
« * « *
In Maoriland it is customary for youthful
rabbit-hunters to enter holes feet-first and pull
bunny out, using feet like a pair of pincers. The
other day a six-year-old youngster was unable to
extricate himself after he got in and was found
dead next morning, irritation to the windpipe and
lungs, from inhalation of sand, added to ex-
posure, having finished him.

Dear Bulletin. —Re H. Stockdale’s par.
(B. 19/5/’00). Great also is the strength of a
blackfellow’s teeth. In robbing bees’-nests, he
takes a bucket between his teeth, cuts nioks with
the tomahawk, puts his big toe in, and up he goes.
When at the nest, either the bucket, nearly full
of honey, or the axe, is held between his teeth. To
get the noney, he puts his arm right into the nest
and hauls it out, and, curiously enough, the bees
never sting him. Coming down, the axe is be-
tween his teeth until about 12 or so feet off the
ground, when he drops it; and then the way he
keeps his eye on it and comes down the tree at
same time is worth seeing. Used to be able to got
a bucket of honey—no bees in it—from King Billy
for fid.— Jimmy a.

A Sydney resident has originated a move-
ment for the acclimatisation in Australia of “one
of the great deer, the eland of South Africa . . .
which runs to 20001 b. when fully grown.” There
are already plenty of red deer in Victoria and
Maoriland, though, of course, this animal cannot
compare as a meat-producer with the oland—
which, by the way, isn’t a “ deer.” It is the
largest of the antelopes (Oreas conna), and has
marked bovine affinities. Thero is (or was) a fine
stuffed speoimen in Sydney Museum.
* * • •
“Yangan”: Re animals drinking salt-
water (B. 12/5/’OO and 26/5/’00). I have never
known wild animals to attempt this, and judging
by the consequences to humans and horses that I
have seen try it, I think thero must bo a mistake
somewhere. Wild cattle, deer, &c., do, however,
lick at the salt left by evaporation of seawater.
Salt, too, is good for most birds, though very
poisonous to parrots.

H. J. M'Cooey
Malformations amongst the mammals of Australia seem
to me almost, if not absolutely, unknown. It is exceed-
ingly doubtful if Australia can produce a two-headed
kangaroo, wombat, or opossum. Two-headed snakes
and double-tailed lizards I have seen and handled ; but
a malformed Australian mammal I have never met.
In this connection mere ipse dixit, unsupported by ex-
amples, is worse than worthless.

THE RELIEF OF MAFEKING.
Come friends and neighbours, let us sing :
Believed at last is Mafeking !
The Boers may rave, the Boers will howl,
While we have men like Baden-Powell.
Six months ago—a raging flood,
The Boers cried loud for British blood,
And Botha, putting forth his cards,
Was stiffened at a thousand yards.
Then Oronje sent in haste his son,
Who said : “ Now, lads, we’ll have some fun ! ”
He said, “ My blade shall never rust,"
But down he went and bit the dust.
Then cruel Cronje tried his best.
He fired from east he fired from west,
He fired from north, he fired from south,
But couldn’t close a brave man’s mouth 1
They fired on woman, children too,
And wounded, sick men. Now, boo-hoo l
They tried their best; but, let us sing,
They couldn’t take brave Mafeking !
Then friends and neighbours let’s rejoice,
And thank God every British voice.
The Boers will rave and foes will howl,
While we have men like Baden-Powell I
Hooray !

“ Protective Anklets,” a certain protective against

from all first-class chemists or from Head Office
of the Proprietary, 14 Castlereagh-street, Sydney.*
Kuppers Elberfeld Lager
often a white was the result. —Canip. [“ Aberrant
pigmentation,” as in the case of the sheep and
of some breeds of dog (not uncommon to have a
black or brown puppy in a litter of pure white
poodle strain), is not defective pigmentation—
which is always in the direction of whiteness, and
has accompanying characters (such as pinkness of
the eye) ihat distinguish it from color-freaks due
probably to “ reversion of type.”

“ Puyyaka ” countereth on the M‘Cooey :
The surprise of Balaam when his long-eared steed
sprung its rhetoric upon him was nothing to my astonish-
ment when I found (8., 9/6/’00) that “The M’Cooey” is
really an individual instead of a humor-created phantom
upon whose elusive shoulders was laid the burden of re-
plying to questions about snakes with a windy tirade
of pseudo-scientific nonsense, but who always promptly
vanished when cornered. However, since he appears sud-
denly to have materialised, it becomes necessary wearily
to traverse his statements. Imprimis : I may state that
in speaking of the death-adder ( acanthophis Antarctica)
as our one viviparous Australian snake, I was referring
only to the venomous class—a fact which should have
been apparent, even to a M’Cooey, from the context. In
a popular article of the kind one can’t stop to explain
to everybody tnat the colubrines(to which the other Aus-
tralian poisonous snakes belong) are not considered as
being oviparous by those who know better. “ M’C.’s ”
dictum about the futility of color as a distinguishing-
mark of species is not new (wide Darwin and others), and
he himself talks glibly of “ red-bellied black snakes,” and
'so forth. “ M’C.’s ” appeal also to “ every country school-
boy ” as an observer and authority, is Maeaulayesque, but
unconvincing, and if “ M’O.” is really by way of believing
himself to be a snake-authority he ought to be con-
sistent and speak either scientifically or popularly. The
mixture is dreadfully difficult to deal with. To “ M’O.’s”
assertion that 12 years ago he discovered and publicly
announced for the first time the fact that in several
species the young seek shelter in the parental throat, I
can only offer a flat contradiction. This was well-known,
even in Gould’s time, as occurring among the Hoploce-
phali, and the late Prof. M’Coy in 1879 (in “ Notes on the
morphology of certain colubrine snakes of Victoria”)
gave a full account of the phenomenon. That’s where I
got my information, and I have never seen or heard of any
publication of “ M’O.’s ” beyond occasional paragraphs.
Mr. M’Oooey should go in for more reading and original
observation, and less yarning to the intelligent school-boy
of the neighbourhood—if he wants to pose as a snake
authority.
• • * *
That was a terrible experience of a Dargo
(Vic.) miner, recently. Hiding home from work,
lie was thrown, dislocating his thigh. It took him
seven hours to drag himself to his hut, a auarter-
of-a-milo on, his elbows being worn to the bone in
the effort. Luckily, his stock of food was high ;
so he lay for nine weeks in agony, dragging him-
self round by the aid of improvised crutches, till
his repeated failures to tum-up for his monthly
supply of provisions induced the constable from
JO miles-distant Dargo to investigate. He arrived
just in time to save the poor miner from death by
starvation.
# # # *
“ Trotter ” tolls of a visit to Surat (Q.): —
Struck Surat after travelling 80 miles of barren, water-
less desert, cursed with flies and prickly-pear. Put up at
a hostelry in which the “room” 1 occupied was only
separated from the others by bagging. The bed was
swarming with strange beasts, while overhead more
animals frolicked on an iron roof, and the largest cock-
roaches I ever saw pervaded floors and “ walls.” On one
side of me a man dilated for two hours on the merits of
his “ dorg ” ; on the other, a drunk used his proboscis
like a fog-liorn. They drink up there like demons, and
the alleged whisky is simply murderous unless you can
stomach molten lead. A shilling is the smallest coin
there ; and if you put down a half-a-crown for a drink they
ask if you have a florin. “We don’t keep no sixpences,
you see ! ” They called it winter when I was there (May
10), yet the glass registered 90' in the shade at noon.
“ God help the summer.” On the banks of the Balonne
a Chinaman keeps an opium-den for the use of the blacks
—and others—in which I counted 10 black and yellow
NANOR O’NEIL.
Photo, by Talma & Co.
people of both sexes in a state of semi-stupefaction.
Don’t know a better place to qualify for a madhouse than
Surat—hard living, bad water, bad liquor, insects, opium
—ugh !
King Oscar, of Sweden, is reported as
saying he “has no sympathy whatever with the
Boers.” No one who, like Oscar, is in the King
line of business, can have any sympathy with such
people.

Extracts from a “letter from the front,”
from a Narandera (N.S.W.) trooper, as published
in a local paper :
Oronje’s men are just placed in a large square sur-
rounded by a strong barbed-wire fence ; then they are
trucked, 1000 at a time, to Capetown. They tried to
escape yesterday while they were being entrained, and
were shot dead immediately. A pity the cores weren't all
brought out and shot! . . . We were jolly glad anyway
to gain shelter of the small kopje, and then we got at
them in real earnest I can tell you, and poured volleys
into them for hours, whilst one battery kept shelling them
as fast as possible, and then another larger battery
sneaked around somehow further forward and dropped
four or five shells into their kopje very quickly and made
the skunks shift their ground further around the hill, and
we then at once mounted and galloped up to another
kopje within 500 yards of them, and, by Jove, they did
pour it into us ; we returned it willingly, you may be
sure, and soon after this up went their white flag, the
carrion, but we didn't stop, but let them have it whilst
they galloped array.
A new London daily paper, which is try-
ing to boom a London international exhibition for
1902, hopes that the Australian will give money
and goods in aid as freely as they have poured
blood and treasure in S’Africa.
When you treat a friend do not offer him
anything counterfeit. Helidon Spa has many
imitators, but none possess the valuable properties
which have won for it the public’s approbation.*

NANCE.
You’re a massive form and fine,
Nance O’Neil,
And we note in its outline
The Eternal Feminine—
Hips and shoulders, oh-o-oh,
divine !
Nance O’Neil.
You look beautiful in black,
Nance O’Neil.
When you sail ahead, or tack,
With your arms outstrained, or
slack,
You’ve a most impressive back,
Nance O’Neil.
You can puti. on lots of pace,
Nance O’Neil,
Though your gait has little grace,
In a ladies’ walking race
We’d support you, “ win and
place,”
Nance O’Neil.
And your voice is loud and clear,
Nance O’Neil ;
As they view you from the rear
All the people, far and near,
Can be guaranteed to hear,
Nance O’Neil.
Simplest speeches you declaim,
Nance O’Neil ;
None can ever call you tame,
For you play the slogging game,
And—you get there just the same,
Nance O’Neil.
You're a show that should attract,
Nance O'Neil,
Houses pretty full, or pack’t:
For, no matter how you act,
You’re a large, important Fact,
Nance O’Neil.

Hospital (who manage the whole business under the
stingy War Office) may grant such pension after 15 years’
service, but life-pensions cannot be granted to men of
shorter service; though temporary pensions may be renewed
indefinitely, if the applications are influentially backed

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H Newman, the well-known jeweller of L7o
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will, hncl it
to their advantage to send their opa' direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 10 years, which u
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.
“ R E.” to The Bulletin :
Re Arnold White’s London Daily Chronicle remarks
on “The canker in the Army ” (recently quoted by >ou,.
The real “ canker ” is the meanness of the British tax-
naver’s representatives in refusing to pay military officers
a?afr wage for a fair work. As to “ the Queen’s uniform
Ling “bad form ” in the officer off duty, the officer, un-
like the private, has to provide his own uniform, aud it is
a saving to mufti off duty. Also out of h,s 6s. 6cL a
dav the infantry lieutenant has to pay '2s. 6d. for his mess
dinner 6 besides certain days’ pay every month for mess
and band subscriptions. Hence the subaltern with-
out private means is actually _^ an
a sergeant. Things mend somewhat as he rises,
but Hie man without private means cannot hold
his own Hence promotions from the ranks are even
less common now than in Peninsula and Crimean times.
•Then 0l quartermasters, paymasters, &c
had "nearly been “ rankers," but now these post.
had nearly always oeen ra,—.,, but now these posts
are tilled bv youngsters from Sandhurst or by older men

It is in other respects that the development is important.
This reef has become a distinct fissu re, the ore has become-
distinctly ore, and the enclosing walls distinctly mullock.

Coolgardie Water Scheme: This great bone of
contention has had a quiet time lately, but it is likely to
be revived now that Federation and Separation have
“ come back ” from an agitatoFs point of view Opinions
as to the result are variable. There is no do >iht about the
enormous cost, but the actual amount is a matter of much
sDeeulation Forrest continues to talk about his great
water scheme with every glass of wine, and its opponents
continue to declare that the water reaching the top of
Mt. Burgess will not be sufficient to mix with their
whisky. The idea of laying the pipes on the ground has
been abandoned ; they are being put 2ft. belowit To
put them above in such a climate was altogether too
ridiculous. The chances are that time will reveal greater
mistakes when it is too late to rectify them, and tor rest
might do a less foolish thing than appoint a commission
consisting of Australia’s chief hydraulic to
ascertain if unanimity amongst them as to the cost and
effect of this mammoth water scheme is possible.
* * •
Amalgamation schemes : This business seems to
have been played for all it is worth at Hannan s. Occasion-
ally a par. is printed that the public at Hannan s-who
have little or no interest in the matter— have received
the proposal to amalgamate the Ivanhoe "
Horseshoe with the greatest satisfaction but it ia the
usual newspaper lie, and the public isn t taking any other
amalgamations such as the Extended Brown Hill with the
Brown Hill itself, the Ivanhoe South Extended with the
Ivanhoe, &c. But those who have lost money by buying
shares at top prices on the strength of these windy amal-
gamations want to know how and what for the rumors
•were created. Well, The Bulletin will do its best.
Kaufman made a big hit with the Lake View Consols, he
repeated it with the Ivanhoe deal, so for a long time it
was fashionable.to follow Kaufman. What was easier
than to buy shares in mines near the big ones—although
off the line—when they were very low,.then to start a
rumor of amalgamation, to sell out on the strength of it
when they were very high—then let the amalgamation
idea “goto ” ? Why, it is easier than lying.
* • • •
Mining at Hannan’s will never be cheap. It
will probablv remain one of the dearest mining places in
the world ; * wages will probably come down, but not
while living of any sort cannot be got for less than 255. a
week, and drinks other than beer remain at a shilling.
Timber is sure to become dearer ; it is cut out for miles
round ; a railway is running to a big forest, but the enor-
mous consumption will soon show itself there. I rest t
average price, 14s. a ton, and inferior wood at that.
Collie coal is nearly three times this su “»* £
ern coal is prohibitive in price. A short railway
from Collie to the main Southern line would save
over 100 miles carriage, and make coal consumption at
Hannan’s cheaper than wood. Abundance of water ob-
tainable at from 4s. to Os per 1000 gals., but condensation
costs many times that amount, and a large proportion
has to be condensed for boilers, &c. One co. has decided,
because of the great cost in this duection to train fresh
water from Ooolgardie, where millions of gallons have
been caught in tanks from recent rains. There are very
few storage dams at Hannan’s ; the Lake monopolises the
catchment area, and the absorption is rapid ; it will be
all gone in a few weeks, and then below the surface will
become salt. There is no virtue in salt-water. More
than half cyanide consumption is taken up by the magne-
sian salts and acids in the water. The P r^ rn * n ?
the lake into a huge storage reservoir, and concreting it
to prevent absorption, “ will not hold water on account
of the intense evaporation, which is said to average over
10ft a year, and which would probably be considerable
even if'the reservoir were covered in. Under all these
distressing circumstances, Tub Bulletin fears that
mining can never become cheap at^Kalgoorlie^
Croydon’s new industry— tin—is going to put
the place into a more prominent position than any other
©’land goldfield. Old tin-miners, disinterested parties,
who have lately visited the scene of the tin discoveries,
stato that the lodes are equal to anything , in
Herberton district. A trial shipment of 3 tons 4cwt,
allegedly bulk ore, sent south for treatment by the pros-
pectors, returned 44'5 per cent, of tin, and gave t
shippers a net profit, after paying heavy expenses, of over
£l2O. Tin lodes have now been found over an area 20
miles long by 6 miles wide. ,
A public trial of a new gold-saving machine,
“Webster’s Gold Elutriator" was lately held at N.S.W.
Government Works at Clyde, it being the invention of
one of the employes. About 50 spectators turned up,
most of them gifted with a profound i&noraice
Elutriator consists of a box pitched very flat, fed by ’ &
hopper under which is a dished well, from which the
crushed material runs over fibre matting, a quicksilver
bath and more matting. The box is covered, allowing
only a narrow space for tailings to run through, and
there are pipes traversing the entire length of box which
sprav water against the line of flow. This creates eddies,
and inventor claims that, owing to those, and the con-
fined space, most of the gold now lost m slimes
will be brought into contact with the matting or silver
and be recovered. Box discharges into well at top of a
similarly-roofed-in percussion table, and both wells are
provided with screw plugs for cleaning up. It is stated
that tailings which had passed twice over copper plates
and vanners at the works were tried in machine and .15
per cent, o? their assay value recovered. It is proposed
to manufacture elutriators for fixing at the Uuls of
ordinary battery tables. From the superficial test giv en,
the co. decided that the patent appeared to do its work
satisfactorily.

cold place,” and related how “ tw r o poor Yankee man
had died of frost right alongside him in their fur sleep-

POLITICAL
POINTS
Tho Jingoes propose to get a majority in
Cape Parliament by “disfranchising the rebels.”
The Bulletin doesn’t believe that the allegedly-
late Bulcock of Queensland is dead at all. It
believes he has gone to S’Africa.
With the idea of converting the thousands
of S.A. Germans—many of whom cannot read
English— to Liberal political views, it has been
suggested that the Advertiser should now and
then publish its leading articles in the Teutonic
language.
The C'institution Bill conferring Re-
sponsible Government on Tasmania, then Van
Diemen’s Land, provided that the Queen might
declare war but should not sign terms of peace
without the consent of Tasmania. That clause
was struck out.
A vivid illustration of the nakedness of
the land as regards Q. Opposition is shown by the
fact that they are going to test the House on tho
military estimates in coming session. This despite
the fact that the country hasn’t got its sore head
yet, aid that seven-eighths of the members
acquiesced in the blood-drunk.
t To save time, S.A. Govt, will this session
give the Council the first chance of reconsidering
the Household Suffrage, Workmen’s Compensa-
tion, and Early Closing Bills—all frequently
passed by the Lower House, only to be rejected by
the Property Chamber, whose springs of action
are suspicion of the people and dogged selfishness.
N.S. W. Opposition-leader Reid has already
got well going with a few censure motions on the
Lyne Govt. It looks quite unlikolv that he will
carry one in this Parliament; but the effect of his
tvlu U > B motions of censure in years during
Dibbs’reign was so enormous outside the House
and the consequences so great at the following
general election, that he will no doubt play the
car ds again. There is now, however, this
difference in his position—formerly he had Sydney
P-T. with him ; now that fickle but powerful
journal is against him and he has only the aid of
that anaemic giant the S.M. Herald.
IMPROVING THE OCCASION.
THE SHEPHERD: “ Oh. my brother! when will
you give up toiling on the Sabbath and come
to church?”
THE MAN WITH A HOE: “ Well, when it pays
me as well as it does you! ”
Mayor Harris told a great truth when he
said that the difficulty in the way of establishing
a Greater Sydney, with one municipal govern-
ment on purely democratic lines (instead of over
40) for the whole metropolis, was the excellence of
the scheme. A Government may keep in office
by promising to do a great and useful thing ; when
it has done the thing it can only keep in office by
promising to do some other great and useful thing.
And if it can’t think of a second great idea, it
doesn’t want to use up the first one too fast. All-
round Local Government was such a great idea
that Parkes lived on being going to do it all his
political life, and left it undone after all.
Premier M‘Lean (Vic.) must be given this
credit. He was a declared opponent of the
Federal Constitution before its acceptance by the
people, but he has, since that acceptance, been
more than faithful to the popular verdict, and has
done his best, not only to help the Bill through,
but to keep procrastinating Forrest and recalcitrant
Philp straight, and to smooth out obstacles gener-
ally.
The resignation of Suttor for Bathurst was
not unconnected with the victory of Pumpkin
Smith for Canterbury. While Smith was a possi-
ble candidate for Bathurst, that proverbially-fickle
constituency was not a safe gift for the Lyne can-
didate ; but with Smith out of the way, the
acceptance by Suttor of the Council Vice-Presi-
dency became possible, and Freetrade Govt.-can-
didate Thompson ought to win easily.
Premier Lyne, for the second time, takes
his Vice-President of the Leg. Council from the
Assembly, not one of his 10 colleagues having a
seat in the higher chamber. And if the Leg.
Councillors resent the fact they don’t say so. Al-
mostall the brainy men intheUpper House are sup-
porters of the present Administration, but some,
at any rate, would not personally identify them-
selves with the pot-pourri of politicians styled the
Lyne Cabinet.
Storey, M.P. (N.S. YV.), in his address at
Randwick, declared that the Anti-Convention-
Bill League had done excellent work, and it was
owing to the League’s action that Queensland was
coming into the Federation and probably also
Westralia. Poor old Anti-Bill then, it appears,
must have been carrying on like the proprietor of
Balaam’s ass when he was retained to curse the
.1 ews and blessed them instead, to the disgust of
his royal client!
The dove-like Mr. M'Court’s personal
popularity pulled him through and made his
nomination to N.S W. Speakership unanimous.
But personal popularity, however great, is a'bad
substratum upon which to build a Speaker’s repu-
tation. In a riotous House like N.S. W. Assembly,
where almost every man is a born interruptor,
and where the “sniping” of a casual interjection
usually brings on a general engagement of vulgar
abuse, it needs a strong man in the chair.
Purves, Q.C., was lately delivered of
another patriotic abortion at a Port Melbourne
Jingo meeting:—
There was no reason, so far as he knew, against men
thinking out and forming opinions upon questions likely
to involve war. In such matters it was the duty of free
citizens to let their opinions be known, but once war was
declared all criticism should cease, and the man who,
whether from Parliament or pulpit, denounced the
national decision was a traitor.
Wherefore the elder Pitt was a traitor, Fox and
Burke traitors, John Bright an unspeakable scoun-
drel, and Morley, Clark, and hosts of others
deserving of the block. And the chameleonic
Purves, Q.C., is a logician !
Acting-Premier Ward proposes to revolu-
tionise the Maoriland railway-system. Among
. other things, workmen’s season-tickets are
to bo issued for a radius of 12 miles from
each chief town for 2s. a week. This is
to reduce the tendency of the poorer
population to congest in cities,
and supposing a workman lives
10 miles out and makes the
journey back and forward
every working day, it
means 120 miles for 2s.—
one-fifth of a penny per
mile. Accommodation
is to be improved and
speed increased. Return
tickets are to be abol-
ished and single fares
cut down nearly one-
half. In Hungary a
still more reckless-look-
ing scheme, instead of
proving a short cut to
ruin, turned an unprofit-
able railway system into
a paying asset, and
Ward’s policy may pos-
sibly, in a few years,
make theM.L. railway
system the best-pay-
ing one in Australasia.
Every day it grows
more apparent that
those in high military
places in Africa have
received “the office”
from Chamberlain and
Co. to crack up the
Australians. The iam
is being laid on thick
and slab. Baden-Powell
was no sooner out of
Mafeking than he too ex-
pressed amazed admiration
for the fighting qualities of
the Queenslanders. The use
of similar phrases in the com-
mendations of the commanders
is very suggestive of a set lesson. Old birds
are not'caught with chaff, but young kangaroos
are very partial to it.
Terry, M.P. for Ryde, is the great joke of
N.S.W. Assembly. He has been persuaded so
often that he is the leader of the ‘ ‘ Independent
Party ” in the House that he has got to firmly
believe it. When Speaker M‘Court took the
chair for the first time after his election, Premier
Lyne for the ins, and Leader of the Opposition
Reid for the outs, formally congratulated the new
Speaker. Labor-leader M ‘Gowen followed suit
for. his crowd, and to the delight of members Mr.
Terry gravely rose in his place and begged to
offer, &c., and hoped, &c., on behalf of the “In-
dependent ” party. The Independent partv con-
sists of one person—Mr. Terry himself, lie is
truly an independent party, being heavily gilded
and well able to afford the gargantuan feasts for
which he at intervals “stands Sam” to the giggling
members who “ pull his leg.”
“ ASSIST THE BLIND.”—AN EPISODE OF
THE REJOICINGS.
The Lyne Ministry runs another freetrader
in its interests—for the Bathurst seat, this time.
The Govt.’s thorough “sinking ” of the fiscal issue
has mightilv displeased the out-and-out Protec-
tionists in the House, and as a vague threat some
20 of them have formed a “party” led by the
veteran Henry Clarke, who still squirms under his
failure to get a portfolio. But their throat is an
empty one, and Lyne is astute enough to know
that the. wider the split in the freetrade ranks, the
firmer his seat on the Treasury benches.
Queensland Parliament meets on 17th
.July—l 7 days after the end of the financial year.
The estimates will probably bo discussed about
the end of the year, when half the money is spent
and the country is pledged to the expenditure of
most of the other half. Then, if Parliament kicks
at any of the items, it will be told that it is too
late to kick. Parliament is supposed to have con-
trol of the purse, but, as things go in Queensland,
it really only has power to say “ditto,” or else
“ We don’t like it, but it can’t be helped now.”
Four big measures are promised in present
session of N.S.W. Parliament—an Old Age Pen-
sions Bill, a Bill for improving and extending
Local Govt., another Bill for the special repair
and improvement of the awful Corporation of
Sydney, and a measure providing for compulsory
arbitration in trade disputes. If these measures
are so drafted as to really and efficiently deal witli
the four great wants they propose to fill, and the
Lyne Govt, can push them through, then it will
be the most democratic Ministry N.S.W. lias
seen up to date.
Bunbury (W.A.) Herald -Bunbury is
Premier Bigjohn’s electorate:
The original (Federation) Dili is to go to the people and
its acceptance or rejection is subject merely to a majority
vote. On this matter the Govt, have played fast and
loose with the people and placed the colony in a peculiar
position. We have been beaten all along the line.
The idea of a question being merely decided by a
majority is evidently regarded as very peculiar in
Bunbury. Bunbury has been so long accustomed
to have everything decided by Brother John
Forrest with the assistance of Brother Alick, and
silently corroborated by little Brother David.
“ Busted to The Bulletin :
Writer had an action against N.S.W. Govt., and won it
on demurrer before Full Court. Crown appealed to Privy
Council, and in about a year or two that tribunal dis-
missed appeal with great disrespect, also costs. After a
few more months, case came on before a jury for assess-
ment of damage, and this scribe was nonsuited on a
technical point which his legal advisers maintained had
been already settled in his favor by the Privy Council.
For want of the £5OO necessary to go back and find out
what that tribunal really did mean, case was dropped.
This is a good example of what a grand thing Privy Coun-
cil appeals are for spoofing the comparatively indigent,
and Australians should fight like one bull-pup to maintain
this right to be spoofed.
Apropos the Indian famine. Soon any-
one who still contends that the introduction of
foreign capital is a boon and a blessing to any
community will be treated as an imbecile. A very
high official authority has recently declared that
the famine in Rajpootana is absolutely due to the
agrarian anarchy called freetrade in land, which
has allowed absentee capitalists to buy up all the
best-watered and most fertile soil for the cultiva-
tion of poppies (required in the manufacture of
opium). The natives of the country have been
left with the badly-watered and barren tracts on
which to grow food. The absentee poppy-farmers,
many of them rich Parsees, who live in luxury at
Bombay, continue to grow wealthy while the mass
of tbepeople are perishing through famine. General
Cotton has recommended drastic reforms in the
way of closer settlement, increased taxation on
poppy-growing, irrigation, and the construction
of light railways through the badly-watered land
which has been left to the people. The project
has been bitterly opposed by the capitalists, and
so far the capitalists have won the day, and the
people perish by thousands. Truly, the absentee
capitalist is the same curse all the world over.
Commonly reported in Melbourne that
the M‘Lean Ministry will go down crash when
Parliament assembles, unless it meets the House
with such a strong programme that it is impossible
to refuse it a longer trial. Some seven or eight of
the deserters from Turner are said to be ready to
desert back again—among them Fink, the ‘‘in-
telligent man ” who was to help Shiels at the
Treasury. Fink has never forgotten that adjec-
tive, “ intelligent.” It was about as aggravating
as if one member assured the House that another
M.L.A. was quite clean, and any suspicion that
he was caked with dirt which would fall off him
in clods if he shook himself might be dismissed at
once.
It isn’t actually a crime to have a vote in
Westralia, but it is at least a suspicious
circumstance and the people in author-
ity do all they can to avert it. One
essential before you get on the W.A.
Parliamentary roll is to get on a muni-
cipal roll. The way to get on amunici-
pal roll is to pay up, before 30th Sept,
in each year, all rates due to 30th June.
So Perth City Council last year struck
upon a brilliant device. It made the
year’s rates payable up to Ist June.
The rates from Ist June to 30th June
were included in next half-year and
didn’t become payable till Ist Jan.,
1900, and yet if they weren’t paid be-
fore 30th September, 1899, the rate-
payer’s vote was forfeit. Unless a
man paid his rates three months be-
fore they were due he was struck off
the municipal roll and consequently off
the electoral roll. No less than 600
Perth ratepayers who didn’t understand
the inwardness of the dodge lost their votes
in consequence—carefully-selected ratepayers,
that is.

Soon after taking “ the leap in the dark ” at the refer-
endum, it was recognised that if there was to be “ Union
under the Crown ” the unrestricted right of appeal to the
Queen in Council would have to be retained. Indeed, the
majority of those who voted for its abolition under the
Draft Constitution now favor —nay, demand—its retention
as desired by the Imperial authorities. So much for the
wisdom of a mob or referendum vote, obtained from an
ignorant people under false pretences.
Is John really running for a C.M.G. ?
F. B. Suttor, who vacates the Bathurst
seat to represent the Lyno Ministry in N.S.W.
Bog. Council, is the “Vicar of Bray” in local
politics. Ho has held office—under Parkes, twice;
Bibbs, twice; Jennings, once; and now under-
takes a sixth term, under Lyne. He has held at
various times tho portfolios of Justice, Publio
Instruction, Post-office, Mines, and Acting Chief
Secretary. This versatile Parliamentary old hand
was first returned for Bathurst in ’75 ; ne has sat
either in the Upper or the Lower House ever
since, and has spent a third of the period as a
Minister.

CHAMBERLAIN’S PIE.
Sing a song of Chamberlain !
(Forget the widow’s cry)
Twenty thousand fighting men
Baked in his pie.
Sing a song of Chamberlain 1
Eighty millions gone,
Getting to Pretoria —
And Europe looking on.
Sing a song of Chamberlain 1
A pocket-full of gold,
The Russian bear is off the chain.
Unguarded is the fold.
Sing a song of Chamberlain 1
Our army on the Rand—
Yes, British ships control the sea
But armies hold the land.
Sing a song of Chamberlain !
Siberian Railway too,
A million Cossacks on the way,
And what will England do 1
Sing a song of Chamberlain !
Who saved the Stock Exchange,
And China’s sub-division left,
For Russia to arrange.
And now the pie is opened,
And birds begin to sing,
Pray, isn’t that a dainty dish
To set before the Queen ?

Meat is at a ruinous price on the W.A.
goldfields —as much as Is. sd. for steak and Is. Id.
for mutton. The heavy stock-tax is in part
responsible there. But even in Adelaide sheep
for slaughter have been sold up to 265. The
drought has cleared off hundreds of thousands of
head; most, or certainly many, stations are
understocked, and now, when it looks as though
the run of bad seasons was over, stock-breeders
are buying in order to “stock up” instead of
selling.
Apropos of the foundering of the Glenelg,
one witness, a marine surveyor, stated that there
was a flaw in the shaft, and he had been keeping
his eye on same ever since January ? 1899.
Later another flaw developed, and he kept his eye on
it also. Orders were given that a new certificate
should not be issued until a new shaft was pro-
vided, and the old certificate was within a few
days of expiring when the venerable craft, with
its damaged shaft, went out on its last voyage. It
was allowed to start on condition the engine
worked at reduced pressure in ofder not to bustle
the injured part too violently. So the Glenelg
went forth in the capacity of a vessel in delicate
health, and saved any further wear and tear of
the witness’s eye by not coming back.

“ Pipe ” : Re plague-precautions: —
“Titus Sale” (8., 2/6/’00) had much the same experience
as mine. My paper, however, was required from me some
distance down the line. In Albury there is no drainage
to speak of. Everything is thrown into yard or paddock,
and the first rain washes it up against the back-door.
When I was there (about 7 weeks ago) the Council was in
a fix over sanitary night-work. The contractor was pre-
pared to carry out his contract; but the Council, under
instructions from plague-scared Sydney, wanted him to
do more than was specified therein. He declined, and
instructed his men to collect the night-pans (he owned
them all) when on next round ; whilst the Council advised
Albury to keep its back-gate shut, and to stand by its pan
like a man and a brother and perish, if need be, in its
defence. And how it all panned out I don’t know.

FALSE
ECONOMY.
Every housekeeper worthy of the
name knows what “false economy ’’ is.
There is no need for us to go into
details.
But why ladies, whose management
of their domestic affairs is so skilful as
to call forth the admiration of their
friends, can be so careless—there is no
other word for it—in the. purchase of
foot-wear is beyond explanation.
Your shoemaker may offer you an
article which he declares to be of the
highest grade, and which may only be
of poor leather, finished in good style ;
in short, an expensive shoe which will
be worn out in “no time.” False
Economy 1
The “ HERZ ” Shoe
Has a reputat on for Quality and
Durability which the makers are
jealous to maintain.

What a difference there is in women? The
actual number of years they have lived seems to
have little to do with their looks. Some women
at 45 look and act younger than others at_ 30.
Their eyes are brighter, their complexions fairer,
and there are not nearly so many wrinkles. Old
Father Time deals gently with a healthy woman,
but when a woman is run down with ailments
peculiar to her sex she ages fast. The wrinkles
come early. The roses in the cheeks vanish, and
an unwholesome pallor spreads over her features.
She becomes nervous ; she loses flesh, and drags
about in a most miserable state of body and mina.
Such a sick woman needs Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. “More than two years ago I began
to suffer from acute indigestion. From the
beginning of feeling out of sorts, this ailment
attacked me very severely, the ailment soon de-
veloped into debility, and I became thin and run
down,” writes Mrs. D. Byrant, Fitzroy, Victoria.
“I bought various medicines, but none of them'
acted up to their stated qualities, and then I went
to several doctors (at various times), thinking they
might understand my complaint and be better
able to treat it; but the medicines they pre-
scribed for me did not have the desired result.
Then I went on living what I might call a life of
misery, thinking that time alone could cure me,
or end me, when 1 was advised to try Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills. This I did, always eager to
get something, in the hope that it might prove the
right thing. And in getting this valuable remedy
it proved the right thing, for it began to cure me
from the first dose, and every day afterwards I
felt better than the day before. People 1 knew
remarked how very well I was looking. My
cheeks filled out, and my eyes lost their naggard
look, and my lost energy began to return, and
when 1 had taken three bottles of the pills I felt
that I was a well as ever I’d been in my life ; and
I am now quite cured.” They positively cure
biliousness, indigestion, dyspepsia, sallow com-
plexion, liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples,
and blotches, a perfect blood purifier, and for
female ailments they stand alone as womans’ best
friend. Hold by chemists and storekeepers, price,
Is. lid per bottle, or six bottles (is. (id. Sole pro-
prietors, The W. H. Comstock Co., Ltd. (Austra-
lasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street, Sydney. They are
packed in amber bottles, and full name blown
thereon.*
