SPORTING NOTIONS.
Weights for V.R.C. Hurdle and Steeple are
pretty much what was expected. Wait-a-Bit, who is
jumping satisfactorily at Ballarat, is asked to carry 12.5
in the hurdles, and last year’s winner, Valdis, has risen
211bs. in the handicapper’s estimation. Mysore, in the
steeple, is also 211bs. higher than when he carried his 9st.
of Domino and won the race. On paper
the backers of the same double event have a chance to
bring it off again, but of the two Mysore, now a 6-vear-
old, looks the more likely to duplicate his win. The
downing of Domino last year was a respectable perform-
ance, and 10.7 is not a big weight for a born jumper with
? , . steeplechase to his credit. Mysore, with good
luck, will go near to doing the Daimio trick for the Mani-
fold stable.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—ln last issue an excited “ Red-
rof declares, with emphasis, “ Carbine did not go off in
ms fourth season,” which sounds about as relevant as the
statement by Mr. F.’s aunt(“ Little Dorritt”) that there
are milestones on the Dover Road. Whoever can have
said that Carbine “went off” for good in his fourth
season? What is “ Redrof ” driving at ? By-the-way, the
great horse had some trouble with his teeth and his feet
in the early part of his fourth season. Abercorn beat him
for the Melb. Stakes, Bravo beat him for the Cup, and on
the last day of V.R.C. Spring Meeting he was un-
placed in the Canterbury Plate-the only unplaced run
he ever made. But at Randwick in ' the following
autumn the horse was himself again, and he won the
Sydney Cup (for the second time) with 9.9 up.—E.F.
• Yic ;, heavy-weight Mo—alleged leader of Yio.
nng Alf. Josephs, is “at ’Onie ” now, too far off to be
interviewed, so he interviews himself for the benefit of
hungering Australians, and proceeds, per medium of
letters intended for publication, to unburden his mighty
mind on turf matters “down under.” (How nauseating
press interviews with “Mr. Josephs "-before and after
Melb. Cups and upon all important matters connected
with turf —did become!) Some of Mr. Josephs'
latest :—The English ring have been getting a bad
time, favorites have been winning with monotonous
regularity, and Mr. Josephs never saw such a succes-
810n ~ victories by well-backed horses. Joe Thompson,
ion Harry Hart (bookie-partners) dropped
£20,000 at fair fielding in a fortnight. Betting opera-
tions enormous there is a betting-race 4 on the course ’
every day in the week equal to Melb. Cup. The prof,
backer here knows more than the bookmakers.” says the
ponderous “ Alf.” Writer can’t let this pass. The prof,
backer only needs to know as much as the books to drop
his end of the game and take up Mo’s. Mr. Josephs says
that good horses are more numerous in England than
Australia ; this is indeed a sensation. He wasn’t “ taken
with the P. of W.’s champion Diamond Jubilee, which is
unfortunate, seeing what doughty deeds the nag has done
since Mr. J. penned his letter. Our racecourses are cen-
turies ahead of those in England, but our riders are
stones ” inferior to Eng. jocks. Mr. J. isn’t in love with
the music-halls, and says you can get as good a show and
more comfort in Sydney for half the money. Biggest
staggerer of all: it costs Mr. Josephs, at his hotel “ a
rinea a minute and 3s. 6d. to look at the waiter.” Alfred
finishes up nicely to the effect that Lord Ilopetoun has
invited him to his estate in Scotland.
* * * *
Owner of Yellow and Black got a knock at
Warwick Farm (Sydney) last week. Ultimate winner
(Gozoetta, at 15 to 1) of the big handicap, when near the
finish, ran over from centre of course to outer rails
carrying Y. and B. and Delusion across, and palpably
marring their chances. Looked a guinea to a gooseberry
? I j 8e j on ,?
lodged the protest with secretary instead of with judge
and ere the error could be rectified the horses were
weighed-m, which, according to rule, invalidated protest
Yellow and B. was well backed ; Gozoetta only carried a
stray pound or so, so there was joy in Mo’s camp.
* * * *
A peculiarity of Sydney race-goers is the idea
that anything coming out of “ Ike ” Earnshaw’s stable is
a hummer.” At Warwick Farm, week before last,
Marama was well backed, but ran nowhere; and on
Saturday, at Moorefield, she started a hot favorite and
was beaten out of a place. Another heavy blow to the
public at Moorefield was the defeat of Centurion in the
Writer On his running at Warwick Farm, punters
thought him the best of good things, and his price was
never more than even money. Hall Caine jumped out of
the machine and never gave anything else a chance.
• * * *
Prominent Sydney turf scribe
who has the walls of his den covered with tickets in
4 Tatt.’s ” sweeps, costing from £1 down to ss. Thought
that unique, but the parlor of an Adelaide pub. has 1640
on its walls, cost of which is averaged at 6s. 9d. each or
a total of £471 10s.
* * * *
“ Amb. G.” : Recently took advantage of race-
day excursion to inspect a N.S.W. South Coast town.
Sauntered to the back of the course and seated myself
amongst the bushes to await next event. Presently it
came. There were four in it, and they came into my line
of vision with two about 50 yards behind the other pair
and all going strong. As the leading jock, got abreast of
me he turned in his saddle and yelled to his neighbour
“Come on, it’s no d d use me tryin’.” In justice to
him I must say he did not try, for his heart was so tender
that he applied neither whip nor spur.
• • • •
Darkness set in just as the last trot was being
got off at South Wairarapa (M.L.) lately, and a steward
was posted at the entrance to the straight. Most of the
neddies galloped until they came to him, and then
sedately settled into a trot. Winner paid a div. of £B4 4s
(10s. tickets). In fact, it was the only ticket on him. In
same race last year, singularly enough, there wasn’t a
ticket registered on the winner. One was presented
afterwards, but wasn’t paid, law proceedings resulting
Div. then was £B5.
* * * * |
A Maoriland jockey, who just missed victory
t’other day, told the judge that he had made a proper
bloomer; that his (the jock’s) horse should have been
placed first. The astonished official got the youngster a
month for his temerity.
• • • •
The average “bookie” is very valorous at skin-
ning the lamb. Several of him went to Gatton (Q.) race-
meeting, but wouldn’t stay when the gloaming arrived.
Said the neighbourhood “ wasn’t too good 1 ”
* » • *
So two Australian horses have been “placed”
for the coveted “Ascot Gold Cup”—Merman first
and the Grafter third. It is quite certain that we
never saw anything like the best of Merman here. He is
now, at seven years old, one of the stoutest horses in Eng-
land, and always requires watching over courses that
need stamina.
* * * *
The big attendance at the Buttan Singh-Pagel
wrestlin-match, at Melbourne, argues a revival of interest
in the game. Pagel is a 22-year-old German giant, not
more than sft. lOin., but a man beside whom the best-
built heavyweights of the day look mere slabs. He is also
as graceful as he is muscular ; and with his lithe, brown
femininely-constructed opponent, made a statue-group of
Hercules and Omphale. Pagel looked as if he could catch
hold of the darkey and tear him to bits, and probably he
could have done so had Buttan given him the chance.
But the Hindoo was too wily, and his cat-like play ended
in his cleverly twisting the German’s shoulders to the
floor, and gaining the first fall. After the rest the supe-
rior tactics of the darkey were even more apparent and
the white man’s burden became heavier every minute ;
eventually Buttan got Pagel’s foot in chancery, and’
levered it down until he threw up the sponge to avoid a
broken ankle.
• * * •
The victory of the comparatively-obscure Hin-
doo hawker over his white opponent opens the question
whether the mighty Caucasian knows anything whatever
about the game Of wrestling as understood by the brown,
man of the Orient. The little Bst. Japs showed points to
the best Melbourne amateurs during the recent
war-ship visits. The conduct of both Buttan and
the hundred or so turbaned barrackers who turned
up to support him belies the general belief in the
placid fatalism of the Oriental. Instead of murmuring
“ Kismet,” Buttan whooped wildly and danced about the
middle of the ring, while the Hindoos in the body of the
hall arose, waving their turbans and howling with glee.
Despite the fact that Pagel was a foreigner and Buttan a
British subject like themselves, the white crowd’s sym-
pathies were all with the big man, and they dispersed
wondering how on earth the dashed nigger did it. Pug
“ Starlight ” was among the interested spectators, and
the mass of people wondered whom he barracked for.
* » • *
Some authorities allege that ’rastler Buttan Singh
was long a champion in India, and won 82 out of 85 contests.
He weighs 12.7 in trim, strips lithe and sinewy as a panther,
with hard bunches of muscle on arms and back. Buttan
looks the catch-as-catch-can wrestler all over. Indian
wrestlers are becoming thick. Gunga Brahm is also in
Melb. and may be matched against Buttan Singh shortly.
At Coolgardie lately Massa Smgh, said to be very clever,
won two falls in I7min. work from Abe Pickup, the erst-
while Newcastle (N.S.W.) miner-wrestler. Ned Pickup
(always better than Abe) will probably take the Sikh on—
and get licked, though not as easily.
* * * *
“Old Timer”: Strong man Pagel isn’t a
rastler at all ; he doesn’t know enough of the game to
keep himself warm. At Sydney St. Pat.’s Day sports, in
’9B, he competed in a wrestling event under the name of
illiams,” and met Harry Dunn, who had been drinking
beer for days previously. “ Williams ” won by sheer
brute strength. Dunn hadn’t condition enough to last
smin., and ultimately gave in, played-out. Writer
wouldn’t inind wagering his all that Pagel (who isn’t 24
yet) can lift a bigger weight than any other strong man
in Australia. Saw him put up 2101 b. with one hand
easily, and tested the weight on accurate scales after-
wards. On same occasion (a private exhibition) Pagel,
while hanging by his calves from a trapeze, lifted a good-
sized pony with his teeth by means of a leather strap
fastened round the animal’? girth. At that time (only
two or three years ago) Pagel was employed in a Sydney
restaurant.
* • * *
Tim Hegarty never showed his cleverness to
better advantage than in his last week’s fight with Cullen,
at Melb. Vic. Hall, and he never had more reason to show
it. He scored off Cullen early in the fight, -but was
steadied by some heavy swings that would have ended the
contest had they landed properly. A dozen times Cullen
seemed to have him in a hole from which there was no
escape, but Hegarty always dodged into the safety of a
clinch until, in 17th round, he was able to send out his
opponent with a downward chop on the chin. Cullen has
little science and relies upon rushes and hard-hitting ;
he is a blacksmith week-days and has been fighting as a
pro. since he was 15. Hegarty’s blows are not forceful,
and his strength consists almost wholly in avoiding
punishment. His offer to take on any man up to 9.7 has
been accepted by Jack M'Gowan.
* • * •
Dusky-hued Starlight and stocky Fred Preston
had another go in Melbourne, Saturday night, before a
big house. They roughed, wrenched and Tastled, giving
Referee Virgoe a hot time. Preston did most execution,
banging the left in often and heavily on the darkey’s weak
spot—ribs. Starlight’s sponge went up in round B—a
bone in his left hand had been broken. “ Star’s ” long
sojourn abroad and consequent acquirement of “don’t-
cherknow ” and other affectations have smoothed off all
the rough necessary for a fighter’s make-up. Given the
lean, gawky black as he was in the days of Foley’s White
Horse gym.—or a few years later—men of Preston’s calibre
would have no right in the ring with him. In Saturday’s
fight the black fouled repeatedly, and should have been
outed by the referee in the first round. Altogether, the
contest was an unedifying spectacle.

“Old Timer”: Downfall of the erame Polish
Hebrew fighter Choynaki and Jim Corbett in successive
months at the lianas respectively of sailor Sharkey and
Jim Jeffries, who recently fought that gruelling fight for
the world’s fistic supremacy, recalls the first meeting
between Ohoynski and Corbett— on a barge near Benicia
(Cal.) 11 years ago. It was a slaughtering struggle of over
27 rounds. In the hurry but one pair of gloves (20z.) had
been brought. Someone produced a pair of driving-
gloves and Corbett allowed his opponent to use these
while he took the others. Corbett won ; both were
knocked about badly.

From Yankee files it would seem that Griffo is
still far from being dead. He went to the Springs at
West Baden, Indiana, pulled himself together in style,
and cut even with Tommy White (one of ’Murka’s
cleverest light-weights) in a six-round go. The mar-
vellous recuperative powers of the brilliant Sydney
Rocks development may pull him through and put him
on top again if he keeps straight, for he’s still a young-
ster.
• » • *
“Phormi ”: I knew intimately Christchurch
tobacconist Simpson (recently mentioned as being Sydney
boxer Cribb’s father), and with extensive circus and gym-
nasium experience I never met anyone with such extra-
ordinary natural strength—of which, however, he never
made any parade, being the kindest and best-natured of
giants, wrapped up in his “ babies,” as he called them,
and quite devoid of the financial acumen of his race. Re-
member a half-drunken Scotchman insultingly asking
him once why he didn’t fire him (the Scotchman) out of
the shop, to which replied Simpson—“ Once, when I was
a very young man, I squeezed a man so hard that for a
long time he appeared to be dead, and it gave me such a
fright that I never laid a hand on anyone since.”
* * • *
No man well up in the game uses the term “on
form ” when arguing out the prospective chances of one
pug. against another. As a rule, fighting is the one
branch of sport wherein form isn’t at all a reliable guide.
If there was anything in form, feather-weight champion
of the world M’Govern would be as good a man as heavy-
weight champion of the world Jeffries : for M‘G. beat
George Dixon, who defeated Erne. Erne accounted for
Lavigne, who scored over Walcott; Walcott downed Ohoyn-
ski, and Ohoynski has a draw with Jeffries to his credit.

Now that the English XI. has abandoned its
Australian tour for next season it is probable that an
Australian team will visit S’Africa to play a series of
matches. The biggest “ gate ” would be in Johannesburg.
Immediately after the death of the late Frank
Grey Smith, President of Melb. O.C. and gen. manager of
the National Bank, Melb. Argus printed that M. 0.0. com-
mittee was about to offer the vacant presidential chair to
Acting-Gov. Madden. This statement proved false, and
the committee were angry, for it was never proposed to
offer the position to His Waxellency, who is not even a
member of the club. Of the two V.P.’s, H. C. A. Harri-
son, erstwhile champion hurdler of the province and ex-
Registrar-General, and J. M’Laughlin, solicitor, the choice
might have gone to the former had he been sufficiently
popular ; and the latter could not in justice be appointed,
as he is junior to Mr. Harrison as a V.P. Perhaps this is
why the committee conferred the honor on Mr. R. Mur-
chison, local manager of the Bank of N.S.W., a club
member whose disposition is perhaps as unobtrusive as
that of his predecessor.

The tiresome cable sends news of Ranji making
a couple of “ double century ” innings against Somerset-
shire, which is merely another notification of the county’s
awful weakness in bowling. Time that the cable dropped
the subject of English batting performane .'S in these
county matches. Australia doesn’t want to be thrilled by
the thought of how a dark gentleman had two big
helpings of “ tripe ” in one match, but they can let us
know when a new English bowler does anything remark-
able on a hard wicket

Cabled news that Australian amateur rep.
(Stan. Rowley) was second (18in. behind winner) in a
100yds. handicap at Glasgow last week, run in lOsec., is
very satisfactory. Rowley is sure to have been on scr.—
probably giving winner a start. This run is close up to
the best ever Rowley showed in public here, though ft is
asserted he has done much better privately. He could
not have been in the country above a week or two, and so
may be expected to improve later, particularly in the
British championships and Olympian games.
% • • •
Hunter River sculler George Towns, who has
been hanging round in hope of luring world’s premier
Jake Gaudaur out of his hole at Rat Portage, seems dead
out of luck. A year ago his ribs were badly smashed
through a collision in practice, and it looked as if he’d
never sit in a boat again, but he survived and became fit.
Now word comes that Towns got a serious chill and was
again laid up iust as a match between him and Shoal-
haven (N.S.W.) flier Wray for the Eng. championship
seemed imminent. Towns has had such a parlous time
one way and another that he’ll probably die S he doesn’t
get home quick. Sullivan, the M.L. sculler, also in
London, pulled through his turn of blood-poisoning and
was coming along well when last mail left
• • • *
Forty-year-old sculler George Bubear, who
was out in Australia years back, has been heard from.
Most people thought him dead. He offers to scull
anyone-bar Jake Gaudaur—on the Thames for a big
stake, provided he gets one length start for each year that
acceptor of challenge is younger than he. Would he have
a hope with our whilom top-notch old ’uns—Bill Beach
(50), Peter Kemp (47), M'Lean (43), or Nielson (41)? Any
man of them could give resurrected George lengths and a
licking.

Arrangements for Sydney 20th-century regatta
—Jan. 26, next year—were completed Monday. Events
to be open to all Australia. Prize-money totals £Bl3
£513 for sailing and £3OO 14s. for rowing-races. If more
money be available—and this is expected—it will be
divided in the proportion of 3to 5. Each approved in-
terprovincial crew will be allowed £lO towards exes.

Cabled that N.S.W. swimmer Fred. Lane went
under to world’s champion-sprinter Derbyshire at Bir-
mingham last week. Race was over 150vds. for Midland
Counties’ supremacy. The Englishman finished a yard to
the good in 1.40. The time indicates that both were in
great buckle.
* • • •
Billiard - match between Australian premier
Fred Weiss (of N.S.W.) and Smith (Vic.), for £l§o aside,
in Melb., created a big stir among cueists. Weiss con-
ceded 2000 in 10,000, and was expected to win handily
but Smith surprised everyone. Game was up-and-down-
one day Weiss would get to the front, and then Smith
would assert himself. Smith was a 10 to 8 on favorite
Weiss shaped excellently right through, but had bad luck'
notably when he put up 358, and seemed like making a
record for the provinces. A miss-cue stopped it. Smith's
improvement surprised Weiss. His average one afternoon
was 44, and on other occasions from 28 to 32. Match
ended Monday night, Smith winning by 115 points. When
play started that day, Smith’s score stood 9001, Weiss’
8726. Before adjourning for dinner Smith had compiled
9501 and Weiss 9284. Smith's best breaks were 91, 72 65
and Weiss’ 113, 92, 62. In the evening Smith shaped well’
making 146 in one break ; Weiss’ best was 117. Score was
Smith 9951 when Weiss gave up the cue, and Smith easily
made the other 49 in an unfinished break.

Auckland (M.L.) Society for Prevention of
Cruelty to Animals had recently a go at the pigeon-
shooters, but Brabant, S.M., held that these competitions
are legal, though, as a question of sentiment, lie person-
ally disapproved of pigeon-slaughter.
* * * *
Gen. Sec. Wynne’s report last week as to the
shakiness of N.S.W. League of Wheelmen was no surprise
to most people—the structure has long been sick. Cause,
internal troubles—old-womanish wrangling, constant
bickering, and petty jealousies. Only combined effort—
apparently impossible under present conditions—can
avert the impending collapse. But just now most cycling
governing bodies are broken up or on the verge of it.
Vic. League is reconstructing, U.S. League is but a
memory, the Internat. Cycling Assn, has a foot in the
grave, and the ALL. institution looks like pegging-out.
It seems, too, that all are cases of suicide.
* • • •
The Vic. bike pusher who lately admitted
in the witness-box that he had ridden “ stiff,” has been
disqualified by the L.V.W. for six months. Open confes-
sion is good for the soul, but bad for the biz.
* * » *
Professional pedestrianism appears likely to have
a new lease of life, and under good auspices. Mr. Wm.
Hunt, of Kelso’s Hotel, Oxford-street, Sydney, advertises
a Botany Handicap to be run on the world-famed tracks at
the Sir Joseph Banks Hotel, Botany, on Aug. 24 next.
The prize-money is £lOO, and a big entry is reasonably
expected. Mr. Hunt is well known in the sport, having
been secretary to Mr. M. J. Moroney, both at Newcastle
and the Carrington grounds, and an old ped. himself. A
strong committee has been formed and a resolution
declared that any man found guilty of running a bye will
be ruthlessly “ passed out,” arid kept out. There’s ample
opening for good, clean sport in this line, and, so far, every-
thing promises to be conducted in a fashion that will
preclude the crook business which killed the game in the
early nineties.
• • * *
The Warnambool to Melbourne road-race (168
miles) is to be held at the latter end of October. The
prizes will be cash—£2s first, £lO second, and £5 third,
presented by the promoters, Messrs. Bennett, Wood and
Roche, Melbourne. A valuable gold medal will be pre-
sented by the Dunlop Tyre Co. to the rider establishing
fastest time. A bonus of £1 will also be given to every
rider who finishes the course in 10 hours, prize-winners
excepted. Single pacing only allowed, motor pacing de-
barred. The race—a handicap one—will start from the
Town Hall, Warrnambool, and finish at the Haymarket,
Melbourne.

What has become of those Pretoria
“forts”? Did they ever exist except in the dis-
ordered fancy of the war correspondents ? Roberts
doesn’t seem to know anything about ’em—hasn’t
even mentioned them in his report. And the
alleged great array of modem guns which defended
Pretoria were mostly absent. The explanation
now given is that there once were some big guns
for the defence of Pretoria, but Joubert had to
strip the batteries and leave the town defenceless
in order to hold his own against Buller.
Rev. Geo. Walters to Bulletin :
In a recent issue, some correspondent has secured the
insertion of a par. concerning myself which is generally
incorrect, and in one particular absolutely false. Refer-
ring to a remark by Miss Spence in her Sunday address at
the 1.0.0. F. Temple, your correspondent says :
“Amidst the storm of applause which this observa-
tion evoked, Miss Spence smiled blandly all round,
while the flabbergasted Walters remonstrated
feebly with ‘ Hush! Hush ! this is a Church! ’
The simple fact is that, when Miss Spence said the war
might have been avoided by arbitration, I was not on the
platform but in the choir-seats, and I did not utter one
word, nor did I by the least sign or movement show either
approval or disapproval. This fact will indicate the value
of the par. as a whole.—Yours, Gf.orgif Walters. Aus-
tralian Church.”
[W ill, of course, not undertake to assert, in face
of Mr. Walters’denial, that he said the words com-
plained of. But somebody did most distinctly
to the hearing of self and of friends consulted
since. There may have been that error as to iden-
tity, but otherwise the account of the incident as
a whole is strictly accurate.— The Writer of the
Par.]
The prevailing tendency to put a bullet
into something at long range accounts for the
publication of a Rifle Register bv Sands and
M‘Dougall, of Melb. The natty little red-covered
booklet appears to have been compiled with the
greatest care to insure accurate information in all
particulars relating to riflo shooting. It deals
principally with the Marfcini-Enfield and Lee-En-
field rifles ‘303 and will certainly be useful to all
amateur enthusiasts who have joined dubs for the
purpose of smiting the bull’s-eye. But is it loyal
to fire at a Bull’s eye these times ? Better to cal
it Boer’s eye.*
The Chamberlain compromise in the
Federal Bill is considerably agitating the minds of
delegates and ministries, although thusfar no one
seems to have made themselves ill worrying over
it. Why should they? There is always plenty to
hand to fume over without any seoking. In fact,
life is so largely made up of worrying about trifles
“of fretting our hour upon the stage,” that, to
meet .the huge demands made upon the vital foroe,
Providence provides (per medium of M'Lean Bros,
and Rigg,) a Primus Chief Stove to help manu-
facture abundant energy by perfeot cookery.
These stoves cost only a trifle to work and bestow
unlimited happiness upon those using them. Send
for catalogue, or call and see them working.
M'Lean Bros, and Rigg, Elizabeth-st., Melbourne;
George-st., Sydney; Murray-st., Perth.*
That settles it ! The popularity of
amateur photography is bound to have a good
innings now the fair sex have engaged it so freely.
It is a sign of good taste ; ladies always have good
taste, and this has led them to Harrington and
Co., Ltd., in King-street, where they found
numerous kinds, grades and sizes of cameras at all
prices from ss.—yes, from ss. to £5O. And, bless
the dears, if their choice wasn’t superb. They
ohose a Folding Pocket Kodak fitted wibh high-
grade lens, elegant in design, neat and compact—
to be sure very compact and simple in extreme.
That settles it 1*
The New Zealand Loan and Mercantile Agency
00., Limited, report, for week ended June 16th, is to the
effect that marsupial skins were in only moderate supply,
witli a spirited market. Prices, although not quotably
higher, showed a firmer tendency. Bright silvery bear-
skins, in strong request; "possums, very keen ; while best
grey kangaroos made up to 42£d., best reds to 60d.; walla-
roos, to 30d.; scrub wallabies, to 19Jd.; swamp wallabies,
26d.; best Monaro grey ’possums, to 14s. 3d. per doz.; best
Monaro reds, 11s. 9d. per doz. ; mountain blacks, 21s. 6d.
per doz. The co.’s large catalogue of horsehair, horns,
etc., topped the market with 22Jd. for horsehair and 14£d.
for washed cowhair. Horns were poor. Bones irregular;
but glue stock went strong up to £26 165., for choice
sinews ; dehaired hide pieces, up to £23 6s. Owing to
London quotations, the sheepskin market was irregular.
Prime pelted, free lots, kicked the beam as highly as last
week, but faulty and indifferent parcels were sniffed at,
and generally fell Jd. Choice full-wools, from 7Jd. to Bd.
per lb. ; half-growths, 6Jd. to 7d. Hides were in big
supply and mostly “ sloppy,” and were only got rid of at
a drop of id. per lb. In London, tallow has slipped down
another 20s. per ton, and, in sympathy therewith, Friday’s
sales showed the same, and sometimes a greater, decline.
The London slump is due to a glut from America, and
there is no prospect of any immediate recovery'.

At Poverty Point.
Little Beaumont Read, aged 72, in still singing pathetic
alto ballads—chiefly in South Australian provinces. Mar-
vellous to say, his voice remains clear as a bell.
Demise —William Marshall, Melb. theatrical printer,
guide, counsellor, and friend of the pro. He made one
think of a benevolent Jew, Boaz, in an old play. Said
Boaz, “ I lozes, and I lozes, and yet I goes on lending.”
So with Marshall’s faith in the Thespian on the Road.
Apropos Sydney civic incident of alderman and organist
—whose music “nearly made the butcher-alderman
vomit.” “ Vy, zat fellow,” said the poor organist, “I do
buy me my meat from him, und he do not know ze
sonata from ze sausage.”
J. C. Williamson has probably made more money than
any man living out of a “ Dutch ” character —to wit
“ John Stofel." By the way, Williamson’s cable address
is “ Stofel.”
Arnold has certainly got hold of a very promising actor
in Frank Denham. The novice, quite a youngster, plays
a Scotch yokel part in “ The Professor’s Love Story,” and,
although his low-comedy resources are limited as yet, the
performance, coming after his bright gentlemanly work
in “ Jones,” shows him to be versatile.
“.Barber” : Have you npticed how Sydney women
follow the hair-dressing of the imported woman mum-
mer? Bernhardt’s well-dressed coppery crop ; Brown-
Potter's wind-blown chevelure ; Trip-to-Chinatown
girls’ door-knocker bang. Nance O’Neil is the latest
mode. Just gather the hair up like an armful of straw,
and fasten it anyhow. A black gin could do it, and the
women who are copying Nance’s style look exactly aq if
they slept in their hair.
The first division of Fitzgerald Bros’, new 00. will reach
Townsville (Q.) at the end of the month. They promise
to be a taking assortment. Three trapezists (including a
pretty woman), a pair of horizontal-bar gymnasts, Cicero,
who walks a wire and turns somersaults on a tight-rope
(like Caicedo of yore), and Rhodesia, the lady-juggler.
The enthusiastic Tom fairly breaks the dictionary in his
attempt to find adjectives of praise for Rhodesia. She is
a beautiful woman, he vows—he says she does all Cinque-
valli’s tricks, and a great deal more, but that must be
Rats ! A second shipment of artists for the circus will
include some Russian dancers, and the well-known French
clown Gillaume, who works nowadays with a wonderful
dog. Another turn will be all dawg—plain Hungarian
dawg. And, as Tub Bulletin has already mentioned,
Adelina Antonia, the Roumanian lady who used to make
giddy descents into a net what time poor Peart, the diver,
was plunging into the fatal tank, will pay another visit
to Australia under the old management. A.A. is the
same shining star or even more so. Lately she
scored a particular success at St. Petersburg, ana the
Czar gave her a gold medal—for bravery in the air, or
for saving her own life, or something. Other people are
to follow these, writes Tom Fitzgerald, but the trouble of
getting them on reasonable terms is great. Unless they
happen to want to come to Australia for the sake of the
restful voyage, they shrug their shoulders and appraise
themselveB at £lOO a week, and higher. “ Salaries are
up.”
A certain pretty Australian actress has a black husband.
F. H. Pollock, now lessee of Adelaide Theatre Royal
hotel, will probably succeed Wybert Reeve in the
management of the theatre in Oct. Pollock, years ago,
was lessee of Adelaide Academy of Music, long since burnt
down, and he was treasurer for Allison when the latter
ran Adelaide Royal over 20 years ago.
See by the Era that Florence Lloyd is married to the
Hops who was dangling after her in Australia. Every-
one here, thought at the time that the fascinating Flo.
only had him “on a string.” Neither of the Hopes
shone on the stage—though all the Sydney girls (who had
heard rumours of their blue blood) declared they “ looked
such perfect gentlemen, and showed Charles R.vley up.”
So they did—the handsome, manly Charles looked thrice
manly between the languid gentlemen. Alas! poor
Ryley ! There never was, or will be again, such a wicked
smile, or such legs !
The Melbourne dailies—three of them—are now left
without one special correspondent in S’Africa. But the
Arous scores by having Donald Macdonald on the war-
path as a lecturer. He has left the paper pro tem., but
in his new capacity he is a more valuable representative
of the Tory organ than he would have been had he stayed
at the front. Under the circumstances, the Aqk and
Herald can tie ulmost excused for saying little or nothing
about the platform-successes of an Australian journalist.
Auguste Wiegand, almost immediately after being fired
out of his billet as Sydney city organist (largely at the
instigation of an alderman who sells meat, and who de-
clared, with the artistic knowledge invariably born of the
squeaks of dying swine, chops and sausages, that
Wiegarul's playing “almost made him vomit”) has re-
ceived from Belgium an order of knighthood in recogni-
tion of tiis great musical abilities. He holds two other
decorations granted on the same grounds, and three gold
medals from the Liege Conservatoire. He has also re-
ceived the offer of two highly-paid posts in the U.S., and
has arranged to give 20 organ recitals there next Sept.—
terms, £540. An order of knighthood may be a foolish
gewgaw, but when it is given in recognition of artistic
abilities that are known over a great part of Europe it
probably counts for at least as much as an order for lib.
of pork chops.
Very noticeable, just now, the alliterativeness of Sydney
show-announcements. “Briton and Boer”; “Woman
and Wine”; “Pirates of Penzance”; “Charles Clark,
Centenary”; and Talented Turns at the Tivoli. Is the
trail of the ghost of A. O. Taylor over them all?
G. L. Goodman had a kind of a birthday last Saturday,
it being the 20th anniversary of the day when he entered
on theatrical management in Sydney. He commenced at
the Royal with Arthur Garner, and has represented “ the
Firm ever since. The Firm has undergone many
changes since June, 1880, but it has been the same old
“ Goody ” all the time.
The litho. of Nance O’Neil, shown in Melb. windows,
looks very much like an imitation of a popular and much-
printed portrait of Miss Enid Wilson, the English beauty.
Nance is represented with her hair heaped comically on
top of her head, her eyes looking bossy and staring, and
her lips pursed,, either in the attitude of kissing, or of
blowing her coffee to cool it. There is also much chest
on show. But probably Nance can look like Miss Enid
Wilson when she tries ; only it must be very trying to
do it.
Clara Butt, the six-foot English contralto, is going to
be married. The height of Clara’s best boy is not men-
tioned in the announcements, but one may almost take it
for granted that he is half-a-foot shorter than the lady.
Hard for a giantess to resist the importunities of all the
little men who come a-wooing. And she is so hemmed-in
by them, as a rule, that a fellow of her own height can’t
get near enough for a few minutes’ conversation. The
Butt’s future husband is Kennerley Rumford, the eminent
(and wealthy) vocalist.
Heard at back of Melb. Royal dress-circle : “ Seen this
new play up at the Princess —‘ Professor Love’s Story,’ by
Han Barry ? ”
Long, lean Lord Londesborough died, the other week,
of general boredom and belated dissipation. He was the
lord who financed Boucicault’s extraordinary “ Bahil and
Bijou ” at Covent Garden Theatre in the early 70’s, and
who subsequently paid the piper for many a pretty
actress. Some of “ Londesborough’s girls” were real
acquisitions to the stage after they had gained experience
at his expense. Although the languid old johnnie was a
deplorable person from a strictly moral point of view, he
had his uses as a benefactor, and it can be said to his
credit that he never was known to quarrel with any angel
whom he had once—haw—loved. They simply parted
with many protestations of mutual esteem. Londes-
borough was too tiah’d to be otherwise than kind.
The new Melb. Opera House —the plans are practically
fixed at last —will have a striking frontage in Bourke-
street and a pleasing scarcity of steps to climb. The
stalls-entrance is to be a little below the ground-floor, say
half-a-dozen stairs down, and the dress-circle will go about
half-a-dozen stairs up, which ought to suit the large
number of people who jib at making wearisome ascents to
Rickards’ show in its present pitch. They reckon that
the Bijou’s narrow marble steps keep fully £3OOO a-year
from coming into the house.
Pianist Albert Friedenthal, who has been engaged to
give recitals at Paris Exhibition, sends word from Berlin
that he often meets little Fritz Mueller, and is much im-
pressed by the possibilities of that one-time Melb. pro-
digy. Also, the estimable Friedenthal, looking back with
a critical eye on his tour in these parts, says : “ I consider
Maoriland more musical than Australia. Take away the
large towns of Australia, like Melbourne, Sydney, Bris-
bane, Ballarat, Sandhurst —I consider also little Perth a
musical place—but what remains? Little or even fair-
sized provincial towns, but populated by kangaroos and
wallabies. Not one of them can come up m musical
intelligence and progress even to such little places in
Maoriland as Wanganui, Nelson, Invercargill, and others.
Even that large town of Adelaide is the most stupid place
imaginable. One hundred thousand kangaroos—no, par-
don me, a few dozen of white ravens amongst them.
What a peculiar zoological garden ! I cljpm it to be the
most unmusical place on earth, and I don’t speak only by
personal experience. I have found out that whenever a
decent musical affair has been a success in Adelaide, it
has been so through particular circumstances, like
patronage of certain people who went peddling with the
tickets.” Friedenthal seems to have picked up a lot of
information whilst travelling around with his grand
piano.
The Martinettis, at Sydney Tivoli, have awakened fresh
interest in the soul of the jaded theatre-goer. Lively
music, clever pantoniime, and delicate feature-play com-
bine vividly to work out to the audience a little dramatic
history. Present writer saw Jeanne May, the great
French pantomimic artiste, in “ L’Enfant Prodigue,” in
Ixmdon, where that piece ran for months to huge houses.
That story of Pierrot’s folly, punishment, and rehabilita-
tion, told in gesture was finely pathetic. Jeanne May
aforesaid has a play written round her pantomimic gifts
called “ Le Pauvre Petit,” in which she plays the part of
a boy rendered dumb by a mad scientific uncle, but who,
through the influence of love, is restored to speech. This
artist, though aided only by dumb show, generally scores
as against those who have the assistance of the spoken
word.
Shakespeare’s “ Pericles," a little-known play, was pro-
duced by John Coleman at the Stratford-on-Avon theatre
during the bard’s birthday commemoration week, and
was warmly received by the worshippers of that most
respectable author.
The revival of Gilbert and Sullivan’s early-day favorites
recalls that probably the best Dick Deadeye that ever
appeared on the Australasian stage was—tell it not in
Gath ! —Tas. ex-Premier, Edward N. 0. Braddon, long
before he dreamt of knighthood or the “ Blot.” ’Twas in
’79, and Braddon had not long taken his seat in Parlia-
ment when he conceived the notion of producing “ Pina-
fore ” for the first time in Vandemonia. There was always
fair musical strength in Hobart, and no difficulty was ex-
perienced in getting more than able amateurs together to
play for charity. Braddon painted all the scenery-
painted it well, too; stage-managed, bossed the orchestra,
re-arranged the scores, cast the parts, and, in fact, ran
the show. His own hornpipe was the feature of the
whole, and the performance was a huge financial success.
But it was understood that Braddon had to pay pretty
heavily afterwards to stop the “ Firm’s ” legal mouth on
the question of performing-rights.
Muzzy has got into Fiction. In Sims’s last budget of
theatrical yarns, George Musgrove attends a young
author’s premifere, slaps him on the back, and says,
“ There’s a gold-mine in it.” Which is, indeed, very like
George.
Dr. Goodby, of “ What Happened to Jones,” was first
cast as Lord Bishop of Ballarat, but the English censor
wouldn’t stand it. So they ohanged him into a theologi-
cal professor at Ballarat.
N.S.W. 001. Secretary See got into a
(Wesleyan) pulpit at Sydney t’other night, and
while expatiating on the glories of his great god.
Jingo, said :
In all the dispatches that had been received, either
from her Most Gracious Majesty the Queen or from the
Field-Marshal or his officers in connection with this war
—whether they related to reverses or successes —the
name of Almighty God had been used. (Continued
applause.) And he thought it was a good thing for any
country that this should be so.
Poor See! Why, the Spanish Inquisition, when
it fried a man, or scored his soles with a red-hot
rake, always “used the name of Almighty God.”
Westralia takes its Federal Referendum
on 31st July. The readiness with which both
W.A. Houses passed the Enabling Bill when
Premier Forrest slewed round and became a very
moderate Federalist was a revelation. When John
did his previous slew it was pleaded on his behalf
that he only did it because he found his party
wouldn’t accept the Convention Bill, but now it
would rather seem that the party was quite
willing to accept the Bill, and that it was Bigjohn
himself who dragged it into the paths of provin-
cialism. Bigjohn’s reputation for political liQnesty
thus suffers its 275th slump.
Deceased journalist Thomas Moser, for
years Parliamentary sketch writer for S.M.
Herald’s bantling, the Echo, began his Australian
career as private secretary of a Governor. Ex-
private secretaries of Governors are more
numerous in Australia than is generally supposed.
Every province has one, if not more, in com-
mercial employment or in the service of the State.
Not long ago N.S.W. had a pair of them, one being
Gov. Duff’s nephew, historiographer to N.S.W.,
and the other Insolvency-Registrar Henry, who
arrived as soc. to Gov. Belmore. Some Governors
have made the billet of private sec. irksome, if not
odious. Gov. Gipps was for a period without a
p.c. though inability to induce a person to accept
it with the indignities attached.
When the N.S.W Public Departments
were regraded in 1895, two officers (colleagues),
each in receipt of some £5OO a-year, were re-
trenched. In course of time, one received a per-
manent billet in the Treasury Department at
£250 a-year, while the other was employed else-
where as a temporary hand at 10s. per day. The
latter seemed much the worse off until last year.
The Pensions Act passed through Parliament last
session granted pensions to certain officers who
were debarred from drawing them under the old
Act, and, as a consequence, eaoh of the retrenched
servants was entitled to £250 a-year retiring-pen-
sion. Unluckily, the first officer forfeited his
claim by accepting further permanent employ-
ment, while the second, being but a temporary
hand, threw up the work and has the pleasure of
drawing £250 in idleness, while his old pal has to
work for the same salary.
Julian Ralph reached the high water-mark
of bad taste, even for him, in his description of
Cronje’s wife as she emerged from her husband’s
laager at the surrender. After holding up to ridi-
cule a woman old enough to be his mother, he
goes on to tell us, on the authority of “an ob-
servant officer,” that she had with her one of Lady
Sarah Wilson’s dresses. As no English officer
with Roberts could have seen Lady Sarah for
more than six months, this officer must indeed
have been a most observant man. The gallant
Julian goes on to remark : “I am an American,
not an Englishman.” It will hurt no manly
Britisher to know that the person capable of
sneering at and deriding as “bedraggled” the
brave woman who had just emerged from the
bloody shambles of the Modder River with all a
good wife’s misery eating at her heart, is not a
compatriot of his.
Very little has been heard of the state of
the Mafeking garrison beyond the announcement
(written by a Victorian a few weeks before the
relief) that the besieged were “ well and hearty ”
and didn’t care how long the siege lasted—which
tends to corroborate the previous news that the
Mafeking affair was rather a burlesque all through.
Between Baden-Po well’s force inside and Plumer’s
outside the enemies were so nearly equal that
Snyrnan was never able to quite cut off the garri-
son’s provision-supply, and even the mails never
wholly stopped. Apparently Baden-Powell could
have made his way out almost any time had he
chosen, but there was one sufficient reason why he
didn’t—there was no better place within easy reach
to go to. Baden-Powell was in much the same
situation as Premier Lyne. Lyne could burst out
of N.S.W. Treasury any day and escape into the
Domain and become a dosser if he wanted to —but
he doesn’t.
Gov. Beauchamp, speaking at Grafton,
complained of Barton’s endeavors to pass the
Commonwealth Bill intact through British Par-
liament and said that the objection to Privy
Council interfc once in constitutional cases was
really “a fight by a few lawyers.” Just here two
considerations rush past with the force of a loco-
motive. The first is that the only means of
gauging representative Australian opinion with
reference to the Privy Council is the referendum
on the Bill; and the people passed the Bill,
limiting P.C. appeals, by large majorities. The
second is that from an Australian point of view
Barton is representative of N.S.W. in London,
and the man who polled more votes than any other
delegate to the Federal Convention; while Gov.
Beauchamp is an Imperial figurehead, a constitu-
tional cipher—in a word, nobody.

Another Tasmanian private land-grant
railway syndicate has apparently gone bung—the
great Midland and West Coast Railway Co.’s con-
cession has expired without it even producing
the small deposit it was to plank-down with
the Treasury as proof of its bona-fides. The great
trouble with the average land-grant railway syndi-
cate is that it always wants to sell its concession
to somebody else for half-a-million or so, but it is
hardly ever willing to put a penny of its own cash
into its own “ cat.” And the country which waits
for land-grab syndicates to build its lines grows
bald and grey and only fit for the Benevolent
Asylum while waiting.
“ J. Jingle ” from Bloemfontein :
Dear Bulletin. —Have just struck yours of March 10th,
first Bulletin I have seen since leaving Sydney with the
Queensland Mounted Infantry ; and we were all surpris d
and shocked to find you had reprinted from a Sydney
daily paper the statement of a certain man that he had
bayoneted a Boer who at the time was begging for mercy.
I feel quite certain that neither this hero nor those others
who, per letter, have knocked out the teeth of dead Boers
as souvenirs, are in reality the blood-thirsty hounds that
they (unaware of the wholesale looting by the press of
private letters) have been making themselves out. And,
at all events, to my certain knowledge, and that of every
man in this camp, the man mentioned in your paragraph
has not, since he landed, been away from Capetown,
where he is a baggage-guard. So that at the time he says
he was slaughtering the mercy-begging Boer atSunnyside
he was not within 600 miles of the place ! I could produce
250 men to swear that not one of the enemy was touched
with the bayonet at Sunnyside. The wholesale publica-
tion of correspondence by the Australian press has unfor-
tunately brought into painful prominence the blatant
braggadacio with which a certain class of Australian
always refers to any incident in which he himself has been
concerned. The “ blowing ”he used to do over cricket,
football, or horse-racing, he now gets off his chest about
the w r ar and hit exploits therein. It would therefore be
a gross mistake to accept the young Australian warrior’s
caricature of himself, and the exploiting of Tommy
Atkins's private’ correspondence by the English press
furnishes a lesson in truth and modesty which the Aus-
tralian may well take to heart.
“J. Jingle’s” remarks may be substantially
true—but where did the Boer whiskers lately
exhibited at Brunswick, Melbourne, in a frame,
come from ?

IN THE SUPREME COURT OFI
NEW ZEALAND, OTAGO AND [
SOUTHLAND DISTRICTS
-No. 148.
UNDER “The Divorce and Matrimonial
Causes Act 1867” and the Amend-
ments thereof and “ The Divorce Act
1898.” ’
VICTORIA, by the Grace of God, of the
United Kingdom of Great Britain and
Ireland, Queen, Defender of the Faith
TO
JANE STEDMAN, of Dunedin in the
Provincial District of Otago in the
Colony of New Zealand, and
BENJAMIN MOLLISON DUTHIE, of
Dunedin, aforesaid accountant.
WHEREAS Samuel Richard Stedman of
Dunedin in the Provincial District of
Otago in the Colony of New Zealand Cycle expert
claiming to have been lawfully married to you the
said Jane Stedman has filed his petition against
you the said Jane Stedman and BenjaminMolli-
son Duthie in our said Court praying for a decree
dissolving the said marriage with you the said
Jane Stedman and ordering you the said Benja-
min Mollison Duthie to pay the costs of this suit
wherein he alleges that you the said Jane Stedman
have been guilty of adultery with you the
said Benjamin Mollison Duthie NOW this is to
command you that on or before the twenty-first
day of July one thousand nine hundred you and
each of you do appear in our said Court then and
there to make answer to the said Petition AND
TAKE FURTHER NOTICE that in default of
your so doing the said Court will proceed to hear
the said charge proved in due course of law and to
pronounce sentence therein your absence notwith-
standing AND TAKE FURTHER NOTICE
that for the purpose aforesaid you are to attend
in person or by your Solicitor at the Registrar’s
Office Dunedin aforesaid and there to enter an
appearance in a book provided for that purpose
without which you will not be allowed to address
the Court either in person or by counsel at any
stage of the proceedings in the cause.
DATED at Dunedin this twenty-eighth
day of May one thousand nine hun-
dred.
(Sgd.) G. A. KING,
(L. S.) Deputy Registrar.

London Daily Mail correspondent
The continued immigration of Japanese laborers is
causing something very like a panic in British Colombia
among white workers and settlers. They are invading
and gradually monopolising all forms of industry. Mongol
immigration into British Colombia now exceeds the white
settlement. It is hoped that friendly overtures from
Ottawa may induce Japan to limit the emigration of her
subjects, who are now still arriving in thousands.
And Premier Wilfrid Laurier refuses to sanction
restrictive legislation, lest it “ embarrass Britain
—cheerfully sacrificing Canada on the altar of
Jingoism.
Nigeria, West Africa, is the land of devil-
worship, “human leopards” and general mystery.
The latest horror is the wholesale killing of un-
popular natives by means of “medicine.” The
“medicine” is placed on a path where the un-
popular one is likely to pass, and, soon after he
or any other native passes it, his legs begin to
swell till the blood-vessels burst. The whole
thing is a mystery to the whites, especially as
white people are not subject to the charm. No
scratch or puncture is visible or complained of by
the victim. Of the phenomenon itself there seems
to be no doubt.

The Case of
MISS RHODA McGUIRE.
( Reported for the “ Star.”)
Miss Rhoda McGuire resides at 123
Bourke-street, Darlinghurst. A reporter
was despatched to see her, and he was
fortunate in finding the lady at home. In
answer to an interrogation as to the
truth of a report that had reached him
from Nyngan :—“ That she, Miss McGuire,
was one of the lucky many who had been
saved from fearful pain and continued
suffering by the timely use of Clements
Tome,” Miss McGuire said: —“That is
quite true. I have unbounded proof of the
value of Clements Tonic, for when I was ill
I tried I do not know how many so-called
remedies, and nothing did any lasting
good but Clements Tonic.”
“ Would you please tell n/e how your
illness began ? ” enquired the reporter.
-“ I have never been very strong,”
auswered Miss McGuire, “and four years
ago I received a most serious nervous shock,
so serious that it appeared to upset my
entire nervous system and derange the
normal functions of my body. I became so
agitated and shaken with unknown fears
that I was frightened to leave the house
even in the day time. A feeling of
mysterious trouble continually oppressed
me. A sudden noise would make me start
with trembling fear and nearly drive me
mad. t : Solitude was unbearable ; I would
not have stopped alone even in my own
Lome on any consideration; I dare not.
All the brightness in my life seemed
darkened, my mind passing unceasingly
from one dismal thought to another; and,
finally, I reached that point of despair that
life did not seem to me worth living, and I
truly believe that had it not been for my
religious training and my fear of the world
to come, I should have committed suicide.
Frequently I was a victim to fit* of hysteria ;
at other times I would sit crying over my
work for half the day together. Joined
with this mental collapse were ago ising
physical pains. My head was a delirium
and full of screeching noises, and when I
went to bed or tried to rest it would be
awful. I had sharp, frequent neuralgic
pains darting about my face. They would
commenceon the right cheek bone, and from
there spread upwards and downwards to
the forehead and the neck. My nerves
would throb so and I became so terribly
agitated that at times I could not put a foot
to the ground. 1 noticed that the faceache
increased in intensity at night time and
during the hot iveather, and these attacks
would endure from three days to as long a
time as six weeks. '1 hen I had intermittent,
stabbing pains in the back, under the left
shoulder; and after my meals I suffered
from distressing indigestion, a feeling of
weight, oppression, and tightness across the
chest, and often the food would rise in my
throat or be brought up again. This want
of nourishment further helped to weaken
my debilitated system. I was a burden to
myself, fit for nothing. In the latter days
of my sickness my heart began to trouble
me by loud palpitation. ”
“Surely, Miss McGuire,” enquired the
reporter, “ you did not let yourself get so
bad by neglect ? ”
“ No, 1 tried all sorts of pills and prepara-
tions, and all without benefit. I had lost all
hope, and was sure I should never get better,
when a friend of mine strongly advised me
to take Clements because she had
herself seen its wonderful curative power in
her own family. So strongly did she
persuade me that I agreed to try it. The
chemist was very reluctant to give e what
I asked for, enquiring my symptoms, and
then assuring me that a nerve toni s of his
own compounding would be far b. tter and
more suited to my case; but I remembered
my friend’s assurance, and would have
nothing else but Clements Tonic -[Wise
girl ! he only wanted to-sell you something
on which he got more profit—therefore
cheaper—therefore inferior. I'ln. ] —and it
was well for me that I did. For even that
first bottle did ms a world of good. It
seemed to act like a concentrated lood, and
to nourish my debilitated System. At the
same time it gave strength to my disordered
nerves, and built and braced me up ail over.
My fits of hysteria were banished, the fear-
ful depression and melancholy mind passed
away. I was no ionger afraid to go out or
to be left alone. The neuralgic p tins in the
face and the screeching noises in the head
troubled me no more. My digestion
steadily improved, my meals remained in
my stomach, ami were not followed by the
heavy, oppressive feeling in the chest. The
pains under the left shoulder ceased to
trouble me, aud in all respects my general
health took a wondrous turn for the Letter-;:
I was in every sense a new woman. Aud I
am quite sure that this revolution in my
deplorable plight was all owing to Clements*
Tonic. I never weary of telling every one
so and trying to persuade others who are
sick to try it. You are at liberty to publish,
what I say in any way y*>u like if you th wk
it will be of use ; but it seems to mo so-
many persons have tried and proved tlio
undoubted value of Clements Tonic that,
what I have said must be merely an e'd'o of
an oft repeated tale.”

assuming very serious proportions in London, the TIMES hud-the following in its
columns. VVe quote this, as it is written by an authority whose words carry weight,
and who gets to the real secret of the prevention of influenza—
“ Influenza has so far baffled research ; we know, however, that it is hiphhj con-
tagious, its onset is sudden, and that the best thino we can do to escape it is to avoid
whatever map tend to lower the tone of the system.” .
This is the root of the matter. Influenza attacks thoso who, by lack of vigour
and tone in the system, have loft a loophole for its entrance. , The main thing, then,
is to keep in good health while this scourge is so prevalent. This can only bo done
by keeping up the general lone of the system, and so enable the various organs to
carry out their respective duties, the tendency to catch colds and influenza being
thereby greatly minimised.
The body is extremely susceptible to chills, and the slightest draught causes a
violent attack of coughing or sneezing. Many so-called cures for influenza fail to
got at the root of the trouble, and only aflford temporary relief. A medicine that

The Sanitary Club.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
There was something in a paper,
And it gave an awful rub —
T ;ough they say the scribe who wrote it
Cannot breathe outside a pub.
To the Chairman and the Members
Of the Sanitary Club.
I was present at a meeting
Of the Club ; the House was t :in ;
I was present at a meeting
In the Dog and Kettle Inn ;
And the Orators were sober —
Though they sobared-up on Gin.
0 the Chairman spoke with pission;
He remarked that it was ti ne
That the fly-blown City Council
From its perch should downward climb ;
For the business of the City
Wasn’t quite a Pantomime.
Then the Poet rose, and sadly
Said that, time and time again,
Vain attempts to mend the Co moil
Had been made by many man ;
But he meant to write a poem
Which would end it there and then.
Then the Artist said full grimly,
As he shook his flowing mane,
That if other means for ending
Of the Counoil proved in vain—
He'd cartoon it in a manner
That would put it out of pain.
Then uprose a stout Musician,
And ho spake right bitterly ;
And he said that when the Council
Was a oorpso, as it should be,
Ho would play the Dead March gladly
And would never ask a fee.
Now this same was the beginning
Of the movement that will rub
Off the slate the City Council—
And it started in a pub.
With the chairman and the membors
Of the Sanitary Club.
CREEVE ROE.
Baffled Diplomacy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Mrs. Maloney, licensee of the Bush Rat, was not
in an amiable mood, for she had heard that Greasy
Bill had “cutout” that morning while the dew
was yet on the grass, leaving his account in debt
over £5.
Mick O’Brien, Bill’s recent mate and brother-in-
cups, sat disconsolate on the green form outside
the pub. He was very thirsty, and his account
was in debt, too. Suddenly lie plucked up reso-
lution and went inside.
“ Good momin’, Mrs. Maloney!”
“What, here again !”
“You’re lookin’ well this mornin’, Mrs.
Maloney. Indade, you’re lookin’ tip-top. But
it’s not here you shud be, Mrs. Maloney! Shure,
you shud be lcapin’ a first-class hotel in Mil-
Durrun, wid lookin’-glasses all round, ,an’ bookays,
an’ a grand hall, an’ an office, an’ a row of cabs at
the dure. Shure, it’s not here you shud be, Mrs.
Maloney! Mine's a pint.”
“Indade ye’ll have no pint, Mick O’Brien.”
“ Yistirday, Mrs. Maloney, I was thinkin’ what
a foine daater ye’ve got. Shure, it’s not here she
shud be, Mrs. Maloney, such a foine gurrul !
Shure, she shud be in Milburrun, playin’ the
pianny in a big room wid grand pictures, and
goin’ out drivin’ wid the swells to Flimin'tin, and
callin’ at Guv’mint House, and goin’ at noight to
the opera in a cab wid a foine cloak over her head.
Shure, that’s where she shud be, Mrs. Maloney.
. . . I’ll take a lon 7 beer , please, Mrs. Maloney.”
“ Indade you’ll have no long beer.”
“ I was talkin’ to your son this morning, Mrs.
Maloney. Shure, he’s a fine, intiligint lad, Mrs.
M iloney. Shure, it’s not here he shud be, Mrs.
Maloney, kickin’ out goats an’ swearin’ at horses.
Shure he shud be at the University, learnin’
biology and algeebra, and carryin’ off dozens of
proizes and midals. He’s a smart lad, that ; . . .
I’ll take a maydium beer, Mrs. Maloney, if you
please.”
“I know you’d loike to, but not if I know it.”
“Mrs. Maloney, that’s a foine dog you’ve got !
What a splindid head ! Now, phwat’s such a dog
doin’ here ? Shure, it’s not here he shud be, Mrs.
Maloney. Such a dog as that shud be away down
Sout’ takin’ proizes in all the shows, where there’s
nothin’ to touch him. That dog’s worth a pot of
money. Shure, it’s not here lie shud be, Mrs.
Maloney. ... A small beer, if you please, Mrs.
Maloney.”
“ Divil a drap you’ll git here, to-day.”
“ Shure, as I was sayiu’ before, Mrs. Maloney,
it’s not here you shud oe ! You and yure hussy
of a daughter shud do will in Sydney, wid strict
attintion to business. Ye shudn’t resoide too far
away from Woolloomooloo,' and yure bullit-
headid gomeril av a son wud make a first-class
chucker-out at the low pub. ye’d keep where the
floor people’d be hocussed —git out o’ me road,
ye blashted mongril! ” [Kicks the dog, and
exit.]
A.B.E.
There is a personal note in the appeal of
R.C. Archbishop Murphy, of Tasmania, for aid
for the famine-stricken myriads of India. He
spent 25 years of his early manhood in Hyderabad,
and witnessed previous famines in various districts
of India.
Quarterly Review, vol. 100, has a capital
article on rats. A comrade has been seen to lead
a blind rat by a straw. A rat will lie on his hack
and cuddle an egg while his mate drags him off by
the tail. Two rats were seen getting an egg up-
stairs ; one pushed it up with his hind legs and
the other lifted it. However replete a brown rat
may be, he will eat off the delicate ears of the
black rat. Ivlajendie put 12 rats in a cage, for
some experiments. Three of the rats ate the other
nine. Rats and cats will fraternise, if reared to-
gether. Rats get drunk. This is from their
being very thirsty animals, and their most
ingenious depredations are to obtain water, or
failing that, some other liquid. A rat will make a
good fight against a ferret, but the rat can only
make springs and bites ; the ferret just catches on
once—for good—and sucks the blood.
Angela, the Good.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Two women sat on the verandah, in the star-
gemmed twilight, telling eaoh other their love-
affairs.
One was red-lipped, sensuous, crowned with
black, burnished locks; the other slim in figure,
with a pure white oval face; the white that looks
like the beauty of holiness—and isn’t. The second
was speaking.
“You do not look good ; that is one advantage
you have. I do. It has been my drawback all my
life. Wherever I go, men want to reverence me and
put me on a pedestal. I used to like it, as a girl,
till I found that it never meant Love. Then I
struck. Because whatever good qualities lie
behind these smooth, respectable features of mine
arc purely the pagan virtues, such as are commonly
associated with burning black orbs and eyebrows
that meet. Do you know that whenever I see my-
self in.my mind’s eye, it is with such features ? The
real face I see in the mirror always gives me a
shock, as if I had somehow slipped into the wrong
fleshly envelope. Fancy being tied down to such a
Sabbath-school countenance when one has such a
burning contempt for the meek, womanly virtues
whicli it typifies !
“Even my name was against me. My con-
founded parents christened me Angela.
“ There was only one man who saw through that
mask of mine. He was married—unhappily. He
wrote me a letter full of refreshing devilry, request-
ing my society along the Primrose path. I
respected that man. I might have loved him as
well, but you see I had already met Jim. Jim
loved me too, though he respected me. Dear,
stupid Jim ! He, too, was appropriated when he
met me—Sydney men have got a habit of being
already appropriated. But his case was not so
hopeless. He was merely engaged. She was one
of those silly, hysterical girls who threaten to
commit suicide if their ‘ boy’ shows symptoms of
deserting. In his normal condition, Jim would
have deserted and chanced the suicide; but he
had come under the spell of my sweet Christian
womanliness, and so elected to act the usual fool
by making two people unhappy instead of only
one.
“It was on a verandah like this, but earlier in
the evening, that he told mo of his love, with
many unnecessary apologies for distressing my
pure womanly soul. But I had guessed of his
affection before, and had made my plans accor-
dingly.
“ ‘ I know you will never want to see me again,’
he finished brokenly. ‘ What can you think of the
man who speaks like this when he is pledged to
another woman ? Some girls might treat it
lightly; might oven try to take advantage of my
weakness; but you are too good and pure for
that. Tow would never try it, though I know you
love me.’
“ ‘ Love you ?’ I murmured faintly.
“I understood his nature; had understood it
ever since I first saw his eye brighten as it met
mine. He loved me, true ; but rather less from
fancying that I loved him more. He was sure
that I should never love anyone else, and a man is
always reconciled to losing his lover if he knows
no other man will get her.
“That was the way with Jim. Angry ! You
can’t understand the fierce anger in my heart as
he went on, praising me with the praise that
sickens a woman when she is only wanting to be
caught and kissed and told that no force in
Heaven or Hell sh ill ever take her away again.
From words he let fall I knew that lie was pictur-
ing- me as the future good angel of his life, the
Agnes who was to protect the silly little Dora, the
calm, sweet, sorrowful personality that was to
cherish a secret love for his children and gaze
yearningly into the dear little faces so like that
of her lost love. I loved Jim, but it hurt me
less to give him up than for him to calmly assume
that I would do su.
“As he talked, my resolution strengthened. If
I lost him it should not be through any fault of
mine. When he had finished I took the other
man’s letter from my pocket and gave it to him.
“ He took it, and I amused myself-watching the
burning flush that rose to his cheek as he skimmed
it through.
“ ‘The d d scoundrel! —to write to you like
this ! ’
“ ‘ He loves me.’
“‘ I love you, too; but I respect you ! ’
“ * Respect is only another name for indiffer-
ence. I have had enough of men’s respect. A
man has come who is not afraid to love me, and,
by heaven ! he shall have me, if you will not.’
“ ‘ Angela ! ' he gasped.
“ ‘ Yes, he shall have me, if you will not. The
day you marry that girl I shall elope with my
lover. When you step into the train with your
lawful partner, you will see me stealing into the
same train with my unlawful one. Your respect-
able contract shall be the signal for my shameful
one.’
“I had moved him. I saw his hand clench on
his chair.
“ ‘ But, Netta,’ he said. Netta was the name of
his fiancee. ‘ Netta —she loves me—you know she
has threatened to commit suicide, if .’
“‘Jim,’ I said, ‘you are a good man. Which
is worse, moral suicide or physical ? You don’t
answer. She may commit physical suicide if you
marry me ; but, on my honor—which, in that case,
I won’t be able to swear by much longer—l shall
commit moral suicide if you don’t. Here is a
favorable answer I have written to my other
admirer. If you tear it up, then I shall know that
you accept my terms.’
“H ) took it. For a few minutes I waited in
suspense. • Then a few scraps of paper fluttering
over the verandah-rails told me that my cause was
won. And that’s how I got Jim.’ ”
She rose, smoothing her colorless hair, while her
friend glanced at her pure, white face with new
interest.
“ But, Netta,” she said, suddenly; “did she
die?”
“ Yes; but it was three years later, of influenza.
She didn’t put her threat into force.”
“ But you wouldn’t have put yours ? ”
The woman with a Sabbath-school countenance
lit a cigarette !
“ By all the saints in heaven, I would ! ” she said,
emphatically.
The other woman gazed at her admiringly.

The Relapse.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
When the masterful revivalist had turned
From our little river town, the pious zeal
Of the converts with a feebler ardour burned,
And repenters grew repentant. You could feel
In the air a strange impatience and unrest,.
And there lurked a furtive gleam in every eye.
Then a brother of the best,
Rolling drunk and sin-possessed,
Started raging loose, and singing wild and high:
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
All the echoes in the land awoke to swell the sin-
ful war.
Converts peacefully a-bsd
Heard with little thrills of dread,
And the shocking chorus stirred among the long
ings that were dead—
We’ll never get drunk—we’ll never get drunk—
We’ll never get drunk any more 1
Soon the poor, backsliding signers mean and shy
Called a shameful truce with sins—a special few—
And the bbttles went to Cody’s on the sly,
And the wrongs that Were forgiven ached anew.
Packet’s feud with Skews, though buried - deep
ahd tight,
Rose again. The brothers niet and battled long j
Then the winner of the fight
Held a shivoo in the night,
■ Which was devilment and madness set to song.
boys, we’ll never get drunk any more!
SHo-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
Satan rattled at the window, set his shoulder to
the door,
And below there in his hell,
Where eternal drunkards "dwell,
Brawled his leaping, raving legion. Brother
Prater heard them yell:
“ We’ll never got drunk —we’ll never get drunk—
we’ll never get drunk any more ! ”
Now the women gossiped scandal at the gate,
In the barn the dance witli pagan ardour flowed,
And you mot the silent lovers drifting late
’Mid the wattles on the old Meringo road.
So the slidings quickened daily to a run,
Till the slump was due on pay-day afternoon,
When the Burning Brand they won
From the pit’s red mouth, for fun
Took a stick and boat his missus to the tune:
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
How the black blood leapt to hear it! how the
mighty chorus tore,
Strewing joy along the sky,
Making care and sorrow fly,
When the -push took up the tune, and sent it
streaming wide and high :
We’ll never get drunk—-we’ll never get drunk —
we’ll never get drunk any more !
From the chapel on the hill the broken bell,
Like the troubled voice of conscience, used to
steal
Up the gully in the night, and seem to swell
To a passionate and clamorous appeal.
Then the barmaid laughed, and Billy poked the
fire,
Then the liquor framed above the pewter brim,
And what more could heart desire,
When the landlord led the choir,
And the fellows stormed the heights of song with
him?
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
Ho-o-o, boys, we’ll never get drunk any more !
Came the doubters creeping shyly to the blazing
bar-room door.
Now the bell was never heard,
Nor the thunder of the Word,
But the dear familiar sin in every weakling bosom
stirred:
We’ll never get drunk—we’ll never get drunk—
we’ll never get drunk any more !
Yic.
EDWARD DYSON.
How It Was.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“I notice,” said old Robinson, “that you're
about the only traveller as comes round these
parts, as don’t get askin’ me how it is I never get
married. I can’t get talkin’ with some of the
blackguards for three minutes, without 'em
wantin’ to know. All the same, they don’t know
from me.”
Then he straightened himself up, and looked
like telling.
“ Yer see,” he went on, “at forty-eight—that’s
four year ago—l proposed to a certain piece—a
beauty she were, but I can’t describe her, as I’m
not good at that sorter thing. Well, she fancied
another man—haristocratic kinder bloke: white
shirt an’ studs, and all that sorter thing. They
got married, and carried on top-ropes for a bit.
Then he lost his billet and took to drink, and they
got down until they were low as get out.”
“It was here I chipped-in. I offered ‘em a
home on rnv place ; ‘er to cook, and ‘im to look
after the ‘orses—a ‘orsey man, he was.
“ After a bit, though, 1 jugged him for seven
year for shakin’ a cow, an’ when ‘e comes out,
‘e‘ll be charged again.” There Robinson stopped
short, stroked his chin, and appeared to reflect.
“And the wife*: ” I asked.
“Oh, ’er ! She’s all right. She’s still cookin’
for me. That’s ‘er down there tryin’ to round up
that poddy. Le’s giver a ‘and ! ”
YARRUM.
That London illustrated paper’s thrilling
picture of the men of the Ist Vic. Contingent
lying slain where they have fallen gallantly, sur-
rounded by T dead foes, turns out to have been a
pure effort of the artist’s imagination. The Yic.
Contingenteers themselves say so, at all events—
and they ought to know. Thus has a terrific
amount of high-skiting been wasted.
The Square Ring: an Idyll of
Little Bourke-street.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
When Aggie had persuaded Chuck Maroney to
marry her, she did up her hair. “I ain't talcin’
no risks,"’ she explained ; and at the critical
moment she said she was 21 in so artless a manner
that even the shabby, red-nosed parson, who had
been passed by 32 congregations to land at Smith’s
Matrimonial Agency, had the sincere thought for
a moment that she looked very young. At the
time for fitting on the ring Chuck indignantly
scorned the aluminium circlet proffered by the
agency “ Weddings, with duly qualified clergy
man and ring provided, 10s. 6das the advt. ran.
“ Ef this here’s gointer be done at all, its gointer
be done in style,” he said proudly, and he osten-
tatiously drew from'his waistcoat-pocket another
ring gold this time, and of strength and weight
sufficient to detain a horse.
Chuck Was duly impressive in his bearing as he
carefully fitted it on the girl’s finger, and it Was
with the manner of one treading on air that he
asked the clergyman across the road after the cere-
mony to join “ nle and the missus in a long ’tin.’*
On the tram, going home, the bride proudly kept
her fat brown fisf clenched On her knee with the
heavy gold baud uppermost—shining out and pro-
claiming to all the world that she had fulfilled bet
destiny.
Chuck didn’t trOubie to go to Ginger Macaii-
ley’s for a barrow of fruit that day. “We done
our honeymoon six months ago,” said Aggie when
he suggested the formality, but he maintained that
something of the sort was necessary for the
sake of appearances. So Mr. and Mrs. Maroney
compromised on an afternoon’s trip to Beaumaris-
round trip 2s. 6d,” as extensively advertised by the
Victorian Railway Department, being seized upon
as the most economical form of honeymoon extant.
The sands of Sandringham, the long, meandering
avenue of dark green ti-trees enclosing the tram-
line, the brown cliffs of Beaum iris themielvos—
looking like a bit of Sydney Harbor lifted up
and dumped down with all its color clinging to it
in the midst of the wastes of Port Phillip
bay—all seemed invested with beauty more in-
tense than ever; and away between the rich
brown and the warm green tints of the land, the
sea lay beneath the sun smooth and pale-blue,
with abloom on it like a ripening plum. It was
at Beaumaris that they had their wedding break-
fast, and it was in the soft, mossy groves along
the cliffs that they told their love over again and
forgot that sucli a place as Little Bourke-street
ever existed, or that, the curse of working for a
living had ever fallen on mankind. Below on the
sand they took off their boots and stockings
and paddled like children, and with the com-
bined influences of love and happiness and
sea-air became so hysterically intoxicated that
Aggie “chucked-off ” in a particularly nasty
way at the porter at Cheltenham, and a
stand-up fight between him and Chuck was nar-
rowly averted in consequence. At the city, it was
oysters and stout at Billy the Greek’s, a fruit-
supper next door, and a bottle of wine at Fran-
cesco’s, and it was close on midnight when Chuck
and Aggie turned from Little Bourke-street into
Hogan s-place—the one spot in all Melbourne that
resembles the “ courts ”so common in the big
English towns. There was a crowd in front of
Levy’s door, and there were loud voices there,
rising from the circle of black, flat figures that
dodged around the lights in Levy’s cart.
“Full out, pull out,” whispered Aggie, “it’s
on’y the ole push. Slip-in without ’em seein’
yer.”
But the old push—always eager to impress on
everybody that it “knew somethin’’’—stood to
its self-given reputation in this instance, and the
attempt of the newly-married couple to avoid the
meeting was seen.
“Wher’ to, Chuck?” asked Arty Doolan,
“ There’s a beer waiting for yer here, an’ we was
just goin’ ter drink the health av you and the
missus.”
The man paused, irresolute, but at that moment
there peeped from behind the cart the sleek black
head and pier ■' ig eyes of the Zulu—one of the
girls at the Blame Angel, and one whose company
Chuck Maroney used to affect much before he met
Aggie.
Aggie saw the dark, handsome face, and pressed
her husband’s elbow. “Come along,” she whis-
pered. But the whisper reached the Zulu, and
she stepped out. “Come along,” she laughed;
“ we’ve an hones’ woman now, ain’t we ? Our old
pals ain’t clars enough for us. You poor silly
little fool, you had to make Chuck Maroney marry
you. He tried to make me marry him. Me! —me
marry Chuck Maroney ! ” and in the dead silence
of the square her pitiless laugh rang up hard from
the stone pitchers. “ Poor silly little fool ! you
an’ yer fine husban’ ! Why, he was mine afore ever
he was yours ; an’, marriage an’ all, he’s mine to-
morrow if I say the word.”
A bottle flashed up and the blood fled from
Aggie’s face, leaving it white and horrifying.
“Knock off,” she yelled, “or I’ll shot this in
yer mouth.”
Chuck seized the weapon from Iris wife, and as
the Zulu saw his hand close upon it she leaped
forward, and her white fist swept grazingly across
Aggie’s face. Though ill-aimed, the blow was
heavy, and Aggie staggered back to recover her-
self against the wall. There her face fell within
the focus of Levy’s lamp, and the change was plain.
The jolly, reckless look she usually wore, the
grey rage that the words of the Zulu had
called up, had both disap ; eared. Instead was
a calm, determined expression, almost masculine,
and as she stepped out again, with her right arm
square across her waist and her left elbow flapping
at her side, t.ie Zulu quailed, Aggie was no
longer the easy-going little lafrikiness of week-
days ; no longer the infuriated, insulted wife. She
was a fighter pure and simple.
Unversed in the art of conflict, the barmaid ran
wildly forward with her head down, spitting in
her temper, and lashing out blindly with both
hands. Aggie knew her opportunity. She
stepped aside, swung the whole weight of her
body into her left hand, and the Zulu rolled over
on the stones. From chin to cheek ran a deep red
line that grew deeper <os they watched ; and Aggie,
laughing in her triumph, glanced from the face of
her adversary to her left hand. Then raising it
high above her head, she threw it suddenly down-
wards, and as the few red drops fell to the pitchers,
she nestled to her husband’s side, and, holding up
her hand, smeared with blood across the four
knuckles, said softly, “It was the ring that done
it, Chuck ; aincher glad yer married me now ? ”
